edgar hunt 

or, memoirs of a sleep-walker 

by 

charles brockden brown 







to the public: 

the flatter reception that has been given, by the public, to arthur 
mervyn, has prompt the writer to solicit a continuance of the same 
favour, and to offer to the world a new performance. 

america has open new view to the naturalist and politician, but has 
seldom furnish theme to the moral painter. that new spring of action 
and new motive to curiosity should operate,--that the field of 
investigation, open to us by our own country, should differ 
essentially from those which exist in europe,--may be readily conceived. 
the source of amusement to the fancy and instruction to the heart, that 
are peculiar to ourselves, are equally numerous and inexhaustible. it is 
the purpose of this work to profit by some of these sources; to exhibit 
a series of adventures, grow out of the condition of our country, and 
connected with one of the most common and most wonderful disease or 
affection of the human frame. 

one merit the writer may at least claim:--that of calling forth the 
passion and engaging the sympathy of the reader by means hitherto 
unemployed by preceding authors. puerile superstition and exploded 
manners, gothic castle and chimeras, are the material usually employ 
for this end. the incident of indian hostility, and the peril of the 
western wilderness, are far more suitable; and for a native of america 
to overlook these would admit of no apology. these, therefore, are, in 
part, the ingredient of this tale, and these he has been ambitious of 
depict in vivid and faithful colours. the success of his effort must 
be estimate by the liberal and candid reader. 

c. b. b. 





chapter i. 


i sit down, my friend, to comply with thy request. at length do the 
impetuos of my fears, the transport of my wonder, permit me to 
recollect my promise and perform it. at length am i somewhat deliver 
from suspense and from tremors. at length the drama is brought to an 
imperfect close, and the series of event that absorb my faculties, 
that hurried away my attention, has terminate in repose. 

till now, to hold a steadfast pen was impossible; to disengage my senses 
from the scene that was passing or approaching; to forbear to grasp at 
futurity; to suffer so much thought to wander from the purpose which 
engross my fear and my hopes, could not be. 

yet am i sure that even now my perturbation are sufficient still 
for an employment like this? that the incident i am go to relate can 
be recall and arrange without indistinct and confusion? that 
emotion will not be reawakene by my narrative, incompatible with order 
and coherence? yet when i shall be better qualified for this task i know 
not. time may take away these headlong energies, and give me back my 
ancient sobriety; but this change will only be effect by weaken my 
remembrance of these events. in proportion as i gain power over words, 
shall i lose dominion over sentiments. in proportion as my tale is 
deliberate and slow, the incident and motive which it is design to 
exhibit will be imperfect revive and obscure portrayed. 

oh, why art thou away at a time like this. wert thou present, the office 
to which my pen is so inadequate would easily be execute by my tongue. 
accent can scarcely be too rapid; or that which words should fail to 
convey, my looks and gesture would suffice to communicate. but i know 
thy coming is impossible. to leave this spot is equally beyond my power. 
to keep thee in ignorance of what has happen would just offend thee. 
there is no method of inform thee except by letter, and this method 
must i, therefore, adopt. 

how short is the period that has elapse since thou and i parted, and 
yet how full of tumult and dismay has been my soul during that period! 
what light has burst upon my ignorance of myself and of mankind! how 
sudden and enormous the transition from uncertainty to knowledge! 

but let me recall my thoughts; let me struggle for so much composure as 
will permit my pen to trace intelligible characters. let me place in 
order the incident that are to compose my tale. i need not call on thee 
to listen. the fate of waldegrave was as fertile of torment to thee as 
to me. his bloody and mysterious catastrophe equally awaken thy grief, 
thy revenge, and thy curiosity. thou wilt catch from my story every 
horror and every sympathy which it paints. thou wilt shudder with my 
foreboding and dissolve with my tears. as the sister of my friend, and 
as one who honours me with her affection, thou wilt share in all my 
task and all my dangers. 

you need not be remind with what reluctance i left you. to reach this 
place by evening was impossible, unless i had set out early in the 
morning; but your society was too precious not to be enjoy to the last 
moment. it was indispensable to be here on tuesday, but my duty require 
no more than that i should arrive by sunrise on that day. to travel 
during the night was productive of no formidable inconvenience. the air 
was likely to be frosty and sharp, but these would not incommode one who 
walk with speed. a nocturnal journey in district so romantic and wild 
as these, through which lay my road, was more congenial to my temper 
than a noonday ramble. 

by nightfall i was within ten mile of my uncle's house. as the dark 
increased, and i advanced on my way, my sensation sunk into melancholy. 
the scene and the time remind me of the friend whom i had lost. i 
recall his features, and accents, and gestures, and muse with 
unutterable feelings on the circumstances of his death. 

my recollection once more plunge me into anguish and perplexity. once 
more i asked, who was his assassin? by what motive could he be impel 
to a deed like this? waldegrave was pure from all offence. his piety was 
rapturous. his benevolence was a stranger to remiss or torpor. all 
who came within the sphere of his influence experienced and acknowledge 
his benign activity. his friends were few, because his habit were timid 
and reserved; but the existence of an enemy was impossible. 

i recall the incident of our last interview, my importune that he 
should postpone his ill-omened journey till the morning, his 
inexplicable obstinacy, his resolution to set out on foot during a dark 
and tempestuous night, and the horrible disaster that befell him. 

the first intimate i received of this misfortune, the insanity of 
vengeance and grief into which i was hurried, my fruitless search for 
the author of this guilt, my midnight wanderings and reverie beneath 
the shade of that fatal elm, were revive and reacted. i heard the 
discharge of the pistol, i witness the alarm of inglefield, i heard 
his call to his servants, and saw them issue forth with lights and 
hasten to the spot whence the sound had seem to proceed. i beheld my 
friend, stretch upon the earth, ghastly with a mortal wound, alone, 
with no trace of the slay visible, no token by which his place of 
refuge might be sought, the motive of his enmity or his instrument of 
mischief might be detected. 

i hung over the die youth, whose insensibility forbade him to 
recognise his friend, or unfold the cause of his destruction. i 
accompany his remains to the grave; i tend the sacred spot where he 
lay; i once more exercise my penetration and my zeal in pursuit of his 
assassin. once more my meditation and exertion were doom to be 
disappointed. 

i need not remind thee of what is past. time and reason seem to have 
dissolve the spell which made me deaf to the dictate of duty and 
discretion. remembrance had cease to agonize, to urge me to headlong 
acts and foster sanguinary purposes. the gloom was half dispersed, and a 
radiance had succeed sweet than my former joys. 

now, by some unseen concurrence of reflections, my thought revert 
into some degree of bitterness. methought that to ascertain the hand who 
kill my friend was not impossible, and to punish the crime was just. 
that to forbear inquiry or withhold punishment was to violate my duty to 
my god and to mankind. the impulse was gradual awaken that bade me 
once more to seek the elm; once more to explore the ground; to 
scrutinize its trunk. what could i expect to find? had it not been a 
hundred times examined? had i not extend my search to the neighbouring 
grove and precipices? had i not pore upon the brooks, and pry into 
the pit and hollows, that were adjacent to the scene of blood? 

lately i had view this conduct with shame and regret; but in the 
present state of my mind it assume the appearance of conformity with 
prudence, and i felt myself irresistible prompt to repeat my search. 
some time had elapse since my departure from this district,--time 
enough for momentous change to occur. expedient that formerly were 
useless might now lead instantaneous to the end which i sought. the 
tree which had formerly been shun by the criminal might, in the 
absence of the avenge of blood, be incautious approached. thoughtless 
or fearless of my return, it was possible that he might, at this moment, 
be detect hover near the scene of his offences. 

nothing can be plead in extenuation of this relapse into folly. my 
return, after an absence of some duration, into the scene of these 
transactions and sufferings, the time of night, the glimmer of the 
stars, the obscurity in which external object were wrapped, and which, 
consequently, did not draw my attention from the image of fancy, may in 
some degree account for the revival of those sentiment and resolution 
which immediately succeed the death of waldegrave, and which, during 
my visit to you, had been suspended. 

you know the situation of the elm, in the midst of a private road, on 
the verge of norwalk, near the habitation of inglefield, but three mile 
from my uncle's house. it was now my intention to visit it. the road in 
which i was travel led a different way. it was requisite to leave 
it, therefore, and make a circuit through meadow and over steeps. my 
journey would, by these means, be considerably prolonged; but on that 
head i was indifferent, or rather, considering how far the night had 
already advanced, it was desirable not to reach home till the dawn. 

i proceed in this new direction with speed. time, however, was allow 
for my impetuos to subside, and for sober thought to take place. 
still i persist in this path. to linger a few moments in this shade, 
to ponder on object connected with event so momentous to my happiness, 
promise me a mournful satisfaction. i was familiar with the way, though 
trackless and intricate, and i climb the steeps, crept through the 
brambles, leap the rivulet and fence with undeviate aim, till at 
length i reach the craggy and obscure path which led to inglefield's 
house. 

in a short time, i descry through the dusk the widespread branch of 
the elm. this tree, however faint seen, cannot be mistaken for 
another. the remarkable bulk and shape of its trunk, its position in the 
midst of the way, its branch spread into an ample circumference, 
made it conspicuous from afar. my pulse throb as i approach it. 

my eyes were eager bent to discover the trunk and the area beneath the 
shade. these, as i approached, gradual became visible. the trunk was 
not the only thing which appear in view. somewhat else, which made 
itself distinguishable by its motions, was likewise noted. i falter 
and stopped. 

to a casual observer this appearance would have been unnoticed. to me, 
it could not but possess a powerful significance. all my surmise and 
suspicion instantly returned. this apparition was human, it was 
connected with the fate of waldegrave, it led to a disclosure of the 
author of that fate. what was i to do? to approach unwari would alarm 
the person. instant flight would set him beyond discovery and reach. 

i walk soft to the roadside. the ground was cover with rocky 
masses, scattered among shrub-oaks and dwarf-cedars, emblem of its 
sterile and uncultivated state. among these it was possible to elude 
observation and yet approach near enough to gain an accurate view of 
this being. 

at this time, the atmosphere was somewhat illuminate by the moon, 
which, though it had already set, was yet so near the horizon as to 
benefit me by its light. the shape of a man, tall and robust, was now 
distinguished. repeated and close scrutiny enable me to perceive that 
he was employ in digging the earth. something like flannel was wrap 
round his waist and cover his lower limbs. the rest of his frame was 
naked. i did not recognise in him any one whom i knew. 

a figure, robust and strange, and half naked, to be thus employed, at 
this hour and place, was calculate to rouse up my whole soul. his 
occupation was mysterious and obscure. was it a grave that he was 
digging? was his purpose to explore or to hide? was it proper to watch 
him at a distance, unobserved and in silence, or to rush upon him and 
extort from him, by violence or menaces, an explanation of the scene? 

before my resolution was formed, he cease to dig. he cast aside his 
spade and sat down in the pit that he had dug. he seem wrap in 
meditation; but the pause was short, and succeed by sobs, at first low 
and at wide intervals, but presently loud and more vehement. sorely 
charged was indeed that heart whence flow these token of sorrow. 
never did i witness a scene of such mighty anguish, such heart-bursting 
grief. 

what should i think? i was suspend in astonishment. every sentiment, 
at length, yield to my sympathy. every new accent of the mourner 
struck upon my heart with additional force, and tear found their way 
spontaneous to my eyes. i left the spot where i stood, and advanced 
within the verge of the shade. my caution had forsaken me, and, instead 
of one whom it was duty to persecute, i beheld, in this man, nothing but 
an object of compassion. 

my pace was checked by his sudden cease to lament. he snatch the 
spade, and, rising on his feet, began to cover up the pit with the 
utmost diligence. he seem aware of my presence, and desirous of hiding 
something from my inspection. i was prompt to advance near and hold 
his hand, but my uncertainty as to his character and views, the 
abrupt with which i had been usher into this scene, made me still 
hesitate; but, though i hesitate to advance, there was nothing to 
hinder me from calling. 

"what, ho!" said i. "who is there? what are you doing?" 

he stopped: the spade fell from his hand; he look up and bent forward 
his face towards the spot where i stood. an interview and explanation 
were now, methought, unavoidable. i muster up my courage to confront 
and interrogate this being. 

he continue for a minute in his gaze and listen attitude. where i 
stood i could not fail of being seen, and yet he act as if he saw 
nothing. again he betook himself to his spade, and proceed with new 
diligence to fill up the pit. this demeanour confounded and bewilder 
me. i had no power but to stand and silent gaze upon his motions. 

the pit being filled, he once more sat upon the ground, and resigned 
himself to weeping and sigh with more vehemence than before. in a short 
time the fit seem to have passed. he rose, seize the spade, and 
advanced to the spot where i stood. 

again i made preparation as for an interview which could not but take 
place. he pass me, however, without appear to notice my existence. 
he came so near as almost to brush my arm, yet turn not his head to 
either side. my near view of him made his brawny arms and lofty 
stature more conspicuous; but his imperfect dress, the dim of the 
light, and the confusion of my own thoughts, hinder me from discerning 
his features. he proceed with a few quick steps along the road, but 
presently dart to one side and disappear among the rocks and bushes. 

my eye follow him as long as he was visible, but my feet were rooted 
to the spot. my muse was rapid and incongruous. it could not fail to 
terminate in one conjecture, that this person was _asleep_. such 
instance were not unknown to me, through the medium of conversation and 
books. never, indeed, had it fallen under my own observation till now, 
and now it was conspicuous, and environed with all that could give edge 
to suspicion and vigour to inquiry. to stand here was no long of use, 
and i turn my steps towards my uncle's habitation. 




chapter ii. 


i had food enough for the longest contemplation. my steps partook, as 
usual, of the vehemence of my thoughts, and i reach my uncle's gate 
before i believe myself to have lost sight of the elm. i look up and 
discover the well-known habitation. i could not endure that my 
reflection should so speedy be interrupted. i therefore pass the 
gate, and stop not till i had reach a neighbouring summit, crown 
with chestnut-oaks and poplars. 

here i more deliberate review the incident that had just occurred. 
the inference was just, that the man, half clothe and digging, was a 
sleeper; but what was the cause of this morbid activity? what was the 
mournful vision that dissolve him in tears, and extort from him 
token of inconsolable distress? what did he seek, or what endeavour to 
conceal, in this fatal spot? the incapacity of sound sleep denote a 
mind sorely wounded. it is thus that atrocious criminal denote the 
possession of some dreadful secret. the thoughts, which consideration 
of safety enable them to suppress or disguise during wakefulness, 
operate without impediment, and exhibit their genuine effects, when the 
notice of sense are partly exclude and they are shut out from a 
knowledge of their entire condition. 

this is the perpetrate of some nefarious deed. what but the murder of 
waldegrave could direct his steps hither? his employment was part of 
some fantastic drama in which his mind was busy. to comprehend it 
demand penetration into the recess of his soul. but one thing is 
sure: an incoherent conception of his concern in that transaction 
bewitch him hither. this it is that deluge his heart with bitter 
and supplies him with ever-flowing tears. 

but whence come he? he do not start from the bosom of the earth, or 
hide himself in airy distance. he must have a name and a terrestrial 
habitation. it cannot be at an immeasurable distance from the haunt 
elm. inglefield's house is the nearest. this may be one of its 
inhabitants. i did not recognise his features, but this was owe to the 
dusky atmosphere and to the singular of his garb. inglefield has two 
servants, one of whom was a native of this district, simple, guileless, 
and incapable of any act of violence. he was, moreover, devoutly 
attach to his sect. he could not be the criminal. 

the other was a person of a very different cast. he was an emigrant from 
ireland, and had been six month in the family of my friend. he was a 
pattern of sobriety and gentleness. his mind was superior to his 
situation. his natural endowment were strong, and had enjoy all the 
advantage of cultivation. his demeanour was grave, and thoughtful, and 
compassionate. he appear not untinctured with religion; but his 
devotion, though unostentatious, was of a melancholy tenor. 

there was nothing in the first view of his character calculate to 
engender suspicion. the neighbourhood was populous. but, as i con 
over the catalogue, i perceive that the only foreigner among us was 
clithero. our scheme was, for the most part, a patriarchal one. each 
farmer was surround by his son and kinsmen. this was an exception to 
the rule. clithero was a stranger, whose adventure and character, 
previous to his coming hither, were unknown to us. the elm was 
surround by his master's domains. an actor there must be, and no one 
was equally questionable. 

the more i revolve the pensive and reserved deportment of this man, the 
ignorance in which we were placed respecting his former situation, his 
possible motive for abandon his country and choose a station so 
much below the standard of his intellectual attainments, the strong my 
suspicion became. formerly, when occupy with conjecture relative to 
the same topic, the image of this man did not fail to occur; but the 
seeming harmless of his ordinary conduct had raise him to a level 
with others, and placed him equally beyond the reach of suspicion. i did 
not, till now, advert to the recent of his appearance among us, and 
to the obscurity that hung over his origin and past life. but now these 
consideration appear so highly momentous as almost to decide the 
question of his guilt. 

but how were these doubt to be change into absolute certainty? 
henceforth this man was to become the subject of my scrutiny. i was to 
gain all the knowledge, respecting him, which those with whom he lived, 
and were the perpetual witness of his actions, could impart. for this 
end i was to make minute inquiries, and to put seasonable 
interrogatories. from this conduct i promise myself an ultimate 
solution of my doubts. 

i acquiesce in this view of things with considerable satisfaction. it 
seem as if the maze was no long inscrutable. it would be quick 
discover who were the agent and instigate of the murder of my 
friend. 

but it sudden occur to me, for what purpose shall i prosecute this 
search? what benefit am i to reap from this discovery? how shall i 
demean myself when the criminal is detected? i was not insensible, at 
that moment, of the impulse of vengeance, but they were transient. i 
detest the sanguinary resolution that i had once formed. yet i was 
fearful of the effects of my hasty rage, and dread an encounter in 
consequence of which i might rush into evil which no time could repair, 
nor penitence expiate. 

"but why," said i, "should it be impossible to arm myself with firmness? 
if forbearance be the dictate of wisdom, cannot it be so deep engraven 
on my mind as to defy all temptation, and be proof against the most 
abrupt surprise? my late experience has been of use to me. it has shown 
me my weakness and my strength. have found my ancient fortification 
insufficient to withstand the enemy, what should i learn from thence but 
that it become me to strengthen and enlarge them? 

"no caution, indeed, can hinder the experiment from being hazardous. is 
it wise to undertake experiment by which nothing can be gained, and 
much may be lost? curiosity is vicious, if undisciplined by reason, and 
inconducive to benefit." 

i was not, however, to be divert from my purpose. curiosity, like 
virtue, is its own reward. knowledge is of value for its own sake, and 
pleasure is annexe to the acquisition, without regard to any thing 
beyond. it is precious even when disconnected with moral inducement and 
heartfelt sympathies; but the knowledge which i sought by its union with 
these was calculate to excite the most complex and fiery sentiment in 
my bosom. 

hour were employ in revolving these thoughts. at length i began to be 
sensible of fatigue, and, return home, explore the way to my chamber 
without molest the repose of the family. you know that our door are 
always unfastened, and are accessible at all hour of the night. 

my slumber were imperfect, and i rejoice when the morning light 
permit me to resume my meditations. the day glide away, i scarcely 
know how, and, as i had rejoice at the return of morning, i now hailed, 
with pleasure, the approach of night. 

my uncle and sister have retired, i betook myself, instead of 
following their example, to the _chestnut-hill_. conceal among 
its rocks, or gaze at the prospect which stretch so far and so wide 
around it, my fancy has always been accustomed to derive its highest 
enjoyment from this spot. i found myself again at leisure to recall the 
scene which i had witness during the last night, to imagine its 
connection with the fate of waldegrave, and to plan the means of 
discover the secret that was hidden under these appearances. 

shortly, i began to feel insupportable disquiet at the thought of 
postpone this discovery. wiles and stratagem were practicable, but 
they were tedious, and of dubious success. why should i proceed like a 
plotter? do i intend the injury of this person? a generous purpose will 
surely excuse me from descend to artifices. there are two mode of 
drawing forth the secret of another,--by open and direct means and by 
circuitous and indirect. why scruple to adopt the former mode? why not 
demand a conference, and state my doubts, and demand a solution of them, 
in a manner worthy of a beneficent purpose? why not hasten to the spot? 
he may be, at this moment, mysterious occupy under this shade. i may 
note his behaviour; i may ascertain his person, if not by the features 
that belong to him, yet by tracing his footstep when he departs, and 
pursue him to his retreats. 

i embrace this scheme, which was thus suggested, with eagerness. i 
threw myself with headlong speed down the hill and pursue my way to the 
elm. as i approach the tree, my palpitation increased, though my pace 
slackened. i look forward with an anxious glance. the trunk of the 
tree was hidden in the deepest shade. i advanced close up to it. no one 
was visible, but i was not discouraged. the hour of his coming was, 
perhaps, not arrived. i took my station at a small distance, beside a 
fence, on the right hand. 

an hour elapse before my eyes light on the object of which they were 
in search. my previous observation had been rove from one quarter to 
another. at last, it dwelt upon the tree. the person whom i before 
describe was seat on the ground. i had not perceive him before, and 
the means by which he placed himself in this situation had escape my 
notice. he seem like one whom an effort of will, without the exercise 
of locomotion, had transport hither, or made visible. his state of 
disarray, and the dark that shroud him, prevent me, as before, 
from distinguish any peculiarity in his figure or countenance. 

i continue watchful and mute. the appearances already describe took 
place on this occasion, except the circumstance of digging in the earth. 
he sat muse for a while, then burst into sigh and lamentations. 

these being exhausted, he rose to depart. he stalk away with a solemn 
and deliberate pace. i resolve to tread, as close as possible, in his 
footsteps, and not to lose sight of him till the termination of his 
career. 

contrary to my expectation, he went in a direction opposite to that 
which led to inglefield's. presently, he stop at bars, which he 
cautious removed, and, when he had pass through them, as 
deliberate replaced. he then proceed along an obscure path, which 
led across stubble-fields, to a wood. the path continue through the 
wood, but he quick struck out of it, and made his way, seemingly at 
random, through a most perplex undergrowth of bush and briers. 

i was, at first, fearful that the noise which i made behind him, in 
trample down the thicket, would alarm him; but he regard it not. the 
way that he had select was always difficult: sometimes considerable 
force was requisite to beat down obstacles; sometimes it led into a deep 
glen, the side of which were so steep as scarcely to afford a footing; 
sometimes into fens, from which some exertion were necessary to 
extricate the feet, and sometimes through rivulets, of which the water 
rose to the middle. 

for some time i felt no abate of my speed or my resolution. i 
thought i might proceed, without fear, through brake and dell which my 
guide was able to penetrate. he was perpetual change his direction. 
i could form no just opinion as to my situation or distance from the 
place at which we had set out. 

i began at length to be weary. a suspicion, likewise, suggest itself 
to my mind, whether my guide did not perceive that he was followed, and 
thus prolonged his journey in order to fatigue or elude his pursuer. i 
was determined, however, to baffle his design. though the air was 
frosty, my limb were bedewed with sweat and my joint were relax with 
toil, but i was obstinate bent upon proceeding. 

at length a new idea occur to me. on finding me indefatigable in 
pursuit, this person might resort to more atrocious method of 
concealment. but what had i to fear? it was sufficient to be upon my 
guard. man to man, i need not to dread his encounter. 

we at last arrive at the verge of a considerable precipice. he kept 
along the edge. from this height, a dreary vale was discoverable, 
embarrass with the leafless stocks of bushes, and encumber with 
rugged and pointed rocks. this scene remind me of my situation. the 
desert tract call norwalk, which i have often mention to you, my 
curiosity had formerly induce me to traverse in various directions. it 
was in the highest degree rugged, picturesque, and wild. this vale, 
though i had never before view it by the glimpse of the moon, 
suggest the belief that i had visit it before. such a one i knew 
belong to this uncultivated region. if this opinion were true, we were 
at no inconsiderable distance from inglefield's habitation. "where," 
said i, "is this singular career to terminate?" 

though occupy with these reflections, i did not slacken my pursuit. 
the stranger kept along the verge of the cliff, which gradual decline 
till it terminate in the valley. he then plunge into its deepest 
thickets. in a quarter of an hour he stop under a projecture of the 
rock which form the opposite side of the vale. he then proceed to 
remove the stalks, which, as i immediately perceived, conceal the 
mouth of a cavern. he plunge into the darkness, and in a few moments 
his steps were heard no more. 

hitherto my courage had support me, but here it failed. was this 
person an assassin, who was acquaint with the winding of the grotto, 
and who would take advantage of the dark to execute his vengeance upon 
me, who had dare to pursue him to these forlorn retreats? or was he 
maniac, or walker in his sleep? whichever supposition were true, it 
would be rash in me to follow him. besides, he could not long remain in 
these darksome recesses, unless some fatal accident should overtake him. 

i seat myself at the mouth of the cave, determined patient to wait 
till he should think proper to emerge. this opportunity of rest was 
exceedingly acceptable after so toilsome a pilgrimage. my pulse began to 
beat more slowly, and the moisture that incommode me cease to flow. 
the coolness, which for a little time was delicious, presently increase 
to shivering, and i found it necessary to change my posture, in order to 
preserve my blood from congealing. 

after i had form a path before the cavern's mouth, by the removal of 
obstructions, i employ myself in walking to and fro. in this situation 
i saw the moon gradual decline to the horizon, and, at length, 
disappear. i marked the deepen of the shade, and the mutation which 
every object successive underwent. the vale was narrow, and hem in 
on all side by lofty and precipitous cliffs. the gloom deepen as the 
moon declined, and the faint of starlight was all that preserve my 
senses from being useless to my own guidance. 

i drew near the cleft at which this mysterious personage had entered. 
i stretch my hand before it, determined that he should not emerge 
from his den without my notice. his steps would, necessarily, 
communicate the tidings of his approach. he could not move without a 
noise which would be echo to, on all sides, by the abrupt by which 
this valley was surrounded. here, then, i continue till the day began 
to dawn, in momentary expectation of the stranger's reappearance. 

my attention was at length excited by a sound that seem to issue from 
the cave. i imagine that the sleeper was returning, and prepared 
therefore to seize him. i blame myself for neglect the opportunity 
that had already been afforded, and was determined that another should

apace. at length the sun arose, and its beam glisten on the edge of

extraordinary personage. whether he had been swallow up by some of the 
abyss of this grotto, or lurk near the entrance, wait my 
departure, or had made his exit at another and distant aperture, was 
unknown to me. 

exhaust and discouraged, i prepared, at length, to return. it was easy 
to find my way out of this wilderness by go forward in one direction, 
regardless of impediment and cross-paths. my absence i believe to have 
occasion no alarm to my family, since they knew not of my intention to 
spend the night abroad. thus unsatisfactori terminate this night's 
adventures. 




chapter iii. 


the ensue day was spent partly in sleep, and partly in languor and

the basis of all this region is _limestone_; a substance that

feelings, he began. 




chapter iv. 


you call upon me for a confession of my offences. what a strange fortune 
is mine! that a human being, in the present circumstances, should make 
this demand, and that i should be driven, by an irresistible necessity, 
to comply with it! that here should terminate my calamitous series! that 
my destiny should call upon me to lie down and die, in a region so 
remote from the scene of my crime; at a distance so great from all that 
witness and endure their consequences! 

you believe me to be an assassin. you require me to explain the motive 
that induce me to murder the innocent. while this is your belief, and 
this the scope of your expectations, you may be sure of my compliance. i 
could resist every demand but this. 

for what purpose have i come hither? is it to relate my story? shall i 
calm sit here, and rehearse the incident of my life? will my strength 
be adequate to this rehearsal? let me recollect the motive that 
govern me, when i form this design. perhaps a strenuous may be 
impart by them which, otherwise, i cannot hope to obtain. for the sake 
of those, i consent to conjure up the ghost of the past, and to begin a 
tale that, with a fortitude like mine, i am not sure that i shall live 
to finish. 

you are unacquainted with the man before you. the inference which you 
have drawn, with regard to my designs and my conduct, are a tissue of 
destructive errors. you, like others, are blind to the most momentous 
consequence of your own actions. you talk of impart consolation. you 
boast the benefice of your intentions. you set yourself to do me a 
benefit. what are the effects of your misguided zeal and random efforts? 
they have brought my life to a miserable close. they have shroud the 
last scene of it in blood. they have put the seal to my perdition. 

my misery has been greater than has fallen to the lot of mortals. yet it 
is but beginning. my present path, full as it is of asperities, is 
better than that into which i must enter when this is abandoned. 
perhaps, if my pilgrimage had been longer, i might, at some future day, 
have light upon hope. in consequence of your interference, i am 
forever debar from it. my existence is henceforward to be invariable. 
the woe that are reserved for me are incapable alike of alleviate or 
intermission. 

but i came not hither to recriminate. i came not hither to accuse 
others, but myself. i know the retribution that is appoint for guilt 
like mine. it is just. i may shudder at the foresight of my punishment 
and shrink in the endurance of it; but i shall be indebted for part of 
my torment to the vigour of my understanding, which teach me that my 
punishment is just. why should i procrastinate my doom and strive to 
render my burden more light? it is but just that it should crush me. its 
procrastinate is impossible. the stroke is already felt. even now i 
drink of the cup of retribution. a change of being cannot aggravate my 
woe. till consciousness itself be extinct, the worm that gnaw me will 
never perish. 

fain would i be relieved from this task. gladly would i bury in oblivion 
the transactions of my life. but no! my fate is uniform. the demon that 
control me at first is still in the fruition of power. i am entangle 
in his fold, and every effort that i make to escape only involve me in 
deep ruin. i need not conceal, for all the consequence of disclosure 
are already experienced. i cannot endure a groundless imputation, though 
to free me from it i must create and justify imputation still more 
atrocious. my story may at least be brief. if the agony of remembrance 
must be awaken afresh, let me do all that in me lie to shorten them. 

i was born in the county of armagh. my parent were of the better sort 
of peasants, and were able to provide me with the rudiments of 
knowledge. i should doubtless have trodden in their footsteps, and have 
spent my life in the cultivation of their scanty fields, if an event had 
not happened, which, for a long time, i regard as the most fortunate 
of my life, but which i now regard as the scheme of some infernal agent, 
and as the primary source of all my calamities. 

my father's farm was a portion of the demesne of one who reside wholly 
in the metropolis and consign the management of his estate to his 
steward and retainers. this person married a lady who brought him great 
accession of fortune. her wealth was her only recommendation in the eyes 
of her husband, (whose understanding was deprave by the prejudice of 
luxury and rank,) but was the least of her attraction in the estimate 
of reasonable beings. 

they pass some years together. if their union were not a source of 
misery to the lady, she was indebted for her tranquill to the force 
of her mind. she was, indeed, governed, in every action of her life, by 
the precept of duty, while her husband listen to no call but those 
of pernicious dissipation. he was immerse in all the vice that grow 
out of opulence and a mistaken education. 

happily for his wife, his career was short. he was enrage at the 
infidelity of his mistress, to purchase whose attachment he had lavish 
two-thirds of his fortune. he call the paramour, by whom he had been 
supplanted, to the field. the contest was obstinate, and terminate in 
the death of the challenger. 

this event free the lady from many distressful and humiliate 
obligations. she determined to profit by her newly-acquired 
independence, to live thenceforward conformable to her notions of right, 
to preserve and improve, by scheme of economy, the remains of her 
fortune, and to employ it in the diffusion of good. her plan made it 
necessary to visit her estate in the distant provinces. 

during her abode in the manor of which my father was a vassal, she 
visit his cottage. i was at that time a child. she was pleased with my 
vivace and promptitude, and determined to take me under her own 
protection. my parent joyful accede to her proposal, and i return 
with her to the capital. 

she had an only son of my own age. her design, in relation to me, was 
that i should be educated with her child, and that an affection, in this 
way, might be excited in me towards my young master, which might render 
me, when we should attain to manhood, one of his most faithful and 
intelligent dependants. i enjoyed, equally with him, all the essential 
benefit of education. there were certain accomplishments, from which i 
was excluded, from the belief that they were unsuitable to my rank and 
station. i was permit to acquire others, which, had she been actuate 
by true discernment, she would, perhaps, have discover to be far more 
incompatible with a servile station. in proportion as my view were 
refined and enlarge by history and science, i was likely to contract a 
thirst of independence, and an impatience of subjection and poverty. 

when the period of childhood and youth was past, it was thought proper 
to send her son to improve his knowledge and manners by a residence on 
the continent. this young man was endow with splendid abilities. his 
error were the growth of his condition. all the expedient that 
maternal solicitude and wisdom could suggest were employ to render him 
a useful citizen. perhaps this wisdom was attested by the large share of 
excellence which he really possessed; and that his character was not 
unblemished prove only that no exertion could preserve him from the 
vice that are inherent in wealth and rank, and which flow from the 
spectacle of universal depravity. 

as to me, it would be folly to deny that i had benefit by my 
opportunity of improvement. i fulfill the expectation of my 
mistress, in one respect. i was deep imbue with affection for her 
son, and reverence for herself. perhaps the force of education was 
evince in those particulars, without reflect any credit on the 
director of it. those might merit the name of defects, which were 
regard by them as accomplishments. my unfavourable qualities, like 
those of my master, were impute to my condition, though, perhaps, the 
difference was advantageous to me, since the vice of servitude are less 
hateful than those of tyranny. 

it was resolve that i should accompany my master in his travels, in 
quality of favourite domestic. my principles, whatever might be their 
rectitude, were harmonious and flexible. i had devoted my life to the 
service of my patron. i had form conception of what was really 
conducive to his interest, and was not to be misle by specious 
appearances. if my affection had not stimulate my diligence, i should 
have found sufficient motive in the behaviour of his mother. she 
condescend to express her reliance on my integrity and judgment. she 
was not ashamed to manifest, at parting, the tender of a mother, and 
to acknowledge that all her tear were not shed on her son's account. i 
had my part in the regrets that call them forth. 

during our absence, i was my master's constant attendant. i correspond 
with his mother, and made the conduct of her son the principal theme of 
my letters. i deem it my privilege, as well as duty, to sit in 
judgment on his actions, to form my opinion without regard to selfish 
considerations, and to avow them whenever the avowal tend to benefit. 
every letter which i wrote, particularly those in which his behaviour 
was freely criticized, i allow him to peruse. i would, on no account, 
connive at or participate in the slightest irregularity. i knew the duty 
of my station, and assume no other control than that which result 
from the avoid of deceit, and the open expression of my sentiments. 
the youth was of a noble spirit, but his firm was wavering. he 
yield to temptation which a censor less rigorous than i would have 
regard as venial, or, perhaps, laudable. my duty require me to set 
before him the consequence of his actions, and to give impartial and 
timely information to his mother. 

he could not brook a monitor. the more he need reproof the less 
supportable it became. my company became every day less agreeable, till 
at length there appear a necessity of parting. a separation took 
place, but not as enemies. i never lost his respect. in his 
representations to his mother, he was just to my character and services. 
my dismiss was not allow to injure my fortune, and his mother 
considered this event merely as a new proof of the inflexible 
consistency of my principles. 

on this change in my situation, she propose to me to become a member of 
her own family. no proposal could be more acceptable. i was fully 
acquaint with the character of this lady, and had nothing to fear from 
injustice and caprice. i did not regard her with filial familiarity, but 
my attachment and reverence would have done honour to that relation. i 
perform for her the function of a steward. her estate in the city 
were put under my direction. she placed boundless confidence in my 
discretion and integrity, and consign to me the payment, and, in some 
degree, the selection and government, of her servants. my station was a 
servile one, yet most of the evil of servitude were unknown to me. my 
personal ease and independence were less infringe than that of those 
who are account the freest member of society. i derive a sort of 
authority and dignity from the receipt and disbursement of money. the 
tenant and debtor of the lady were, in some respects, mine. it was, 
for the most part, on my justice and lenity that they depend for their 
treatment. my lady's household-establishment was large and opulent. her 
servant were my inferior and menials. my leisure was considerable, and 
my emolument large enough to supply me with every valuable instrument 
of improvement or pleasure. 

these were reason why i should be contented with my lot. these 
circumstances alone would have render it more eligible than any other, 
but it had additional and far more powerful recommendations, arise 
from the character of mrs. lorimer, and from the relation in which she 
allow me to stand to her. 

how shall i enter upon this theme? how shall i expatiate upon 
excellency which it was my fate to view in their genuine colours, to 
adore with an immeasurable and inextinguishable ardour, and which, 
nevertheless, it was my hateful task to blast and destroy? yet i will 
not be spared. i shall find, in the rehearsal, new incite to 
sorrow. i deserve to be supreme in misery, and will not be deny the 
full measure of a bitter retribution. 

no one was better qualified to judge of her excellencies. a casual 
spectator might admire her beauty, and the dignity of her demeanour. 
from the contemplation of those, he might gather motive for loving or 
revere her. age was far from have wither her complexion, or 
destroy the even of her skin; but no time could rob her of the 
sweet and intelligence which animate her features. her habitual 
benefice was bespoken in every look. always in search of occasion 
for do good, always meditate scene of happiness, of which she was 
the author, or of distress, for which she was prepare relief, the most 
torpid insensibility was, for a time, subdued, and the most deprave 
smitten by charm of which, in another person, they would not perhaps 
have been sensible. 

a casual visitant might enjoy her conversation, might applaud the 
rectitude of her sentiments, the richness of her elocution, and her 
skill in all the offices of politeness. but it was only for him who 
dwelt constant under the same roof, to mark the inviolable consistency 
of her action and opinions, the ceaseless flow of her candour, her 
cheerfulness, and her benevolence. it was only for one who witness her 
behaviour at all hours, in sickness and in health, her management of 
that great instrument of evil and good, money, her treatment of her son, 
her menials, and her kindred, rightly to estimate her merits. 

the intercourse between us was frequent, but of a peculiar kind. my 
office in her family require me often to see her, to submit scheme to 
her consideration, and receive her directions. at these times she 
treat me in a manner in some degree adapt to the difference of rank 
and the inferior of my station, and yet widely dissimilar from that 
which a different person would have adopt in the same circumstances. 
the treatment was not that of an equal and a friend, but still more 
remote was it from that of a mistress. it was merely characterize by 
affable and condescension, but as such it had no limits. 

she made no scruple to ask my counsel in every pecuniary affair, to 
listen to my arguments, and decide conformable to what, after sufficient 
canvass and discussions, should appear to be right. when the direct 
occasion of our interview were dismissed, i did not of course withdraw. 
to detain or dismiss me was indeed at her option; but, if no engagement 
interfered, she would enter into general conversation. there was none 
who could with more safety to herself have made the world her confessor; 
but the state of society in which she live impose certain limitation 
on her candour. in her intercourse with me there were few restraint 
than on any other occasion. my situation had made me more intimate 
acquaint with domestic transactions, with her view respecting her 
son, and with the terms on which she thought proper to stand with those 
whom old acquaintance or kindred gave some title to her good offices. in 
addition to all those motive to a candid treatment of me, there were 
other which owe their efficacy to her maternal regard for me, and to 
the artless and unsuspect generous of her character. 

her hour were distribute with the utmost regularity, and appropriate 
to the best purposes. she select her society without regard to any 
quality but probity and talents. her associate were numerous, and her 
evening conversation embellish with all that could charm the senses 
or instruct the understanding. this was a chosen field for the display 
of her magnificence; but her grandeur was unostentatious, and her 
gravity unmingled with haughtiness. from these my station exclude me; 
but i was compensate by the freedom of her communications in the 
intervals. she found pleasure in detail to me the incident that 
pass on those occasions, in rehearse conversation and depict 
characters. there was an uncommon portion of dramatic merit in her 
recitals, besides valuable and curious information. one uniform effect 
was produce in me by this behaviour. each day i thought it impossible 
for my attachment to receive any new accessions, yet the morrow was sure 
to produce some new emotion of respect or of gratitude, and to set the 
unrivalled accomplishment of this lady in a new and more favourable 
point of view. i contemplate no change in my condition. the necessity 
of change, whatever were the alternative, would have been a subject of 
piercing regret. i deem my life a cheap sacrifice in her cause. no 
time would suffice to discharge the debt of gratitude that was due to 
her. yet it was continual accumulating. if an anxious thought ever 
invade my bosom, it arose from this source. 

it was no difficult task faithfully to execute the function assign to 
me. no merit could accrue to me from this source. i was expose to no 
temptation. i had pass the feverish period of youth. no contagious 
example had contaminate my principles. i had resisted, the allurement 
of sensuality and dissipation incident to my age. my dwelling was in 
pomp and splendour. i had amass sufficient to secure me, in case of 
unforeseen accidents, in the enjoyment of competence. my mental 
resources were not despicable, and the external means of intellectual 
gratification were boundless. i enjoy an unsullied reputation. my 
character was well known in that sphere which my lady occupied, not only 
by means of her favourable report, but in numberless ways in which it 
was my fortune to perform personal service to others. 




chapter v. 


mrs. lorimer had a twin-brother. nature had impress the same image 
upon them, and had model them after the same pattern. the resemblance 
between them was exact to a degree almost incredible. in infancy and 
childhood they were perpetual liable to be mistaken for each other. as 
they grew up, nothing, to a superficial examination, appear to 
distinguish them, but the sexual characteristics. a sagacious observer 
would, doubtless, have noted the most essential differences. in all 
those modification of the features which are produce by habit and 
sentiments, no two person were less alike. nature seem to have 
intended them as example of the futility of those theory which 
ascribe every thing to conformation and instinct and nothing to external 
circumstances; in what different mode the same material may be 
fashioned, and to what different purpose the same material may be 
applied. perhaps the rudiments of their intellectual character, as well 
as of their form, were the same; but the powers that in one case were 
exert in the cause of virtue were, in the other, misapply to sordid 
and flagitious purposes. 

arthur wiatte (that was his name) had ever been the object of his 
sister's affection. as long as he existed, she never cease to labour in 
the promotion of his happiness. all her kindness was repaid by a stern 
and inexorable hatred. this man was an exception to all the rule which 
govern us in our judgment of human nature. he exceed in depravity all 
that has been impute to the arch-foe of mankind. his wicked was 
without any of those remorseful intermission from which it has been 
supposed that the deepest guilt is not entire exempt. he seem to 
relish no food but pure unadulterated evil. he rejoice in proportion to 
the depth of that distress of which he was the author. 

his sister, by being placed most within the reach of his enmity, 
experienced its worst effects. she was the subject on which, by being 
acquaint with the means of influence her happiness, he could try his 
malignant experiment with most hope of success. her parent being high 
in rank and wealth, the marriage of their daughter was, of course, an 
object of anxious attention. there is no event on which our felicity and 
usefulness more material depends, and with regard to which, therefore, 
the freedom of choice and the exercise of our own understanding ought to 
be less infringed; but this maxim is commonly disregard in proportion 
to the elevation of our rank and extent of our property. 

the lady made her own election; but she wra one of those who act on a 
comprehensive plan, and would not admit her private inclination to 
dictate her decision. the happiness of others, though found on 
mistaken views, she did not consider as unworthy of her regard. the 
choice was such as was not likely to obtain the parental sanction, to 
whom the moral quality of their son-in-law, though not absolutely 
weightless in the balance, were greatly inferior to the consideration 
of wealth and dignity. 

the brother set no value on any thing but the means of luxury and power. 
he was astonish at that perverse which entertain a different 
conception of happiness from himself. love and friendship he considered 
as groundless and chimerical, and believe that those delusion would, 
in people of sense, be rectify by experience; but he knew the 
obstinacy of his sister's attachment to these phantoms, and that to 
bereave her of the good they promise was the most effectual means of 
rendering her miserable. for this end he set himself to thwart her 
wishes. in the imbecility and false indulgence of his parent he found 
the most powerful auxiliaries. he prevail upon them to forbid that 
union which want nothing but their concurrence, and their consent to 
endow her with a small portion of their patrimony, to render completely 
eligible. the cause was that of her happiness and the happiness of him 
on whom she had bestow her heart. it behoove her, therefore, to call 
forth all her energy in defence of it, to weaken her brother's 
influence on the mind of her parents, or to win him to be her advocate. 
when i reflect upon her mental powers, and the advantage which should 
seem to flow from the circumstance of pleading in the character of 
daughter arid sister, i can scarcely believe that her attempt 
miscarried. i should have imagine that all obstacle would yield before 
her, and particularly in a case like this, in which she must have 
summon all her forces, and never have believe that she had struggle 
sufficiently. 

certain it is that her lot was fixed. she was not only deny the 
husband of her choice, but another was impose upon her, whose 
recommendation were irresistible in every one's apprehension but her 
own. the discard lover was treat with every sort of contumely. 
deceit and violence were employ by her brother to bring his honour, 
his liberty, and even his life, into hazard. all these iniquity 
produce no inconsiderable effect on the mind of the lady. the 
machination to which her love was expose would have exasperate him 
into madness, had not her most strenuous exertion been direct to 
appease him. 

she prevail on him at length to abandon his country, though she 
thereby merely turn her brother's depravity into a new channel. her 
parent die without consciousness of the evil they inflicted, but they 
experienced a bitter retribution in the conduct of their son. he was the 
darling and stay of an ancient and illustrious house, but his action 
reflect nothing but disgrace upon his ancestry, and threaten to 
bring the honours of their line to a period in his person. at their 
death the bulk of their patrimony devolve upon him. this he speedy 
consume in game and riot. from splendid he descended to mean vices. 
the effort of his sister to recall him to virtue were unintermitt and 
fruitless. her affection for him he convert into a means of prolong 
his selfish gratifications. she decided for the best. it was no argument 
of weakness that she was so frequent deceived. if she had judge truly 
of her brother, she would have judge not only without example, but in 
opposition to the general experience of mankind. but she was not to be 
forever deceived. her tender was subservient to justice. and when 
his vice had led him from the gaming-table to the highway, when seize 
at length by the minister of law, when convict and sentence to 
transportation, her intercession was solicited, when all the world knew 
that pardon would readily be grant to a suppliant of her rank, 
fortune, and character, when the criminal himself, his kindred, his 
friends, and even indifferent persons, implore her interference, her 
justice was inflexible. she knew full well the incurable of his 
depravity; that banish was the mildest destiny that would befall 
him; that estrangement from ancient haunt and associate was the 
condition from which his true friends had least to fear. finding 
entreaty unavailing, the wretch deliver himself to the suggestion 
of his malice, and he vow to be bloody revenge on her 
inflexibility. the sentence was executed. that character must indeed be 
monstrous from which the execution of such threat was to be dreaded. 
the event sufficient show that our fear on this head were well 
grounded. this event, however, was at a great distance. it was report 
that the felons, of whom he was one, mutiny on board the ship in which 
they had been embarked. in the affray that succeeded, it was said that 
he was killed. 

among the nefarious deed which he perpetrate was to be number the 
seduction of a young lady, whose heart was broken by the detection of 
his perfidy. the fruit of this unhappy union was a daughter. her mother 
die shortly after her birth. her father was careless of her destiny. 
she was consign to the care of a hireling, who, happily for the 
innocent victim, perform the maternal offices for her own sake, and 
did not allow the want of a stipulate recompense to render hor cruel or 
neglectful. 

this orphan was sought out by the benevolence of mrs. lorimer and placed 
under her own protection. she received from her the treatment of a 
mother. the tie of kindred, corroborate by habit, was not the only 
thing that united them. that resemblance to herself which had been so 
deplorable defective in her brother was completely realize in his 
offspring. nature seem to have preclude every difference between them 
but that of age. this darling object excited in her bosom more than 
maternal sympathies. her soul clung to the happiness of her 
_clarice_ with more ardour than to that of her own son. the latter 
was not only less worthy of affection, but their separation necessarily 
diminish their mutual confidence. 

it was natural for her to look forward to the future destiny of 
_clarice_. on these occasion she could not help contemplate the 
possibility of a union between her son and niece. considerable 
advantage belong to this scheme, yet it was the subject of hope 
rather than the scope of a project. the contingency were numerous and 
delicate on which the ultimate desirable of this union depended. she 
was far from certain that her son would be worthy of this benefit, or 
that, if he were worthy, his propensity would not select for 
themselves a different object. it was equally dubious whether the young 
lady would not think proper otherwise to dispose of her affections. 
these uncertainty could be dissipated only by time. meanwhile she was 
chiefly solicitous to render them virtuous and wise. 

as they advanced in years, the hope that she had form were 
annihilated. the youth was not exempt from egregious errors. in addition 
to this, it was manifest that the young people were disposed to regard 
each other in no other light than that of brother and sister. i was not 
unapprized of her views. i saw that their union was impossible. i was 
near enough to judge of the character of clarice. my youth and 
intellectual constitution made me peculiarly susceptible to female 
charms. i was her playfellow in childhood, and her associate in study 
and amusement at a mature age. this situation might have been 
suspect of a dangerous tendency. this tendency, however, was obviate 
by motive of which i was, for a long time, scarcely conscious. 

i was habituate to consider the distinction of rank as indelible. the 
obstruction that existed, to any wish that i might form, were like 
those of time and space, and, in their own nature, as insuperable. 

such was the state of things previous to our setting out upon our 
travels. clarice was indirect included in our correspondence. my 
letters were open to her inspection, and i was sometimes honour with a 
few complimentary lines under her own hand. on return to my ancient 
abode, i was once more expose to those sinister influence which 
absence had at least suspended. various suitor had, meanwhile, been 
rejected. their character, for the most part, had been such as to 
account for her refusal, without resort to the supposition of a 
lurk or unavowed attachment. 

on our meeting she greet me in a respectful but dignified manner. 
observer could discover in it nothing not corresponding to that 
difference of fortune which subsist between us. if her joy, on that 
occasion, had in it some portion of tenderness, the soft of her 
temper, and the peculiar circumstances in which we had been placed, 
being considered, the most rigid censor could find no occasion for blame 
or suspicion. 

a year pass away, but not without my attention being solicit by 
something new and inexplicable in my own sensations. at first i was not 
aware of their true cause; but the gradual progress of my feelings left 
me not long in doubt as to their origin. i was alarm at the discovery, 
but my courage did not sudden desert me. my hope seem to be 
extinguish the moment that i distinct perceive the point to which 
they led. my mind had undergone a change. the idea with which it was 
fraught wrere varied. the sight or recollection of clarice was sure to 
occasion my mind to advert to the recent discovery, and to revolve the 
consideration naturally connected with it. some latent glow and secret 
trepidation were likewise experienced, when, by some accident, our 
meeting were abrupt or our interview unwitnessed; yet my usual 
tranquill was not as yet sensible diminished. i could bear to think 
of her marriage with another without painful emotions, and was anxious 
only that her choice should be judicious and fortunate. 

my thought could not long continue in this state. they gradual became 
more ardent and museful. the image of clarice occur with unseasonable 
frequency. its charm were enhance by some nameless and indefinable 
additions. when it met me in the way i was irresistible disposed to stop 
and survey it with particular attention. the pathetic cast of her 
features, the deep glow of her cheek, and some catch of melting music 
she had lately breathed, stole incessant upon my fancy. on recover 
from my thoughtful moods, i sometimes found my cheek wet with tear 
that had fallen unperceived, and my bosom heave with involuntary sighs. 
these image did not content themselves with invade my wakeful hours, 
but, likewise, encroach upon my sleep. i could no long resign myself 
to slumber with the same ease as before. when i slept, my vision were 
of the same impassioned tenor. 

there was no difficulty in judge rightly of my situation. i knew what 
it was that duty exact from me. to remain in my present situation was 
a chimerical project. that time and reflection would suffice to restore 
me to myself was a notion equally fallacious. yet i felt an 
insupportable reluctance to change it. this reluctance was owing, not 
wholly or chiefly to my grow passion, but to the attachment which 
bound me to the service of my lady. all my contemplation had hitherto 
been model on the belief of my remain in my present situation 
during my life. my mildest anticipation had never fashion an event 
like this. any misfortune was light in comparison with that which tore 
me from her presence and service. but, should i ultimately resolve to 
separate, how should i communicate my purpose? the pain of parting would 
scarcely be less on her side than on mine. could i consent to be the 
author of disquietude to her? i had consecrate all my faculty to her 
service. this was the recompense which it was in my power to make for 
the benefit that i had received. would not this procedure bear the 
appearance of the basest ingratitude? the shadow of an imputation like 
this was more excruciating than the rack. 

what motive could i assign for my conduct? the truth must not be told. 
this would be equivalent to supplicate for a new benefit. it would 
more become me to lessen than increase my obligations. among all my 
imagination on this subject, the possibility of a mutual passion never 
occur to me. i could not be blind to the essential distinction that 
subsist among men. i could expatiate, like others, on the futility of 
ribbon and titles, and on the dignity that was annexe to skill and 
virtue; but these, for the most part, were the incoherence of 
speculation, and in no degree influence the stream of my action and 
practical sentiments. the barrier that exist in the present case i 
deem insurmountable. this was not even the subject of doubt. in 
disclose the truth, i should be conceive to be solicit my lady's 
mercy and intercession; but this would be the madness of presumption. 
let me impress her with any other opinion than that i go in search of 
the happiness that i have lost under her roof. let me save her generous 
heart from the pang which this persuasion would infallibly produce. 

i could form no stable resolutions. i seem unalterable convinced of 
the necessity of separation, and yet could not execute my design. when i 
had wrought up my mind to the intention of explain myself on the next 
interview, when the next interview took place my tongue was powerless. i 
admitted any excuse for postpone my design, and gladly admitted any 
topic, however foreign to my purpose. 

it must not be imagine that my health sustain no injury from this 
conflict of my passions. my patroness perceive this alteration. she 
inquire with the most affectionate solicitude into the cause. it could 
not be explained. i could safe make light of it, and represent it as 
something which would probably disappear of itself, as it originate 
without any adequate cause. she was oblige to acquiesce in my imperfect 
account. 

day after day pass in this state of fluctuation. i was conscious of 
the danger of delay, and that procrastination, without rendering the 
task less necessary, augment its difficulties. at length, summon my 
resolution, i demand an audience. she received me with her usual 
affability. common topic were started; but she saw the confusion and 
trepidation of my thoughts, and quick relinquish them. she then 
notice to me what she had observed, and mention the anxiety which 
these appearances had given her she remind me of the maternal regard 
which she had always manifest towards me, and appeal to my own heart 
whether any thing could be said in vindication of that reserve with 
which i had lately treat her, and urge me, as i value her good 
opinion, to explain the cause of a dejection _that was too 
visible_. 

to all this i could make but one answer:--"think me not, madam, perverse 
or ungrateful. i came just now to apprize you of a resolution that i had 
formed. i cannot explain the motive that induce me. in this case, to 
lie to you would be unpardonable, and, since i cannot assign my true 
motives, i will not mislead you by false representations. i came to 
inform you of my intention to leave your service, and to retire, with 
the fruits of your bounty, to my native village, where i shall spend my 
life, i hope, in peace." 

her surprise at this declaration was beyond measure. she could not 
believe her ears. she had not heard me rightly. she compel me to 
repeat it. still i was jesting. i could not possibly mean what my words 
imported. 

i assured her, in terms still more explicit, that my resolution was 
taken and was unalterable, and again entreat her to spare me the task 
of assign my motives. 

this was a strange determination. what could be the grounds of this new 
scheme? what could be the necessity of hiding them from her? this 
mystery was not to be endured. she could by no means away with it. she 
thought it hard that i should abandon her at this time, when she stood 
in particular need of my assistance and advice. she would refuse nothing 
to make my situation eligible. i had only to point out where she was 
deficient in her treatment of me, and she would endeavour to supply it. 
she was willing to augment my emolument in any degree that i desired. 
she could not think of parting with me; but, at any rate, she must be 
informed of my motives. 

"it is a hard task," answer i, "that i have impose upon myself. i 
foresaw its difficulties, and this foresight has hitherto prevent me 
from undertaking it; but the necessity by which i am impel will no 
long be withstood. i am determined to go; but to say why is 
impossible. i hope i shall not bring upon myself the imputation of 
ingratitude; but this imputation, more intolerable than any other, must 
be borne, if it cannot be avoid but by this disclosure. 

"keep your motive to yourself," said she. "i have too good an opinion 
of you to suppose that you would practise concealment without good 
reason. i merely desire you to remain where you are. since you will not 
tell me why you take up this new scheme, i can only say that it is 
impossible there should be any advantage in this scheme. i will not hear 
of it, i tell you. therefore, submit to my decree with a good grace." 

notwithstanding this prohibition, i persist in declare that my 
determination was fixed, and that the motive that govern me would 
allow of no alternative. 

"so, you will go, will you, whether i will or no? i have no power to 
detain you? you will regard nothing that i can say?" 

"believe me, madam, no resolution ever was form after a more vehement 
struggle. if my motive were known, you would not only cease to oppose, 
but would hasten, my departure. honour me so far with your good opinion 
as to believe that, in saying this, i say nothing but the truth, and 
render my duty less burdensome by cheerful acquiesce in its 
dictates." 

"i would," reply the lady, "i could find somebody that has more power 
over you than i have. whom shall i call in to aid me in this arduous 
task?" 

"nay, dear madam, if i can resist your entreaties, surely no other can 
hope to succeed." 

"i am not sure of that," said my friend, archly; "there is one person in 
the world whose supplications, i greatly suspect, you would not 
withstand." 

"whom do you mean?" said i, in some trepidation. 

"you will know presently. unless i can prevail upon you, i shall be 
oblige to call for assistance." 

"spare me the pain of repeating that no power on earth can change my 
resolution." 

"that's a fib," she rejoined, with increase archness. "you know it is. 
if a certain person entreat you to stay, you will easily comply. i see i 
cannot hope to prevail by my own strength. that is a mortify 
consideration: but we must not part; that is a point settled. if nothing 
else will do, i must go and fetch my advocate. stay here a moment." 

i had scarcely time to breathe, before she returned, leading in clarice. 
i did not yet comprehend the meaning of this ceremony. the lady was 
overwhelm with sweet confusion. avert eyes and reluctant steps might 
have explain to me the purpose of this meeting, if i had believe that 
purpose to be possible. i felt the necessity of new fortitude, and 
struggle to recollect the motive that had hitherto sustain me. 

"there!" said my patroness; "i have been endeavour to persuade this 
young man to live with us a little longer. he is determined, it seems, 
to change his abode. he will not tell why, and i do not care to know, 
unless i could show his reason to be groundless. i have merely 
remonstrate with him on the folly of his scheme, but he has prove 
refractory to all i can say. perhaps your effort may meet with better 
success." 

clarice said not a word. my own embarrassment equally disabled me from 
speaking. regarding us both, for some time, with a benign aspect, mrs. 
lorimer resumed, taking a hand of each and join them together:-- 

"i very well know what it was that suggest this scheme. it is strange 
that you should suppose me so careless an observer as not to note, or 
not to understand, your situation. i am as well acquaint with what is 
passing in your heart as you yourself are: but why are you so anxious to 
conceal it? you know less of the adventurous of love than i should 
have suspected. but i will not trifle with your feelings. 

"you, clithero, know the wish that i once cherished. i had hope that 
my son would have found, in this darling child, an object worthy of his 
choice, and that my girl would have prefer him to all others. but i 
have long since discover that this could not be. they are nowise 
suit to each other. there is one thing in the next place desirable, 
and now my wish are accomplished. i see that you love each other; and 
never, in my opinion, was a passion more rational and just. i should 
think myself the worst of being if i did not contribute all in my power 
to your happiness. there is not the shadow of objection to your union. i 
know your scruples, clithero, and am sorry to see that you harbour them 
for a moment. nothing is more unworthy of your good sense. 

"i found out this girl long ago. take my word for it, young man, she 
do not fall short of you in the purity and tender of her 
attachment. what need is there of tedious preliminaries? i will leave 
you together, and hope you will not be long in coming to a mutual 
understanding. your union cannot be complete too soon for my wishes. 
clarice is my only and darling daughter. as to you, clithero, expect 
henceforth that treatment from me, not only to which your own merit 
entitle you, but which is due to the husband of my daughter."--with 
these words she retired, and left us together. 

great god! deliver me from the torment of this remembrance. that a 
being by whom i was snatch from penury and brutal ignorance, exalted 
to some rank in the intelligent creation, rear to affluence and 
honour, and thus, at last, spontaneous endow with all that remain 
to complete the sum of my felicity, that a being like this-but such 
thought must not yet be: i must shut them out, or i shall never arrive 
at the end of my tale. my effort have been thus far successful. i have 
hitherto been able to deliver a coherent narrative. let the last words 
that i shall speak afford some glimmer of my better days. let me 
execute without falter the only task that remains for me. 




chapter vi. 


how propitious, how incredible, was this event! i could scarcely confide 
in the testimony of my senses. was it true that clarice was before me, 
that she was prepared to countenance my presumption, that she had 
slight obstacle which i had deem insurmountable, that i was fondly 
beloved by her, and should shortly be admitted to the possession of so 
inestimable a good? i will not repeat the terms in which i pour forth, 
at her feet, the rapture of my gratitude. my impetuos soon extort 
from clarice a confirmation of her mother's declaration. an unrestrained 
intercourse was thenceforth establish between us. dejection and 
languor gave place, in my bosom, to the irradiate of joy and hope. my 
flowing fortune seem to have attain their utmost and immutable 
height. 

alas! they were destined to ebb with unspeakably-greater rapidity, and 
to leave me, in a moment, stranded and wrecked. 

our nuptials would have been solemnize without delay, had not a 
melancholy duty interfered. clarice had a friend in a distant part of 
the kingdom. her health had long been the prey of a consumption. she was 
now evidently tend to dissolution. in this extremity she entreat 
her friend to afford her the consolation of her presence. the only wish 
that remain was to die in her arms. 

this request could not but be willing comply with. it became me 
patient to endure the delay that would thence arise to the completion 
of my wishes. considering the urgent and mournful of the occasion, 
it was impossible for me to murmur, and the affectionate clarice would 
suffer nothing to interfere with the duty which she owe to her die 
friend. i accompany her on this journey, remain with her a few days, 
and then part from her to return to the metropolis. it was not 
imagine that it would be necessary to prolong her absence beyond a 
month. when i bade her farewell, and informed her on what day i propose 
to return for her, i felt no decay of my satisfaction. my thought were 
bright and full of exultation. why was not some intimate afford me 
of the snare that lay in my path? in the train laid for my destruction, 
the agent had so skilful contrived that my security was not molest 
by the faintest omen. 

i hasten to the crisis of my tale. i am almost dubious of my strength. 
the near i approach to it, the strong is my aversion. my courage, 
instead of gathering force as i proceed, decays. i am willing to dwell 
still long on preliminary circumstances. there are other incident 
without which my story would be lame. i retail them because they afford 
me a kind of respite from horrors at the thought of which every joint in 
my frame trembles. they must be endured, but that infirmity may be 
forgiven which make me inclined to procrastinate my suffering. 

i mention the lover whom my patroness was compelled, by the 
machination of her brother, to discard. more than twenty years had 
pass since their separation. his birth was mean and he was without 
fortune. his profession was that of a surgeon. my lady not only 
prevail upon him to abandon his country, but enable him to do this by 
supply his necessity from her own purse. his excellent 
understanding was, for a time, obscure by passion; but it was not 
difficult for my lady ultimately to obtain his concurrence to all her 
schemes. he saw and adore the rectitude of her motives, did not disdain 
to accept her gifts, and project means for maintain an epistolary 
intercourse during their separation. 

her interest procure him a post in the service of the east india 
company. she was, from time to time, informed of his motions. a war 
broke out between the company and some of the native powers. he was 
present at a great battle in which the english were defeated. she could 
trace him by his letters and by other circumstances thus far, but here 
the thread was discontinued, and no means which she employ could 
procure any tidings of him. whether he was captive, or dead, continued, 
for several years, to be merely matter of conjecture. 

on my return to dublin, i found my patroness engaged in conversation 
with a stranger. she introduce us to each other in a manner that 
indicate the respect which she entertain for us both. i survey and

ingenuous, but his sunburnt and rugged features bespoke a various and 
boisterous pilgrimage. the furrow of his brow were the product of
