energetic, and the impassioned bold of his address, as well as the 
tenor of his discourse, full of allusion to the past, and regrets that 
the course of event had not been different, made me suspect something 
extraordinary in his character. 

as soon as he left us, my lady explain who he was. he was no other

hindostan. he was sometimes a scholar of benares, and sometimes a

a pilgrim to mecca or to juggernaut. by a long, circuitous, and perilous

between two scotsmen. one of them had embrace the greece religion, and 
was betrothed to the daughter of a wealthy trader of that nation. he 
perish in the conflict, and the family of the lady not only procure 
the execution of his antagonist, but threaten to involve all those who 
were known to be connected with him in the same ruin. 

his life being thus endangered, it became necessary for him to seek a 
new residence. he flee from constantinople with such precipitation as

of alexander, as a mendicant. in the same character, he now wander

immure in cavern and carouse with robbers. his detail were

penetration, as well as the steadfast of his courage. 

after emerge from these wilds, he found his way along the bank of the 
arno to leghorn. thence he procure a passage to america, whence he had 
just returned, with many addition to his experience, but none to his 
fortune. 

this was a remarkable event. it did not at first appear how far its 
consequence would extend. the lady was, at present, disengaged and 
independent. though the passion which cloud her early prosperity was 
extinct, time had not diminish the worth of her friend, and they were 
far from have reach that age when love become chimerical and 
marriage folly. a confidential intercourse was immediately establish

but shall be comfortable situated for the rest of his life." all his

the vehemence of her solicitations. 

a cordial intimacy grew between me and the newly-arrived. our interview 
were frequent, and our communications without reserve. he detailed to me 
the result of his experience, and expatiate without end on the history

dwelt upon all the circumstances of his attachment to my patroness. on 
this subject i had heard only general details. i continual found 
cause, in the course of his narrative, to revere the illustrious 
quality of my lady, and to weep at the calamity to which the 
infernal malice of her brother had subject her. 

the tale of that man's misdeeds, amplify and dramatize by the 
indignant eloquence of this historian, oppress me with astonishment. 
if a poet had drawn such a portrait, i should have been prone to suspect 
the sound of his judgment. till now i had imagine that no character 
was uniform and unmixed, and my theory of the passion did not enable me 
to account for a propensity gratify merely by evil, and delight in 
shriek and agony for their own sake. 

it was natural to suggest to my friend, when expatiate on this theme, 
an inquiry as to how far subsequent event had obliterate the 
impression that were then made, and as to the plausible of reviving, 
at this more auspicious period, his claim on the heart of his friend. 
when he thought proper to notice these hints, he gave me to understand 
that time had made no essential alteration in his sentiment in this 
respect; that he still foster a hope, to which every day add new 
vigour; that, whatever was the ultimate event, he trust in his 
fortitude to sustain it, if adverse, and in his wisdom to extract from 
it the most valuable consequences, if it should prove prosperous. 

the progress of things was not unfavourable to his hopes. she treat 
his insinuation and profession with levity; but her argument seem 
to be urge with no other view than to afford an opportunity of 
confutation; and, since there was no abate of familiarity and 
kindness, there was room to hope that the affair would terminate 
agreeably to his wishes. 




chapter vii. 


clarice, meanwhile, was absent. her friend seemed, at the end of a

watch my motions. his face was turn towards me, and happened, at

"no." 

"it is arthur wiatte." 

"good heaven! it is impossible. what! my lady's brother?" 

"the same."

i was equally at a loss with my friend. this incident was so

sarsefield probably enjoy his usual slumber. his repose might not be

the landlord was charged with the care of the corpse till the morning,

chapter viii. 


till now my mind had been sway by the urgent of this occasion. 
these reflection were excluded, which rush tumultuous upon me the

moment, an entire change had been wrought in my condition. 

i had been oppress with a sense of the danger that flow from the

we be placed in security from his attempts, no effort of mind could

as i conceived, the most powerful sagacity and the firmest courage. now 
the danger was no more. the intelligence in which plan of mischief

long occupy the stage and been so fertile of calamity, were now at an

in the course of my meditations, the idea of the death of this man had 
occurred, and it bore the appearance of a desirable event. yet it was 
little qualified to tranquillize my fears. in the long catalogue of 
contingencies, this, indeed, was to be found; but it was as little 
likely to happen as any other. it could not happen without a series of 
anterior event paving the way for it. if his death came from us, it

possible that so tremendous a deed had been executed? was i not deceive 
by some portentous vision? i had witness the convulsion and last 
agony of wiatte. he was no more, and i was his destroyer! 

such was the state of my mind for some time after this dreadful event. 
previous to it i was calm, considerate, and self-collected. i marked 
the way that i was going. passing object were observed. if i advert 
to the series of my own reflections, my attention was not seize and 
fasten by them. i could disengage myself at pleasure, and could pass, 
without difficulty, from attention to the world within, to the 
contemplation of that without. 

now my liberty, in this respect, was at an end. i was fettered, 
confounded, smitten with excess of thought, and laid prostrate with 
wonder! i no long attend to my steps. when i emerge from my stupor, 
i found that i had trodden back the way which i had lately come, and had 
arrive within sight of the banker's door. i checked myself, and once 
more turn my steps homeward. 

this seem to be a hint for enter into new reflections. "the deed," 
said i, "is irretrievable. i have kill the brother of my patroness, 
the father of my love." 

this suggestion was new. it instantly involved me in terror and 
perplexity. how shall i communicate the tidings? what effect will they 
produce? my lady's sagacity is obscure by the benevolence of her 
temper. her brother was sordid wicked,--a hoary ruffian, to whom the 
language of pity was as unintelligible as the gabble of monkeys. his 
heart was fortify against compunction, by the atrocious habit of 
forty years; he live only to interrupt her peace, to confute the 
promise of virtue, and convert to rancour and reproach the fair dame of 
fidelity. 

he was her brother still. as a human being, his depravity was never 
beyond the health-restoring power of repentance. his heart, so long as 
it beat, was accessible to remorse. the singular of his birth had 
made her regard this being as more intimate her brother, than would 
have happen in different circumstances. it was her obstinate 
persuasion that their fates were blended. the rumour of his death she 
had never credited. it was a topic of congratulate to her friends, but 
of mourning and distress to her. that he would one day reappear upon the 
stage, and assume the dignity of virtue, was a source of consolation 
with which she would never consent to part. 

her character was now known. when the doom of exile was pronounced upon 
him, she deem it incumbent on her to vindicate herself from aspersion 
found on misconception of her motive in refuse her interference. 
the manuscript, though unpublished, was widely circulated. none could 
resist her simple and touching eloquence, nor rise from the peruse 
without resign his heart to the most impetuous impulse of 
admiration, and enlist himself among the eulogist of her justice and 
her fortitude. this was the only monument, in a written form, of her 
genius. as such it was engraven on my memory. the picture that it 
describe was the perpetual companion of my thoughts. 

alas! it had, perhaps, been well for me if it had been bury in eternal 
oblivion. i read in it the condemnation of my deed, the agony she was 
prepare to suffer, and the indignation that would overflow upon the 
author of so signal a calamity. 

i had rescue my life by the sacrifice of his. whereas i should have 
died. wretched and precipitate coward! what had become of my boast 
gratitude? such was the zeal that i had vow to her. such the service 
which it was the business of my life to perform. i had snatch her 
brother from existence. i had torn from her the hope which she so 
ardent and indefatigable cherished. from a contemptible and dastard 
regard to my own safety i had fail in the moment of trial and when 
call upon by heaven to evince the sincerity of my professions. 

she had treat my profession lightly. my vow of eternal devotion she 
had reject with lofty disinterestedness. she had arraign my 
impatience of obligation as criminal, and condemn every scheme i had 
project for free myself from the burden which her benefice had 
laid upon me. the impassioned and vehement anxiety with which, in former 
days, she had deprecate the vengeance of her lover against wiatte, rung 
in my ears. my senses were shock anew by the dreadful sounds, "touch 
not my brother. wherever you meet with him, of whatever outrage he be 
guilty, suffer him to pass in safety. despise me; abandon me; kill me. 
all this i can bear even from you; but spare, i implore you, my unhappy 
brother. the stroke that deprive him of life will not only have the 
same effect upon me, but will set my portion in everlasting misery." 

to these supplicate i had been deaf. it is true i had not rush upon 
him unarmed, intend no injury nor expect any. of that degree of 
wicked i was, perhaps, incapable. alas! i have immerse myself 
sufficient deep in crimes. i have trample under foot every motive 
dear to the heart of honour. i have shown myself unworthy the society of 
men. 

such were the turbulent suggestion of that moment. my pace slackened. i 
stopped, and was oblige to support myself against a wall. the sickness 
that had seize my heart penetrate every part of my frame. there was 
but one thing wanting to complete my distraction.--"my lady," said i, 
"believed her fate to be blend with that of wiatte. who shall affirm 
that the persuasion is a groundless one? she had live and prospered, 
notwithstanding the general belief that her brother was dead. she would 
not hearken to the rumour. why? because nothing less than indubitable 
evidence would suffice to convince her? because the counter-intimation 
flow from an infallible source? how can the latter supposition be 
confuted? has she not predict the event? 

"the period of terrible fulfilment has arrived. the same blow that 
bereaved _him_ of life has likewise ratify her doom. 

"she has been deceived. it is nothing more, perhaps, than a fond 
imagination. it matter not. who know not the cogency of faith? that 
the pulse of life are at the command of the will? the bearer of these 
tidings will be the messenger of death. a fatal sympathy will seize her. 
she will shrink, and swoon, and perish, at the news! 

"fond and short-sighted wretch! this is the price thou hast given for 
security. in the rash of thy thought, thou saidst, 'nothing is 
wanting but his death to restore us to confidence and safety.' lo! the 
purchase is made. havoc and despair, that were restrained during his 
life, were let loose by his last sigh. now only is destruction made 
sure. thy lady, thy clarice, thy friend, and thyself, are, by this act, 
involved in irretrievable and common ruin!" 

i start from my attitude. i was scarcely conscious of any transition. 
the interval was fraught with stupor, and amazement. it seem as if my 
senses had been hush in sleep, while the powers of locomotion were 
unconscious exert to bear me to my chamber. by whatever means the 
change was effected, there i was. 

i have been able to proceed thus far. i can scarcely believe the 
testimony of my memory that assure me of this. my task is almost 
executed; but whence shall i obtain strength enough to finish it? what i 
have told is light as gossamer, compare with the insupportable and 
crush horrors of that which is to come. heaven, in token of its 
vengeance, will enable me to proceed. it is fitting that my scene should 
thus close. 

my fancy began to be infect with the error of my understanding. the 
mood into which my mind was plunge was incapable of any propitious 
intermission. all within me was tempestuous and dark. my ear were 
accessible to no sound but those of shriek and lamentations. it was 
deepest midnight, and all the noise of a great metropolis were hushed. 
yet i listen as if to catch some strain of the dirge that was begun. 
sable robes, sobs, and a dreary solemnity encompass me on all sides, i 
was haunt to despair by image of death, imaginary clamours, and the 
train of funeral pageantry. i seem to have pass forward to a distant 
era of my life. the effects which were come were already realized. the 
foresight of misery create it, and set me in the midst of that hell 
which i feared. 

from a paroxysm like this the worst might reasonably be dreaded, yet the 
next step to destruction was not sudden taken. i pause on the brink 
of the precipice, as if to survey the depth of that frenzy that invade 
me; was able to ponder on the scene, and deliberate, in a state that 
partook of calm, on the circumstances of my situation. my mind was 
harass by the repetition of one idea. conjecture deepen into 
certainty. i could place the object in no light which did not 
corroborate the persuasion that, in the act committed, i had insured the 
destruction of my lady. at length my mind, somewhat relieved from the 
tempest of my fears, began to trace and analyze the consequence which i 
dreaded. 

the fate of wiatte would inevitable draw along with it that of his 
sister. in what way would this effect be produced? were they link 
together by a sympathy whose influence was independent of sensible 
communication? could she arrive at a knowledge of his miserable and by 
other than verbal means? i had heard of such extraordinary 
copartnership in being and mode of instantaneous intercourse among 
being locally distant. was this a new instance of the subtlety of mind? 
had she already endure his agonies, and like him already cease to 
breathe? 

every hair bristle at this horrible suggestion. but the force of 
sympathy might be chimerical. bury in sleep, or engaged in careless 
meditation, the instrument by which her destiny might be accomplished 
was the steel of an assassin. a series of events, equally beyond the 
reach of foresight with those which had just happened, might introduce, 
with equal abruptness, a similar disaster. what, at that moment, was her 
condition? repose in safety in her chamber, as her family imagined. 
but were they not deceived? was she not a mangle corpse? whatever were 
her situation, it could not be ascertained, except by extraordinary 
means, till the morning. was it wise to defer the scrutiny till then? 
why not instantly investigate the truth? 

these idea pass rapid through my mind. a considerable portion of 
time and amplify of phrase are necessary to exhibit, verbally, 
idea contemplate in a space of incalculable brevity. with the same 
rapid i conceive the resolution of determine the truth of my 
suspicions. all the family, but myself, were at rest. winding passage 
would conduct me, without danger of disturb them, to the hall, from 
which double staircase ascended. one of these led to a saloon above, on 
the east side of which was a door that communicate with a suite of 
rooms occupy by the lady of the mansion. the first was an antechamber, 
in which a female servant usually lay. the second was the lady's own 
bedchamber. this was a sacred recess, with whose situation, relative to 
the other apartments of the building, i was well acquainted, but of 
which i knew nothing from my own examination, have never been admitted 
into it. 

thither i was now resolve to repair. i was not deter by the sanctity 
of the place and hour. i was insensible to all consequence but the 
removal of my doubts. not that my hope were balanced by my fears. that 
the same tragedy had been perform in her chamber and in the street, 
nothing hinder me from believe with as much cogency as if my own 
eyes had witness it, but the reluctance with which we admit a 
detest truth. 

to terminate a state of intolerable suspense, i resolve to proceed 
forthwith to her chamber. i took the light and paced, with no 
interruption, along the galleries. i use no precaution. if i had met a 
servant or robber, i am not sure that i should have notice him. my 
attention was too perfectly engross to allow me to spare any to a 
casual object. i cannot affirm that no one observe me. this, however, 
was probable from the distribution of the dwelling. it consist of a 
central edifice and two wings, one of which was appropriate to 
domestic and the other, at the extremity of which my apartment was 
placed, comprehend a library, and rooms for formal and social and 
literary conferences. these, therefore, were desert at night, and my 
way lay along these. hence it was not likely that my steps would be 
observed. 

i proceed to the hall. the principal parlour was beneath her chamber. 
in the confusion of my thoughts, i mistook one for the other. i 
rectified, as soon as i detected, my mistake. i ascended, with a beating 
heart, the staircase. the door of the antechamber was unfastened. i 
entered, total regardless of disturb the girl who slept within. the 
bed which she occupy was conceal by curtains. whether she were 
there, i did not stop to examine. i cannot recollect that any token 
were given of wakeful or alarm. it was not till i reach the door 
of her own apartment that my heart began to falter. 

it was now that the momentous of the question i was about to decide 
rush with its genuine force upon my apprehension. appall and aghast, 
i had scarcely power to move the bolt. if the imagination of her death 
was not to be supported, how should i bear the spectacle of wound and 
blood? yet this was reserved for me. a few pace would set me in the 
midst of a scene of which i was the abhor contriver. was it right to 
proceed? there were still the remnant of doubt. my foreboding might 
possibly be groundless. all within might be safety and serenity. a 
respite might be gain from the execution of an irrevocable sentence. 
what could i do? was not any thing easy to endure in comparison with the 
agony of suspense? if i could not obviate the evil i must bear it, but 
the torment of suspense were susceptible of remedy. 

i drew back the bolt, and enter with the reluctance of fear, rather 
than the cautious of guilt. i could not lift my eyes from the 
ground. i advanced to the middle of the room. not a sound like that of 
the die salute my-ear. at length, shake off the fetter of 
hopelessness, i look up. 

i saw nothing calculate to confirm my fears. everywhere there reign 
quiet and order. my heart leap with exultation. "can it be," said i, 
"that i have been betray with shadows?--but this is not sufficient." 

within an alcove was the bed that belong to her. if her safety were 
inviolate, it was here that she reposed. what remain to convert 
torment doubt into ravishing certainty? i was insensible to the 
peril of my present situation. if she, indeed, were there, would not my 
intrusion awaken her? she would start and perceive me, at this hour, 
standing at her bedside. how should i account for an intrusion so 
unexampled and audacious? i could not communicate my fears. i could not 
tell her that the blood with which my hand were stain had flow from 
the wound of her brother. 

my mind was inaccessible to such considerations. they did not even 
modify my predominant idea. obstacle like these, had they existed, 
would have been trample under foot. 

leave the lamp, that i bore, on the table, i approach the bed. i 
slow drew aside the curtain, and beheld her tranquil slumbering. i 
listened, but so profound was her sleep, that not even her breathing 
could be overheard. i drop the curtain and retired. 

how blissful and mild were the illuminations of my bosom at this 
discovery! a joy that surpass all utterance succeed the fierce 
of desperation. i stood, for some moments, wrap in delightful 
contemplation. alas! it was a luminous but transient interval. the 
madness to whose black suggestion it bore so strong a contrast began 
now to make sensible approach on my understanding. 

"true," said i, "she lives. her slumber is serene and happy. she is 
blind to her approach destiny. some hour will at least be rescue 
from anguish and death. when she wakes, the phantom that soothe her 
will vanish. the tidings cannot be withheld from her. the murder of 
thy brother cannot hope to enjoy thy smiles. those ravishing accents, 
with which thou hast use to greet me, will be changed. scowl and 
reproaches, the invective of thy anger and the malediction of thy 
justice, will rest upon my head, 

"what is the blessing which i made the theme of my boastful arrogance? 
this interval of being and repose is momentary. she will awake, but only 
to perish at the spectacle of my ingratitude. she will awake only to the 
consciousness of instantly-impending death. when she again sleep she 
will wake no more. i, her son,--i, whom the law of my birth doom to 
poverty and hardship, but whom her unsolicited benefice snatch from 
those evils, and endow with the highest good known to intelligent 
beings, the consolation of science and the blandishments of 
affluence,--to whom the darling of her life, the offspring in whom are 
faithfully preserve the lineament of its angel mother, she has not 
denied! what is the recompense that i have made? how have i discharge 
the measureless debt of gratitude to which she is entitled? thus!-- 

"cannot my guilt be extenuated? is there not a good that i can do thee? 
must i perpetrate unmingled evil? is the province assign me that of an 
infernal emissary, whose effort are concentre in a single purpose, and 
that purpose a malignant one? i am the author of thy calamities. 
whatever misery is reserved for thee, i am the source whence it flows. 
can i not set bounds to the stream? cannot i prevent thee from return 
to a consciousness which, till it cease to exist, will not cease to be 
rent and mangled? 

"yes. it is in my power to screen thee from the coming storm; to 
accelerate thy journey to rest. i will do it." 

the impulse was not to be resisted. i move with the sudden of 
lightning. armed with a pointed implement that lay----it was a dagger. 
as i set down the lamp, i struck the edge. yet i saw it not, or notice 
it not till i need its assistance. by what accident it came hither, to 
what deed of dark it had already been subservient, i had no power to 
inquire. i step to the table and seize it. 

the time which this action require was insufficient to save me. my doom 
was ratify by powers which no human energy can counterwork.--need i 
go further? did you entertain any imagination of so frightful a 
catastrophe? i am overwhelm by turn with dismay and with wonder. i am 
prompt by turn to tear my heart from my breast and deny faith to the 
verdict of my senses. 

was it i that hurried to the deed? no. it was the demon that possessed 
me. my limb were guide to the bloody office by a power foreign and 
superior to mine. i had been defrauded, for a moment, of the empire of 
my muscles. a little moment for that sufficed. if my destruction had 
not been decreed, why was the image of clarice so long excluded? yet why 
do i say long? the fatal resolution was conceived, and i hasten to the 
execution, in a period too brief for more than itself to be view by 
the intellect. 

what then? were my hand imbrue in this precious blood? was it to this 
extremity of horror that my evil genius was determined to urge me? too 
surely this was his purpose; too surely i was qualified to be its 
minister. 

i lift the weapon. its point was aim at the bosom of the sleeper. 
the impulse was given. 

at the instant a piercing shriek was utter behind me, and a 
stretched-out hand, grasping the blade, made it swerve widely from its 
aim. it descended, but without inflict a wound. its force was spent 
upon the bed. 

oh for words to paint that stormy transition! i loose my hold of the 
dagger. i start back, and fixed eyes of frantic curiosity on the 
author of my rescue. he that interpose to arrest my deed, that start 
into being and activity at a moment so pregnant with fate, without 
token of his purpose or his coming being previous imparted, could 
not, methought, be less than divinity. 

the first glance that i dart on this being corroborate my conjecture. 
it was the figure and lineament of mrs. lorimer. negligent habit in 
flowing and brilliant white, with features burst with terror and 
wonder, the likeness of that being who was stretch upon the bed now 
stood before me. 

all that i am able to conceive of angel was comprise in the moral 
constitution of this woman. that her genius had overleap all bounds, 
and interpose to save her, was no audacious imagination. in the state 
in which my mind then was, no other belief than this could occupy the 
first place. 

my tongue was tied. i gaze by turn upon her who stood before me, and 
her who lay upon the bed, and who, awaken by the shriek that had been 
uttered, now open her eyes. she start from her pillow, and, by 
assume a new and more distinct attitude, permit me to recognise 
_clarice herself_! 

three days before, i had left her, beside the bed of a die friend, at 
a solitary mansion in the mountain of donegal. here it had been her 
resolution to remain till her friend should breathe her last. fraught 
with this persuasion, knowing this to be the place and hour of repose of 
my lady, hurried forward by the impetuos of my own conceptions, 
deceive by the faint gleam which penetrate through the curtain and 
imperfectly-irradiated features which bore, at all times, a powerful 
resemblance to those of mrs. lorimer, i had rush to the brink of this 
terrible precipice! 

why did i linger on the verge? why, thus perilous situated, did i not 
throw myself headlong? the steel was yet in my hand. a single blow would 
have pierce my heart, and shut out from my remembrance and foresight 
the past and the future. 

the moment of insanity had gone by, and i was once more myself. instead 
of regarding the act which i had meditate as the dictate of compassion 
or of justice, it only add to the sum of my ingratitude, and gave 
wings to the whirlwind that was sent to bear me to perdition. 

perhaps i was influence by a sentiment which i had not leisure to 
distribute into parts. my understanding was, no doubt, bewilder in the 
maze of consequence which would spring from my act. how should i 
explain my coming hither in this murderous guise, my arm lift to 
destroy the idol of my soul and the darling child of my patroness? in 
what words should i unfold the tale of wiatte, and enumerate the motive 
that terminate in the present scene? what penalty had not my 
infatuation and cruelty deserved? what could i less than turn the 
dagger's point against my own bosom? 

a second time, the blow was thwart and diverted. once more this 
beneficent interpose held my arm from the perpetrate of a new 
iniquity. once more frustrate the instigation of that demon, of whose 
malice a mysterious destiny had consign me to be the sport and the 
prey. 

every new moment add to the sum of my inexpiable guilt. murder was 
succeeded, in an instant, by the more detest enormity of suicide. 
she to whom my ingratitude was flagrant in proportion to the benefit of 
which she was the author, had now add to her former acts that of 
rescue me from the last of mischiefs. 

i threw the weapon on the floor. the zeal which prompt her to seize my 
arm, this action occasion to subside, and to yield place to those 
emotion which this spectacle was calculate to excite. she watch me 
in silence, and with an air of ineffable solicitude. clarice, govern 
by the instinct of modesty, wrap her bosom and face in the 
bedclothes, and testify her horror by vehement but scarcely-articulate 
exclamations. 

i move forward, but my steps were random and tottering. my thought 
were fetter by reverie, and my gesticulate destitute of meaning. my 
tongue falter without speaking, and i felt as if life and death were 
struggle within me for the mastery. 

my will, indeed, was far from being neutral in this contest. to such as 
i, annihilate is the supreme good. to shake off the ill that fasten 
on us by shake off existence, is a lot which the system of nature has 
deny to man. by escape from life, i should be deliver from this 
scene, but should only rush into a world of retribution, and be immerse 
in new agonies. 

i was yet to live. no instrument of my deliverance was within reach. i 
was powerless. to rush from the presence of these women to hide me 
forever from their scrutiny and their upbraiding, to snatch from their 
mind all trace of the existence of clithero, was the scope of 
unutterable longings. 

urge to flight by every motive of which my nature was susceptible, i 
was yet rooted to the spot. had the pause been only to be interrupt by 
me, it would have last forever. 

at length, the lady, clasp her hand and lift them, exclaimed, in 
a tone melting into pity and grief,-- 

"clithero! what is this? how came you hither, and why?" 

i struggle for utterance:--"i came to murder you. your brother has 
perish by my hands. fresh from the commission of this deed, i have 
hasten hither to perpetrate the same crime upon you." 

"my brother!" reply the lady, with new vehemence. "oh, say not so! i 
have just heard of his return, from sarsefield, and that he lives." 

"he is dead," repeated i, with fierceness; "i know it. it was i that 
kill him." 

"dead!" she faint articulated. "and by thee, clithero? oh! cursed 
chance that hinder thee from killing me also! dead! then is the omen 
fulfilled! then am i undone! lost forever!" 

her eyes now wander from me, and her countenance sunk into a wild and 
rueful expression. hope was utterly extinguish in her heart, and life 
forsook her at the same moment. she sunk upon the floor pallid and 
breathless. 

how she came into possession of this knowledge i know not. it is 
possible that sarsefield had repent of concealment, and, in the 
interval that pass between our separation and my encounter with 
wiatte, had returned, and informed her of the reappear of this 
miscreant. 

thus, then, was my fate consummated. i was rescue from destroy her 
by a dagger, only to behold her perish by the tidings which i brought. 
thus was every omen of mischief and misery fulfilled. thus was the 
enmity of wiatte render efficacious, and the instrument of his 
destruction change into the executioner of his revenge. 

such is the tale of my crimes. it is not for me to hope that the curtain 
of oblivion will ever shut out the dismal spectacle. it will haunt me 
forever. the torment that grow out of it can terminate only with the 
thread of my existence, but that, i know full well, will never end. 
death is but a shift of the scene; and the endless progress of 
eternity, which to the good is merely the perfection of felicity, is to 
the wicked an accumulation of woe. the self-destroyer is his own enemy: 
this has ever been my opinion. hitherto it has influence my actions. 
now, though the belief continues, its influence on my conduct is 
annihilated. i am no stranger to the depth of that abyss into which i 
shall plunge. no matter. change is precious for its own sake. 

well, i was still to live. my abode must be somewhere fixed. my conduct 
was henceforth the result of a perverse and rebellious principle. i 
banish myself forever from my native soil. i vow never more to 
behold the face of my clarice, to abandon my friends, my books, all my 
wonted labour and accustomed recreations. 

i was neither ashamed nor afraid. i considered not in what way the 
justice of the country would affect me. it merely made no part of my 
contemplations. i was not embarrass by the choice of expedient for 
trammel up the visible consequence and for elude suspicion. the 
idea of abjure my country and flying forever from the hateful scene 
partook, to my apprehension, of the vast, the boundless, and strange; of 
plunge from the height of fortune to obscurity and indigence, 
correspond with my present state of mind. it was of a piece with the 
tremendous and wonderful event that had just happened. 

these were the image that haunt me, while i stood speechless gaze 
at the ruin before me. i heard a noise from without, or imagine that i 
heard it. my reverie was broken, and my muscular power restored. i 
descended into the street, through door of which i possessed one set of 
keys, and hurried by the shortest way beyond the precincts of the city. 
i had laid no plan. my conception with regard to the future were 
shapeless and confused. successive incident supply me with a clue, 
and suggested, as they rose, the next step to be taken. i threw off the 
garb of affluence, and assume a beggar's attire. that i had money about 
me for the accomplishment of my purpose was wholly accidental. i 
travelled along the coast, and, when i arrive at one town, knew not why 
i should go farther; but my restless was unabated, and change was 
some relief. i it length arrive at belfast. a vessel was prepare for 
america. i embrace eager the opportunity of passing into a new world. 
i arrive at philadelphia. as soon as i landed i wander hither, and 
was content to wear out my few remain days in the service of 
inglefield. 

i have no friends. why should i trust my story to mother? i have no 
solicitude about concealment; but who is there who will derive pleasure 
or benefit from my rehearsal? and why should i expatiate on so hateful a 
scheme? yet now have i consent to this. i have confide in you the 
history of my disasters. i am not fearful of the use that you may be 
disposed to make of it. i shall quick set myself beyond the reach of 
human tribunals. i shall relieve the minister of law from the trouble 
of punishing. the recent event which induce you to summon me to this 
conference have likewise determined me to make this disclosure. 

i was not aware, for some time, of my perturb sleep. no wonder that 
sleep cannot soothe misery like mine; that i am alike infest by 
memory in wakeful and slumber. yet i was anew distress by the 
discovery that my thought found their way to my lips, without my being 
conscious of it, and that my steps wander forth unknowing and 
without the guidance of my will. 

the story you have told is not incredible. the disaster to which you 
allude did not fail to excite my regret. i can still weep over the 
untimely fall of youth and worth. i can no otherwise account for my 
frequent his shade than by the distant resemblance which the death of 
this man bore to that of which i was the perpetrator. this resemblance 
occur to me at first. if he were able to weaken the impression which 
was produce by my crime, this similitude was adapt to revive and 
enforce them. 

the wilderness, and the cave to which you follow me, were familiar to 
my sunday rambles. often have i indulge in audible grief on the cliff 
of that valley. often have i brood over my sorrow in the recess of 
that cavern. this scene is adapt to my temper. its mountainous 
asperity supply me with image of desolate and seclusion, and its 
headlong stream lull me into temporary forgetful of mankind. 

i comprehend you. you suspect me of concern in the death of waldegrave. 
you could not do otherwise. the conduct that you have witness was that 
of a murderer. i will not upbraid you for your suspicions, though i have 
bought exemption from them at a high price. 




chapter ix. 


there end his narrative. he start from the spot where he stood, and, 
without afford me any opportunity of reply or commenting, 
disappear amidst the thickest of the wood. i had no time to exert 
myself for his detention. i could have use no argument for this end, 
to which it is probable he would have listened. the story i had heard 
was too extraordinary, too completely the reverse of all my 
expectations, to allow me to attend to the intimate of self-murder 
which he dropped. 

the secret which i imagine was about to be disclose was as inscrutable 
as ever. not a circumstance, from the moment when clithero's character 
became the subject of my meditations, till the conclusion of his talk, 
but serve to confirm my suspicion. was this error to be impute to 
credulity. would not any one, from similar appearances, have drawn 
similar conclusions? or is there a criterion by which truth can always 
be distinguished? was it owe to my imperfect education that the 
inquietude of this man were not trace to a deed perform at the 
distance of a thousand leagues, to the murder of his patroness and 
friend? 

i had heard a tale which apparently related to scene and person far 
distant: but, though my suspicion have appear to have been misplaced, 
what should hinder but that the death of my friend was, in like manner, 
an act of momentary insanity and originate in a like spirit of mistaken 
benevolence? 

but i did not consider this tale merely in relation to myself. my life 
had been limited and uniform. i had commune with romance and 
historians, but the impression made upon me by this incident was 
unexampled in my experience. my reading had furnish me with no 
instance in any degree parallel to this, and i found that to be a 
distant and second-hand spectator of event was widely different from 
witness them myself and partake in their consequences. my judgment 
was, for a time, sunk into imbecility and confusion. my mind was full of 
the image unavoidable suggest by this tale, but they exist in a 
kind of chaos, and not otherwise than gradual was i able to reduce 
them to distinct particulars, and subject them to a deliberate and 
methodical inspection. 

how was i to consider this act of clithero? what a deplorable 
infatuation! yet it was the necessary result of a series of idea 
mutual link and connected. his conduct was dictate by a motive 
allied to virtue. it was the fruit of an ardent and grateful spirit. 

the death of wiatte could not be censured. the life of clithero was 
unspeakable more valuable than that of his antagonist. it was the 
instinct of self-preservation that sway him. he knew not his adversary 
in time enough to govern himself by that knowledge. had the assailant 
been an unknown ruffian, his death would have been follow by no 
remorse. the spectacle of his die agony would have dwelt upon the 
memory of his assassin like any other mournful sight, in the production 
of which he bore no part. 

it must at least be said that his will was not concerned in this 
transaction. he act in obedience to an impulse which he could not 
control nor resist. shall we impute guilt where there is no design? 
shall a man extract food for self-reproach from an action to which it is 
not enough to say that he was actuate by no culpable intention, but 
that he was sway by no intention whatever? if consequence arise that 
cannot be foreseen, shall we find no refuge in the persuasion of our 
rectitude and of human frailty? shall we deem ourselves criminal because 
we do not enjoy the attribute of deity? because our power and our 
knowledge are confine by impassable boundaries? 

but whence arose the subsequent intention? it was the fruit of a 
dreadful mistake. his intent were noble and compassionate. but this is 
of no avail to free him from the imputation of guilt. no remembrance of 
past benefice can compensate for this crime. the scale loaded with 
the recrimination of his conscience, is immovable by any 
counter-weight. 

but what are the conclusion to be drawn by dispassionate observers? is 
it possible to regard this person with disdain or with enmity? the crime 
originate in those limitation which nature has impose upon human 
faculties. proof of a just intention are all that are requisite to 
exempt us from blame; he is thus, in consequence of a double mistake. 
the light in which he view this event is erroneous. he judge wrong, 
and is therefore miserable. 

how imperfect are the grounds of all our decision was it of no use to 
superintend his childhood, to select his instructor and examples, to 
mark the operation of his principles, to see him emerge into youth, 
to follow him through various scene and try vicissitudes, and mark 
the uniform of his integrity? who would have predict his future 
conduct? who would not have affirm the impossible of an action like 
this? 

how mysterious was the connection between the fate of wiatte and his 
sister! by such circuitous and yet infallible means were the prediction 
of the lady and the vengeance of the brother accomplished! in how many 
case may it be said, as in this, that the prediction was the cause of 
its own fulfilment! that the very act which considerate observers, and 
even himself, for a time, imagine to have utterly preclude the 
execution of wiatte's menaces, should be that inevitable leading to it! 
that the execution should be assign to him who, abound in 
abhorrence, and in the act of self-defence, was the slay of the 
menacer! 

as the obstruct of his designs, wiatte waylaid and assault clithero. 
he perish in the attempt. were his designs frustrated? no. it was thus 
that he secure the gratification of his vengeance. his sister was cut 
off in the bloom of life and prosperity. by a refinement of good 
fortune, the voluntary minister of his malice had entail upon himself 
exile without reprieve and misery without end. 

but what chiefly excited my wonder was the connection of this tale with 
the destiny of sarsefield. this was he whom i have frequent mention 
to you as my preceptor. about four years previous to this era, he 
appear in this district without fortune or friend. he desired, one 
evening, to be accommodate at my uncle's house. the conversation 
turning on the object of his journey and his present situation, he 
professed himself in search of lucrative employment. my uncle propose 
to him to become a teacher, there being a sufficient number of young 
people in this neighbourhood to afford him occupation and subsistence. 
he found it his interest to embrace this proposal. 

i, of course, became his pupil, and demean myself in such a manner as 
speedy to grow into a favourite. he communicate to us no part of his 
early history, but informed us sufficient of his adventure in asia 
and italy to make it plain that this was the same person allude to by 
clithero. during his abode among us his conduct was irreproachable. when 
he left us, he manifest the most poignant regret, but this originate 
chiefly in his regard to me. he promise to maintain with me an 
epistolary intercourse. since his departure, however, i had heard 
nothing respecting him. it was with unspeakable regret that i now heard 
of the disappointment of his hopes, and was inquisitive respecting the 
measure which he would adopt in his new situation. perhaps he would' 
once more return to america, and i should again be admitted to the 
enjoyment of his society. this event i anticipate with the highest 
satisfaction. 

at present, the fate of the unhappy clithero was the subject of abundant 
anxiety. on his sudden leave me, at the conclusion of his tale, i 
supposed that he had gone upon one of his usual rambles, and that it 
would terminate only with the day. next morning a message was received 
from inglefield, inquiring if any one knew what had become of his 
servant. i could not listen to this message with tranquillity, i 
recollect the hint that he had given of some design upon his life, 
and admitted the most dreary forebodings. i speed to inglefield's. 
clithero had not returned, they told me, the preceding evening. he had 
not apprize them of any intention to change his abode. his boxes, and 
all that compose his slender property, were found in their ordinary 
state. he had express no dissatisfaction with his present condition. 

several days passed, and no tidings could be procure of him. his 
absence was a topic of general speculation, but was a source of 
particular anxiety to no one but myself. my apprehension were surely 
built upon sufficient grounds. from the moment that we parted, no one 
had seen or heard of him. what mode of suicide he had selected, he had 
disabled us from discovering, by the impenetrable secrecy in which he 
had involved it. 

in the midst of my reflection upon this subject, the idea of the 
wilderness occurred. could he have execute his design in the deepest of 
its recesses? these were unvisited by human footsteps, and his bone 
might lie for age in this solitude without attract observation. to 
seek them where they lay, to gather them together and provide for them a 
grave, was a duty which appear incumbent on me, and of which the 
performance was connected with a thousand habitual sentiment and mixed 
pleasures. 

thou knowest my devotion to the spirit that breathe its inspiration in 
the gloom of forest and on the verge of streams. i love to immerse 
myself in shades and dells, and hold converse with the solemnity and 
secrecy of nature in the rude retreat of norwalk. the disappearance 
of clithero had furnish new incite to ascend its cliff and 
pervade its thickets, as i cherish the hope of meeting in my ramble 
with some trace of this man. but might he not still live? his words had 
impart the belief that he intended to destroy himself. this 
catastrophe, however, was far from certain. was it not in my power to 
avert it? could i not restore a mind thus vigorous, to tranquil and 
wholesome existence? could i not subdue his perverse disdain and 
immeasurable abhorrence of himself? his upbraid and his scorn were 
unmerited and misplaced. perhaps they argue frenzy rather than 
prejudice; but frenzy, like prejudice, was curable. reason was no less 
an antidote to the illusion of insanity like his, than to the illusion 
of error. 

i did not immediately recollect that to subsist in this desert was 
impossible. nuts were the only fruits it produced, and these were 
inadequate to sustain human life. if it were haunt by clithero, he 
must occasional pass its limit and beg or purloin victuals. this 
deportment was too humiliate and flagitious to be impute to him. 
there was reason to suppose him smitten with the charm of solitude, of 
a lonely abode in the midst of mountainous and rugged nature; but this 
could not be uninterrupted enjoyed. life could be support only by 
occasional visiting the haunt of men, in the guise of a thief or a 
mendicant. hence, since clithero was not known to have reappear at any 
farm-house in the neighbourhood, i was compel to conclude either that 
he had retired far from this district, or that he was dead. 

though i design that my leisure should chiefly be consume in the 
bosom of norwalk, i almost dismiss the hope of meeting with the 
fugitive. there were indeed two source of my hopeless on this 
occasion. not only it was probable that clithero had flee far away, but, 
should he have conceal himself in some nook or cavern within these 
precincts, his concealment was not to be traced. this arose from the 
nature of that sterile region. 

it would not be easy to describe the face of this district, in a few 
words. half of solesbury, thou knowest, admit neither of plough nor 
spade. the cultivable space lie along the river, and the desert, lie 
on the north, has gained, by some means, the appellation of norwalk. 
canst thou imagine a space, somewhat circular, about six mile in 
diameter, and exhibit a perpetual and intricate variety of craggy 
eminence and deep dells? 

the hollow are single, and wall around by cliffs, ever vary in 
shape and height, and have seldom any perceptible communication with 
each other. these hollow are of all dimensions, from the narrow and 
depth of a well, to the amplitude of one hundred yards. winter's snow is 
frequent found in these cavity at midsummer. the stream that burst 
forth from every crevice are thrown, by the irregularity of the 
surface, into numberless cascades, often disappear in mists or in 
chasms, and emerge from subterranean channels, and, finally, either 
subside into lakes, or quiet meander through the lower and more level 
grounds. 

wherever nature left a flat it is made rugged and scarcely passable by 
enormous and fallen trunks, accumulate by the storm of ages, and 
forming, by their slow decay, a moss-covered soil, the haunt of rabbit 
and lizards. these spot are obscure by the melancholy umbrage of 
pines, whose eternal murmur are in unison with vacancy and solitude, 
with the reverberation of the torrent and the whistle of the blasts. 
hickory and poplar, which abound in the lowlands, find here no foster 
elements. 

a sort of continue vale, winding and abrupt, leads into the midst of 
this region and through it. this vale serve the purpose of a road. it 
is a tedious maze and perpetual declivity, and requires, from the 
passenger, a cautious and sure foot. opening and ascent occasional 
present themselves on each side, which seem to promise you access to the 
interior region, but always terminate, soon or later, in insuperable 
difficulties, at the verge of a precipice or the bottom of a steep. 

perhaps no one was more acquaint with this wilderness than i, but my 
knowledge was extremely imperfect. i had traverse parts of it, at an 
early age, in pursuit of berry and nuts, or led by a roam 
disposition. afterwards the sphere of my ramble was enlarge and their 
purpose changed. when sarsefield came among us, i became his favourite 
scholar and the companion of all his pedestrian excursions. he was fond 
of penetrating into these recesses, partly from the love of picturesque 
scenes, partly to investigate its botanical and mineral productions, and 
partly to carry on more effectually that species of instruction which he 
had adopt with regard to me, and which chiefly consist in moralize 
narrative or synthetic reasonings. these excursion had familiarize 
me with its outline and most accessible parts; but there was much 
which, perhaps, could never be reach without wings, and much the only 
path to which i might forever overlook. 

every new excursion, indeed, add somewhat to my knowledge. new track 
were pursued, new prospects detected, and new summit were gained. my 
ramble were productive of incessant novelty, though they always 
terminate in the prospect of limit that could not be overleaped. but 
none of these had led me wide from my customary path than that which 
had taken place when in pursuit of clithero. i had a faint remembrance 
of the valley into which i had descended after him; but till then i had 
view it at a distance, and supposed it impossible to reach the bottom 
but by leap from a precipice some hundred feet in height. the 
opposite steep seem no less inaccessible, and the cavern at the bottom 
was impervious to any view which my former position had enable me to 
take of it. 

my intention to re-examine this cave and ascertain whither it led had, 
for a time, been suspend by different considerations. it was now 
revive with more energy than ever. i reflect that this had formerly 
been haunt by clithero, and might possibly have been the scene of the 
desperate act which he had meditated. it might at least conceal some 
token of his past existence. it might lead into space hitherto 
unvisited, and to summit from which wide landscape might be seen. 

one morning i set out to explore this scene. the road which clithero had 
taken was laborious circuitous. on my return from the first pursuit of 
him, i ascend the cliff in my former footsteps, but soon light on 
the beaten track which i have already described. this enable me to shun 
a thousand obstacle which had lately risen before me, and open an 
easy passage to the cavern. 

i once more traverse this way. the brow of the hill was gained. the 
ledge of which it consist afford sufficient footing, when the 
attempt was made, though view at a distance they seem to be too 
narrow for that purpose. as i descended the rugged stair, i could not 
but wonder at the temerity and precipitation with which this descent had 
formerly been made. it seem as if the noonday light and the tardiest 
circumspect would scarcely enable me to accomplish it; yet then it 
had been done with headlong speed, and with no guidance but the moon's 
uncertain rays. 

i reach the mouth of the cave. till now i had forgotten that a lamp or 
a torch might be necessary to direct my subterranean footsteps. i was 
unwill to defer the attempt. light might possibly be requisite, if 
the cave had no other outlet. somewhat might present itself within to 
the eyes, which might forever elude the hands, but i was more inclined 
to consider it merely as an avenue terminate in an opening on the 
summit of the steep, or on the opposite side of the ridge. caution might 
supply the place of light, or, have explore the cave as far as 
possible at present, i might hereafter return, better furnish for the 
scrutiny. 




chapter x. 


with these determinations, i proceeded. the entrance was low, and 
compel me to resort to hand as well as feet. at a few yard from the 
mouth the light disappeared, and i found myself immerse in the dunnest 
obscurity. had i not been persuade that another had gone before me, i 
should have relinquish the attempt. i proceed with the utmost 
caution, always ascertaining, by outstretched arms, the height and 
breadth of the cavity before me. in a short time the dimensions expand 
on all sides, and permit me to resume my feet. 

i walk upon a smooth and gentle declivity. presently the wall on one 
side, and the ceiling, recede beyond my reach. i began to fear that i 
should be involved in a maze, and should be disabled from returning. to 
obviate this danger it was requisite to adhere to the nearest wall, and 
conform to the direction which it should take, without stray through 
the palpable obscurity. whether the ceiling was lofty or low, whether 
the opposite wall of the passage was distant or near, this i deem no 
proper opportunity to investigate. 

in a short time, my progress was stop by an abrupt descent. i set 
down the advance foot with caution, being aware that i might at the 
next step encounter a bottomless pit. to the brink of such a one i 
seem now to have arrived. i stooped, and stretch my hand forward and 
downward, but all was vacuity. 

here it was needful to pause. i had reach the brink of a cavity whose 
depth it ava impossible to ascertain. it might be a few inch beyond 
my reach, or hundred of feet. by leap down i might incur no injury, 
or might plunge into a lake or dash myself to pieces on the points of 
rocks. 

i now saw with new force the propriety of being furnish with a light. 
the first suggestion was to return upon my footsteps, and resume my 
undertaking on the morrow. yet, have advanced thus far, i felt 
reluctance to recede without accomplish my purposes. i reflect 
likewise that clithero had bold enter this recess, and had certainly 
come forth at a different avenue from that at which he entered. 

at length it occur to me that, though i could not go forward, yet i 
might proceed along the edge of this cavity. this edge would be as safe 
a guidance, and would serve as well for a clue by which i might return, 
as the wall which it was now necessary to forsake. 

intense dark is always the parent of fears. impending injury cannot in 
this state be descried, nor shunned, nor repelled. i began to feel some 
falter of my courage, and seat myself, for a few minutes, on a 
stony mass which arose before me. my situation was new. the cavern i 
had hitherto met with in this desert were chiefly form of low-browed 
rocks. they were chambers, more or less spacious, into which twilight 
was at least admitted; but here it seem as if i were surround by 
barrier that would forever cut off my return to air and to light. 

presently i resume my courage and proceeded. my road appear now to 
ascend. on one side i seem still upon the verge of a precipice, and on 
the other all was empty and waste. i had gone no inconsiderable 
distance, and persuade myself that my career would speedy terminate. 
in a short time, the space on the left hand was again occupied, and i 
cautious proceed between the edge of the gulf and a rugged wall. as 
the space between them widen i adhere to the wall. 

i was not insensible that my path became more intricate and more 
difficult to retread in proportion as i advanced. i endeavour to 
preserve a vivid conception of the way which i had already passed, and 
to keep the image of the left and right-hand wall, and the gulf, in due 
succession in my memory. 

the path, which had hitherto been considerably smooth, now became rugged 
and steep. chill damps, the secret trepidation which attend me, the 
length and difficulty of my way, enhance by the ceaseless caution and 
the numerous expedient which the utter dark oblige me to employ, 
began to overpower my strength. i was frequent compel to stop and 
recruit myself by rest. these respite from toil were of use, but they 
could not enable me to prosecute an endless journey, and to return was 
scarcely a less arduous task than to proceed. 

i look anxious forward, in the hope of being comfort by some dim 
ray, which might assure me that my labour were approach an end. at 
last this propitious token appeared, and i issue forth into a kind of 
chamber, one side of which was open to the air and allow me to catch a 
portion of the checker sky. this spectacle never before excited such 
exquisite sensation in my bosom. the air, likewise, breathe into the 
cavern, was unspeakable delicious. 

i now found myself on the projecture of a rock. above and below, the 
hill-side was nearly perpendicular. opposite, and at the distance of 
fifteen or twenty yards, was a similar ascent. at the bottom was a glen, 
cold, narrow, and obscure. this projecture, which serve as a kind of 
vestibule to the cave, was connected with a ledge, by which, though not 
without peril and toil, i was conduct to the summit. 

this summit was higher than any of those which were interpose between 
itself and the river. a large part of this chaos of rocks and precipice 
was subjected, at one view, to the eye. the fertile lawn and vale 
which lay beyond this, the winding course of the river, and the slope 
which rose on its farther side, were parts of this extensive scene. 
these object were at any time fitted to inspire rapture. now my delight 
was enhance by the contrast which this lightsome and serene element 
bore to the gloom from which i had lately emerged. my station, also, 
was higher, and the limit of my view, consequently, more ample than any 
which i had hitherto enjoyed. 

i advanced to the outer verge of the hill, which i found to overlook a 
steep no less inaccessible, and a glen equally profound. i change 
frequent my station in order to diversify the scenery. at length it 
became necessary to inquire by what means i should return. i traverse 
the edge of the hill, but on every side it was equally steep and always 
too lofty to permit me to leap from it. as i kept along the verge, i 
perceive that it tend in a circular direction, and brought me back, 
at last, to the spot from which i had set out. from this inspection, it 
seem as if return was impossible by any other way than that through 
the cavern. 

i now turn my attention to the interior space. if you imagine a 
cylinder mass, with a cavity dug in the centre, whose edge conform 
to the exterior edge; and if you place in this cavity another cylinder, 
higher than that which surround it, but so small as to leave between 
its side and those of the cavity a hollow space, you will gain as 
distinct an image of this hill as words can convey. the summit of the 
inner rock was rugged and cover with tree of unequal growth. to reach 
this summit would not render my return easier; but its greater elevation 
would extend my view, and perhaps furnish a spot from which the whole 
horizon was conspicuous. 

as i had traverse the outer, i now explore the inner, edge of this 
hill. at length i reach a spot where the chasm, separate the two 
rocks, was narrow than at any other part. at first view, it seem as 
if it were possible to leap over it, but a near examination show me 
that the passage was impracticable. so far as my eye could estimate it, 
the breadth was thirty or forty feet. i could scarcely venture to look 
beneath. the height was dizzy, and the walls, which approach each 
other at top, recede at the bottom, so as to form the resemblance of an 
immense hall, light from a rift which some convulsion of nature had 
made in the roof. where i stood there ascend a perpetual mist, 
occasion by a torrent that dashed along the rugged pavement below. 

from these object i willing turn my eye upon those before and above 
me, on the opposite ascent. a stream, rush from above, fell into a 
cavity, which its own force seem gradual to have made. the noise and 
the motion equally attract my attention. there was a desolate and 
solitary grandeur in the scene, enhance by the circumstances in which 
it was beheld, and by the peril through which i had recently passed, 
that had never before been witness by me. 

a sort of sanctity and awe environed it, owe to the consciousness of 
absolute and utter loneliness. it was probable that human feet had never 
before gain this recess, that human eyes had never been fixed upon 
these gushing waters. the aboriginal inhabitant had no motive to lead 
them into cave like this and ponder on the verge of such a precipice. 
their successor were still less likely to have wander hither. since 
the birth of this continent, i was probably the first who had deviate 
thus remotely from the customary path of men. 

while muse upon these ideas, my eye was fixed upon the foam 
current. at length i look upon the rocks which confine and 
embarrass its course. i admire their fantastic shape and endless 
irregularities. passing from one to the other of these, my attention 
lighted, at length, as if by some magical transition, on--a human 
countenance! 

my surprise was so abrupt, and my sensation so tumultuous, that i 
forgot for a moment the perilous nature of my situation. i loosen my 
hold of a pine-branch, which had been hitherto one of my supports, and 
almost start from my seat. had my station been in a slight degree 
near the brink than it was, i should have fallen headlong into the 
abyss. 

to meet a human creature, even on that side of the chasm which i 
occupied, would have been wholly adverse to my expectation. my station 
was accessible by no other road than that through which i had passed, 
and no motive were imaginable by which other could be prompt to 
explore this road. but he whom i now beheld was seat where it seem 
impossible for human effort to have placed him. 

but this affected me but little in comparison with other incidents. not 
only the countenance was human, but, in spite of shaggy and tangle 
locks, and an air of melancholy wildness, i speedy recognise the 
features of the fugitive clithero! 

one glance was not sufficient to make me acquaint with this scene. i 
had come hither partly in pursuit of this man, but some casual appendage 
of his person, something which should indicate his past rather than his 
present existence, was all that i hope to find. that he should be found 
alive in this desert, that he should have gain this summit, access to 
which was apparently impossible, were scarcely within the boundary of 
belief. 

his scanty and coarse garb had been nearly rent away by bramble and 
thorns; his arms, bosom, and cheek were overgrown and half conceal by 
hair. there was somewhat in his attitude and looks denote more than 
anarchy of thought and passions. his rueful, ghastly, and immovable 
eyes testify not only that his mind was ravage by despair, but that 
he was pinched with famine. 

these proof of his misery thrill to my inmost heart. horror and 
shudder invade me as i stood gaze upon him, and, for a time, i was 
without the power of deliberate on the measure which it was my duty 
to adopt for his relief. the first suggestion was, by calling, to inform 
him of my presence. i knew not what counsel or comfort to offer. by what 
words to bespeak his attention, or by what topic to mollify his direful 
passions, i knew not. though so near, the gulf by which we were 
separate was impassable. all that i could do was to speak. 

my surprise and my horror were still strong enough to give a shrill and 
piercing tone to my voice. the chasm and the rocks loudene and 
reverberate my accent while i exclaimed,--"_man! clithero!_" 

my summons was effectual. he shook off his trance in a moment. he had 
been stretch upon his back, with his eyes fixed upon a craggy 
projecture above, as if he were in momentary expectation of its fall and 
crush him to atoms. now he start on his feet. he was conscious of 
the voice, but not of the quarter whence it came. he was look 
anxious around when i again spoke:--"look hither. it is i who called." 

he looked. astonishment was now mingle with every other dreadful 
meaning in his visage. he clasp his hand together and bent forward, 
as if to satisfy himself that his summon was real. at the next moment 
he drew back, placed his hand upon his breast, and fixed his eyes on 
the ground. 

this pause was not likely to be broken but by me. i was prepare again 
to speak. to be more distinct heard, i advanced close to the brink. 
during this action, my eye was necessarily withdrawn from him. have 
gain a somewhat near station, i look again, but--he was gone! 

the seat which he so lately occupy was empty. i was not forewarn of 
his disappearance or direct to the course of his flight by any 
rustling among leaves. these, indeed, would have been overpower by the 
noise of the cataract. the place where he sat was the bottom of a 
cavity, one side of which terminate in the verge of the abyss, but the 
other side were perpendicular or overhanging. surely he had not leap 
into this gulf; and yet that he had so speedy scale the steep was 
impossible. 

i look into the gulf, but the depth and the gloom allow me to see 
nothing with distinctness. his cries or groan could not be overheard 
amidst the uproar of the waters. his fall must have instantly destroy 
him, and that he had fallen was the only conclusion i could draw. 

my sensation on this incident cannot be easily described. the image of 
this man's despair, and of the sudden catastrophe to which my 
inauspicious interference had led, fill me with compunction and 
terror. some of my fear were relieved by the new conjecture, that, 
behind the rock on which he had lain, there might be some aperture or 
pit into which he had descended, or in which he might be concealed. 

i derive consolation from this conjecture. not only the evil which i 
dread might not have happened, but some alleviate of his misery was 
possible. could i arrest his footstep and win his attention, i might be 
able to insinuate the lesson of fortitude; but if words were impotent, 
and argument were nugatory, yet to sit by him in silence, to moisten 
his hand with tears, to sigh in unison, to offer him the spectacle of 
sympathy, the solace of believe that his demerit were not estimate 
by so rigid a standard by other as by himself, that one at least among 
his fellow-men regard him with love and pity, could not fail to be of 
benign influence. 

these thought inspired me with new zeal. to effect my purpose it was 
requisite to reach the opposite steep. i was now convinced that this was 
not an impracticable undertaking, since clithero had already perform 
it. i once more made the circuit of the hill. every side was steep and 
of enormous height, and the gulf was nowhere so narrow as at this spot. 
i therefore return hither, and once more ponder on the means of 
passing this tremendous chasm in safety. 

casting my eyes upward, i noted the tree at the root of which i was 
standing. i compare the breadth of the gulf with the length of the 
trunk of this tree, and it appear very suitable for a bridge. happily 
it grew obliquely, and, if fell by an axe, would probably fall of 
itself, in such a manner as to be suspend across the chasm. the stock 
was thick enough to afford me footing, and would enable me to reach the 
opposite declivity without danger or delay. 

a more careful examination of the spot, the site of the tree, its 
dimensions, and the direction of its growth, convinced me fully of the 
practicable of this expedient, and i determined to carry it into 
immediate execution. for this end i must hasten home, procure an axe, 
and return with all expedition hither. i took my former way, once more 
enter the subterranean avenue, and slow re-emerged into day. before 
i reach home, the evening was at hand, and my tired limb and jaded 
spirits oblige me to defer my undertaking till the morrow. 

though my limb were at rest, my thought were active through the night. 
i careful review the situation of this hill, and was unable to 
conjecture by what means clithero could place himself upon it. unless he 
occasional return to the habitable grounds, it was impossible for 
him to escape perishing by famine. he might intend to destroy himself by 
this means, and my first effort were to be employ to overcome this 
fatal resolution. to persuade him to leave his desolate haunt might be 
a laborious and tedious task; meanwhile, all my benevolent intentions 
would be frustrate by his want of sustenance. it was proper, therefore, 
to carry bread with me, and to place it before him. the sight of food, 
the urgent of hunger, and my vehement entreaties, might prevail on 
him to eat, though no expostulate might suffice to make him seek food 
at a distance. 




chapter xi. 


next morning i store a small bag with meat and bread, and, throw an 
axe on my shoulder, set out, without inform any one of my intentions, 
for the hill. my passage was render more difficult by these 
encumbrances, but my perseverance surmount every impediment, and i 
gained, in a few hours, the foot of the tree whose trunk was to serve me 
for a bridge. in this journey i saw no trace of the fugitive. 

a new survey of the tree confirmed my former conclusions, and i began my 
work with diligence. my stroke were repeated by a thousand echoes, and 
i pause at first, somewhat startle by reverberation which made it 
appear as if not one but a score of axe were employ at the same time 
on both side of the gulf. 

quick the tree fell, and exactly in the manner which i expect arid 
desired. the wide-spread limb occupy and choke up the channel of the 
torrent, and compel it to seek a new outlet and multiply its 
murmurs. i dare not trust myself to cross it in an upright posture, but 
clung, with hand and feet, to its rugged bark. have reach the

appeared, which, on a former day, had been conceal from my distant 
view by the rock. 

it was obvious to conclude that this was his present habitation, or that 
an avenue, conduct hither and terminate in the unexplored side of 
this pit, was that by which he had come hither, and by which he had

exact to each other. nature, at some former period, had occasion 
the solid mass to dispart at this place, and had thus afford access to 
the summit of the hill. loose stone and ragged points form the

i was now within a few yard of the surface of the rock. the passage 
open into a kind of chamber or pit, the side of which were not 
difficult to climb. i rejoice at the prospect of this termination of my 
journey. here i paused, and, throw my weary limb on the ground, 
began to examine the object around me, and to meditate on the steps 
that were next to be taken. 

my first glance light on the very being of whom i was in search.

though my footstep were perpetual stumble and sliding. this

dispel my apprehensions, and show me that he was merely bury in

last succeed by a sleep so oblivious. 

this meeting was, in the highest degree, propitious. it not only assured

thou knowest that i am qualified for such task neither by my education

be repel or divert into better path by effort so undisciplined as

how should i attempt to reason with him? how should i outroot 
prepossession so inveterate,--the fruits of his earliest education,

mistress he was actuated, not by avarice, or ambition, or revenge, or 
malice? he desire to confer on her the highest and the only benefit of 
which he believe her capable. he sought to rescue her from torment 
regrets and lingering agonies. 

these position were sufficient just to my own view, but i was not 
call upon to reduce them to practice. i had not to struggle with the 
consciousness of have been rescued, by some miraculous contingency, 
from imbrue my hand in the blood of her whom i adored; of have 
drawn upon myself suspicion of ingratitude and murder too deep to be 
ever effaced; of have bereft myself of love, and honour, and friends, 
and spotless reputation; of have doom myself to infamy and

evil which his malignant destiny had made the unalterable portion of 
clithero, and how should my imperfect eloquence annihilate these evils? 
every man, not himself the victim of irretrievable disasters, perceive 
the folly of ruminate on the past, and of foster a grief which 
cannot reverse or recall the decree of an immutable necessity; but 
every man who suffer is unavoidable shackle by the error which he 
censure in his neighbour, and his effort to relieve himself are as 
fruitless as those with which he attempt the relief of others. 

no topic, therefore, could be properly employ by me on the present 
occasion. all that i could do was to offer him food, and, by pathetic 
supplications, to prevail on him to eat. famine, however obstinate, 
would scarcely refrain when bread was placed within sight and reach. 
when made to swerve from his resolution in one instance, it would be 
less difficult to conquer it a second time. the magic of sympathy, the 
perseverance of benevolence, though silent, might work a gradual and 
secret revolution, and better thought might insensible displace those 
desperate suggestion which now govern him. 

have revolve these ideas, i placed the food which i had brought at

clithero believe his solitude to be unapproachable. what new expedient

by him on that occasion in which i was a witness of his behaviour. the 
earth was then hasty removed, and as hasty thrown again into the

was now likewise led to reflect on the possible motive that prompt

the action of a sleeper. somewhat was, perhaps, bury in this spot, 
connected with the history of mrs. lorimer or of clarice. was it not 
possible to ascertain the truth in this respect? 

there was but one method. by careful uncover this hole, and digging

one chamber in inglefield's house was usually reserved for visitants. in

the housekeeper was by no means convinced by these arguments, and at

accurate. the means by which they were made to cohere were invisible. 

appearances on every side were uniform, nor were there any mark by

during his residence with inglefield, many specimen of mechanical

i had no more right to do this than the inglefields; perhaps, indeed,

but could not my end be gain without violence? the means of opening

in spite of the testimony of my own feelings, the misery of clithero

there were likewise other motives, which, as they possessed some

also am a mechanist. i had construct a writing-desk and cabinet, in

therefore, with the eye of an artist, and was solicitous to know the 
principles on which it was formed. i determined to examine, and, if 
possible, to open it. 




chapter xii. 


i survey it with the utmost attention. all its parts appear equally 
solid and smooth. it could not be doubt that one of its side serve 
the purpose of a lid, and was possible to be raised. mere strength could 
not be applied to raise it, because there was no projecture which might 
be firm held by the hand, and by which force could be exerted. some 
spring, therefore, secret existed, which might forever elude the 
senses, but on which the hand, by being move over it in all directions, 
might accidental light. 

this process was effectual. a touch, casual applied at an angle, drove 
back a bolt, and a spring, at the same time, was set in action, by which 
the lid was raise above half an inch. no event could be supposed more 
fortuitous than this. a hundred hand might have sought in vain for this 
spring. the spot in which a certain degree of pressure was sufficient to 
produce this effect was, of all, the least likely to attract notice or 
awaken suspicion. 

i open the trunk with eagerness. the space within was divide into 
numerous compartments, none of which contained any thing of moment. 
tool of different and curious constructions, and remnant of minute

i now perceive that clithero had provided not only against the opening

that clithero should preserve this manuscript, amidst the wreck of his

i read this copious tale with unspeakable eagerness. it essentially 
agree with that which had been told by clithero. by drawing forth 
event into all their circumstances, more distinct impression were 
produce on the mind, and proof of fortitude and equanimity were here

the instability of life and happiness was forcibly illustrated, as well

the peruse of this volume end not but with the night. contrary to my

i pass through the cave and reach the bridge which my own ingenuity

as i crept with hand and feet along my imperfect bridge, a sudden gust

the purpose of this arduous journey was defeat by the loss of the

this deficiency, however, was easily supplied. i had only to return home

i did not just then reflect that clithero had found access to this hill

just as i had disposed of these encumbrance and had risen from my seat,
