untamable of that detest race. 

[footnote: the gray cougar. this animal has all the essential 
characteristic of a tiger. though somewhat inferior in size and 
strength, these are such as to make him equally formidable to man.] 

the industry of our hunter has nearly banish animal of prey from 
these precincts. the fastness of norwalk, however, could not but 
afford refuge to some of them. of late i had met them so rarely, that my 
fear were seldom alive, and i trod, without caution, the ruggedest and 
most solitary haunts. still, however, i had seldom been unfurnished in 
my ramble with the means of defence. 

my temper never delight in carnage and blood. i found no pleasure in 
plunge into bogs, wade through rivulets, and penetrating thickets, 
for the sake of dispatch woodcock and squirrels. to watch their 
gambol and flittings, and invite them to my hand, was my darling 
amusement when loiter among the wood and the rocks. it was much 
otherwise, however, with regard to rattlesnake and panthers. these i 
thought it no breach of duty to exterminate wherever they could be 
found. these judicious and sanguinary spoil were equally the enemy 
of man and of the harmless race that sport in the trees, and many of 
their skin are still preserve by me as trophy of my juvenile 
prowess. 

as hunting was never my trade or my sport, i never loaded myself with 
fowling-piece or rifle. assiduous exercise had made me master of a 
weapon of much easy carriage, and, within a moderate distance, more 
destructive and unerring. this was the tomahawk. with this i have often 
sever an oak-branch, and cut the sinew of a catamount, at the 
distance of sixty feet. 

the unfrequence with which i had lately encounter this foe, and the 
encumbrance of provision, made me neglect, on this occasion, to bring 
with me my usual arms. the beast that was now before me, when stimulate 
by hunger, was accustomed to assail whatever could provide him with a 
banquet of blood. he would set upon the man and the deer with equal and 
irresistible ferocity. his sagacity was equal to his strength, and he 
seem able to discover when his antagonist was armed and prepared for 
defence. 

my past experience enable me to estimate the full extent of my danger. 
he sat on the brow of the steep, eye the bridge, and apparently 
deliberate whether he should cross it. it was probable that he had 
scent my footstep thus far, and, should he pass over, his vigilance 
could scarcely fail of detect my asylum. the pit into which clithero 
had sunk from my view was at some distance. to reach it was the first 
impulse of my fear, but this could not be done without exciting the 
observation and pursuit of this enemy. i deep regret the untoward 
chance that had led me, when i first came over, to a different shelter. 

should he retain his present station, my danger was scarcely lessened. 
to pass over in the face of a famished tiger was only to rush upon my 
fate. the fall of the trunk, which had lately been so anxious 
deprecated, was now, with no less solicitude, desired. every new gust, i 
hoped, would tear asunder its remain bands, and, by cutting off all 
communication between the opposite steeps, place me in security. 

my hopes, however, were destined to be frustrated. the fibre of the 
prostrate tree were obstinate tenacious of their hold, and presently 
the animal scramble down the rock and proceed to cross it. 

of all kind of death, that which now menace me was the most abhorred. 
to die by disease, or by the hand of a fellow-creature, was propitious 
and lenient in comparison with being rent to pieces by the fang of this 
savage. to perish in this obscure retreat, by means so impervious to the 
anxious curiosity of my friends, to lose my portion of existence by so 
untoward and ignoble a destiny, was insupportable. i bitter deplore 
my rash in coming hither unprovided for an encounter like this. 

the evil of my present circumstances consist chiefly in suspense. my 
death was unavoidable, but my imagination had leisure to torment itself 
by anticipations. one foot of the savage was slow and cautious move 
after the other. he struck his claw so deep into the bark that they 
were with difficulty withdrawn. at length he leap upon the ground. we 
were now separate by an interval of scarcely eight feet. to leave the 
spot where i crouch was impossible. behind and beside me, the cliff 
rose perpendicularly, and before me was this grim and terrific visage. i 
shrunk still close to the ground and closed my eyes. 

from this pause of horror i was arouse by the noise occasion by a 
second spring of the animal. he leap into the pit, in which i had so 
deep regret that i had not taken refuge, and disappeared. my rescue 
was so sudden, and so much beyond my belief or my hope, that i doubted, 
for a moment, whether my senses did not deceive me. this opportunity of 
escape was not to be neglected. i left my place, and scramble over the 
trunk with a precipitation which had like to have prove fatal. the 
tree groan and shook under me, the wind blew with unexampled violence, 
and i had scarcely reach the opposite steep when the roots were 
sever from the rock and the whole fell thundering to the bottom of the 
chasm. 

my trepidation were not speedy quieted. i look back with wonder on 
my hairbreadth escape, and on that singular concurrence of event which 
had placed me, in so short a period, in absolute security. had the trunk 
fallen a moment earlier, i should have been imprison on the hill or 
thrown headlong. had its fall been delay another moment, i should have 
been pursued; for the beast now issue from his den, and testify his 
surprise and disappointment by token the sight of which made my blood 
run cold. 

he saw me, and hasten to the verge of the chasm. he squat on his 
hind-legs and assume the attitude of one prepare to leap. my 
consternation was excited afresh by these appearances. it seem at 
first as if the rift was too wide for any power of muscle to carry him 
in safety over; but i knew the unparalleled agile of this animal, and 
that his experience had made him a better judge of the practicable of 
this exploit than i was. still there was hope that he would relinquish 
this design as desperate. this hope was quick at an end. he sprung, 
and his fore-legs touched the verge of the rock on which i stood. in 
spite of vehement exertions, however, the surface was too smooth and too 
hard to allow him to make good his hold. he fell, and a piercing cry, 
utter below, show that nothing had obstruct his descent to the 
bottom. 

thus was i again rescue from death. nothing but the pressure of famine 
could have prompt this savage to so audacious and hazardous an effort; 
but, by yielding to this impulse, he had made my future visit to this 
spot exempt from peril. clithero was, likewise, relieved from a danger 
that was imminent and unforeseen. prowl over these grounds, the 
panther could scarcely have fail to meet with this solitary fugitive. 

had the animal lived, my first duty would have been to have sought him 
out and assail him with my tomahawk; but no undertaking would have 
been more hazardous. lurk in the grass, or in the branch of a tree, 
his eye might have descry my approach, he might leap upon me 
unperceived, and my weapon would be useless. 

with a heart beating with unwonted rapidity, i once more descended the 
cliff, enter the cavern, and arrive at hunt farm, drench with 
rain, and exhaust by fatigue. 

by night the storm was dispelled; but my exhaust strength would not 
allow me to return to the mountain. at the customary hour i retired to 
my chamber. i incessant ruminate on the adventure of the last day, 
and inquire into the conduct which i was next to pursue. 

the bridge being destroyed, my customary access was cut off. there was 
no possibility of restore this bridge. my strength would not suffice 
to drag a fallen tree from a distance, and there was none whose position 
would abridge or supersede that labour. some other expedient must, 
therefore, be discover to pass this chasm. 

i review the circumstances of my subterranean journey. the cavern was 
imperfect explored. its branch might be numerous. that which i had 
hitherto pursue terminate in an opening at a considerable distance 
from the bottom. other branch might exist, some of which might lead to 
the foot of the precipice, and thence a communication might be found 
with the summit of the interior hill. 

the danger of wandering into dark and untry paths, and the 
commodious of that road which had at first been taken, were 
sufficient reason for have hitherto suspend my examination of the 
different branch of this labyrinth. now my customary road was no 
long practicable, and another was to be careful explored. for this 
end, on my next journey to the mountain, i determined to take with me a 
lamp, and unravel this darksome maze: this project i resolve to execute 
the next day. 

i now recollect what, if it had more seasonable occurred, would have 
taught me caution. some month before this a farmer, living in the 
skirt of norwalk, discover two maraud in his field, whom he 
imagine to be a male and female panther. they had destroy some sheep, 
and had been hunt by the farmer with long and fruitless diligence. 
sheep had likewise been destroy in different quarters; but the owner 
had fixed the imputation of the crime upon dogs, many of whom had atone 
for their supposed offence by their death. he who had mention his 
discovery of panther received little credit from his neighbours; 
because a long time had elapse since these animal were supposed to 
have been exile from this district, and because no other person had 
seen them. the truth of this seem now to be confirmed by the testimony 
of my own senses; but, if the rumour were true, there still exist 
another of these animals, who might harbour in the obscurity of this 
desert, and against whom it was necessary to employ some precaution. 
henceforth i resolve never to traverse the wilderness unfurnished with 
my tomahawk. 

these images, mingle with those which the contemplation of futurity 
suggested, floated, for a time, in my brain, but at length gave place to 
sleep. 




chapter xiii. 


since my return home, my mind had been fully occupy by scheme and 
reflection relative to clithero. the project suggest by thee, and to 
which i had determined to devote my leisure, was forgotten, or 
remember for a moment and at wide intervals. what, however, was nearly 
banish from my waking thoughts, occur in an incongruous and 
half-seen form, to my dreams. during my sleep, the image of waldegrave 
flit before me. methought the sentiment that impel him to visit me 
was not affection or complacency, but inquietude and anger. some service 
or duty remain to be perform by me, which i had culpable neglected: 
to inspirit my zeal, to awaken my remembrance, and incite me to the 
performance of this duty, did this glimmer messenger, this 
half-indignant apparition, come. 

i commonly awake soon enough to mark the youngest dawn of the morning. 
now, in consequence perhaps of my perturb sleep, i open my eyes 
before the stars had lost any of their lustre. this circumstance 
produce some surprise, until the image that lately hover in my fancy 
were recalled, and furnish somewhat like a solution of the problem. 
connected with the image of my dead friend was that of his sister. the 
discourse that took place at our last interview; the scheme of 
transcribing, for thy use, all the letters which, during his short but 
busy life, i received from him; the nature of this correspondence, and 
the opportunity which this employment would afford me of contemplate 
these ample and precious monument of the intellectual existence and 
moral pre-eminence of my friend, occur to my thoughts. 

the resolution to prosecute the task was revived. the obligation of 
benevolence, with regard to clithero, was not discharged. this, neither 
duty nor curiosity would permit to be overlook or delayed; but why 
should my whole attention and activity be devoted to this man? the hour 
which were spent at home and in my chamber could not be more useful 
employ than in making my intended copy. 

in a few hour after sunrise i purpose to resume my way to the 
mountain. could this interval be appropriate to a better purpose than 
in count over my friend's letters, setting them apart from my own, 
and prepare them for that transcription from which i expect so high 
and yet so mournful a gratification? 

this purpose, by no violent union, was blend with the recollection of 
my dream. this recollection infuse some degree of waver and 
dejection into my mind. in transcribe these letters i should violate 
pathetic and solemn injunction frequent repeated by the writer. was 
there some connection between this purpose and the incident of my 
vision? was the latter sent to enforce the interdict which had been 
formerly imposed? 

thou art not fully acquaint with the intellectual history of thy 
brother. some information on that head will be necessary to explain the 
nature of that reluctance which i now feel to comply with thy request, 
and which had formerly so much excited thy surprise. 

waldegrave, like other men early devoted to meditation and books, had 
adopted, at different periods, different system of opinion on topic 
connected with religion and morals. his earliest creed tend to efface 
the impression of his education; to deify necessity and universal 
matter; to destroy the popular distinction between soul and body, and 
to dissolve the supposed connection between the moral condition of man 
anterior and subsequent to death. 

this creed he adopt with all the fulness of conviction, and propagate 
with the utmost zeal. soon after our friendship commenced, fortune 
placed us at a distance from each other, and no intercourse was allow 
but by the pen. our letters, however, were punctual and copious. those 
of waldegrave were too frequent devoted to the defence of his 
favourite tenets. 

thou art acquaint with the revolution that afterwards took place in 
his mind. placed within the sphere of religious influence, and listen 
daily to the reasoning and exhortation of mr. s----, whose benign 
temper and blameless deportment was a visible and constant lesson, he 
insensible resume the faith which he had relinquished, and became the 
vehement opponent of all that he had formerly defended. the chief object 
of his labours, in this new state of his mind, was to counteract the 
effect of his former reasoning on my opinions. 

at this time, other change took place in his situation, in consequence 
of which we were once more permit to reside under the same roof. the 
intercourse now cease to be by letter, and the subtle and laborious 
argument which he had formerly produce against religion, and 
which were contained in a permanent form, were combat in transient 
conversation. he was not only eager to subvert those opinion which he 
had contribute to instil into me, but was anxious that the letters and 
manuscript which had been employ in their support should be 
destroyed. he did not fear wholly or chiefly on my own account. he 
believe that the influence of former reasoning on my faith would be 
sufficient eradicate by the new; but he dread lest these 
manuscript might fall into other hands, and thus produce mischief 
which it would not be in his power to repair. with regard to me, the 
poison had been follow by its antidote; but with respect to others, 
these letters would communicate the poison when the antidote could not 
be administered. 

i would not consent to this sacrifice. i did not entire abjure the 
creed which had, with great copious and eloquence, been defend in 
these letters. besides, mixed up with abstract reasoning were 
numberless passage which elucidate the character and history of my 
friend. these were too precious to be consign to oblivion; and to take 
them out of their present connection and arrangement would be to 
mutilate and deform them. 

his entreaty and remonstrance were earnest and frequent, but always 
ineffectual. he had too much purity of motive to be angry at my 
stubbornness; but his sense of the mischievous tendency of these letters 
was so great, that my intractable cost him many a pang. 

he was now gone, and i had not only determined to preserve these 
monuments, but had consent to copy them for the use of another; for 
the use of one whose present and eternal welfare had been the chief 
object of his care and efforts. thou, like other of thy sex, art 
unaccustomed to metaphysics refinements. thy religion is the growth of 
sensibility and not of argument. thou art not fortify and prepossessed 
against the subtlety with which the being and attribute of the deity 
have been assailed. would it be just to expose thee to pollution and 
depravity from this source? to make thy brother the instrument of thy 
apostasy, the author of thy fall? that brother whose latter days were so 
ardent devoted to cherish the spirit of devotion in thy heart? 

these idea now occur with more force than formerly. i had promised, 
not without reluctance, to give thee the entire copy of his letters; but 
i now recede from this promise. i resolve merely to select for thy 
peruse such as were narrative or descriptive. this could not be done 
with too much expedition. it was still dark, but my sleep was at an end, 
and, by a common apparatus, that lay beside my bed, i could instantly 
produce a light. 

the light was produced, and i proceed to the cabinet where all my 
papers and books are deposited. this was my own contrivance and 
workmanship, undertaken by the advice of sarsefield, who took infinite 
pains to foster that mechanical genius which display itself so early 
and so forcibly in thy friend. the key belong to this was, like the 
cabinet itself, of singular structure. for greater safety, it was 
constant placed in a closet, which was likewise locked. 

the key was found as usual, and the cabinet opened. the letters were 
bound together in a compact form, lodge in a parchment case, and placed 
in a secret drawer. this drawer would not have been detect by common 
eyes, and it open by the motion of a spring, of whose existence none 
but the maker was conscious. this drawer i had open before i went to 
sleep, and the letters were then safe. 

thou canst not imagine my confusion and astonishment, when, on opening 
the drawer, i perceive that the packet was gone. i look with more 
attention, and put my hand within it; but the space was empty. whither 
had it gone, and by whom was it purloined? i was not conscious of have 
taken it away, yet no hand but mine could have done it. on the last 
evening i had doubtless remove it to some other corner, but had 
forgotten it. i task my understanding and my memory. i could not 
conceive the possibility of any motive induce me to alter my 
arrangement in this respect, and was unable to recollect that i had 
made this change. 

what remained? this invaluable relic had disappeared. every thought and 
every effort must be devoted to the single purpose of regain it. as 
yet i did not despair. until i had open and ransack every part of 
the cabinet in vain, i did not admit the belief that i had lost it. even 
then this persuasion was tumultuous and fluctuating. it had vanish to 
my senses, but these senses were abuse and depraved. to have passed, of 
its own accord, through the pore of this wood, was impossible; but, if 
it were gone, thus did it escape. 

i was lost in horror and amazement. i explore every nook a second and a 
third time, but still it elude my eye and my touch. i open my closet 
and cases. i pry everywhere, unfold every article of clothing, 
turn and scrutinize every instrument and tool, but nothing availed. 

my thought were not speedy collected or calmed. i threw myself on the 
bed and resigned myself to musing. that my loss was irretrievable was a 
supposition not to be endured. yet ominous terror haunt me,--a 
whisper intimate that a relic which i value more than life was 
torn forever away by some malignant and inscrutable destiny. the same 
power that had taken it from this receptacle was able to waft it over 
the ocean or the mountains, and condemn me to a fruitless and eternal 
search. 

but what was he that committed the theft? thou only, of the being who 
live, wast acquaint with the existence of these manuscripts. thou art 
many mile distant, and art utterly a stranger to the mode or place of 
their concealment. not only access to the cabinet, but access to the 
room, without my knowledge and permission, was impossible. both were 
lock during this night. not five hour had elapse since the cabinet 
and drawer had been opened, and since the letters had been seen and 
touched, being in their ordinary position. during this interval, the 
thief had entered, and despoil me of my treasure. 

this event, so inexplicable and so dreadful, threw my soul into a kind 
of stupor or distraction, from which i was sudden rouse by a footstep 
soft moving in the entry near my door. i start from my bed, as if i 
had gain a glimpse of the robber. before i could run to the door, some 
one knocked. i did not think upon the propriety of answer the signal, 
but hasten with tremulous finger and throb heart to open the 
door. my uncle, in his night-dress, and apparently just risen from his 
bed, stood before me! 

he marked the eager and perturbation of my looks, and inquire into 
the cause. i did not answer his inquiries. his appearance in my chamber 
and in this guise add to my surprise. my mind was full of the late 
discovery, and instantly conceive some connection between this 
unseasonable visit and my lost manuscript. i interrogate him in my turn 
as to the cause of his coming. 

"why," said he, "i came to ascertain whether it was you or not who 
amuse himself so strange at this time of night. what is the matter 
with you? why are you up so early?" 

i told him that i had been rouse by my dreams, and, finding no 
inclination to court my slumber back again, i had risen, though early 
by some hour than the usual period of my rising. 

"but why did you go up-stairs? you might easily imagine that the sound 
of your steps would alarm those below, who would be puzzle to guess who 
it was that had thought proper to amuse himself in this manner." 

"up-stairs? i have not left my room this night. it is not ten minutes 
since i awoke, and my door has not since been opened." 

"indeed! that is strange. nay, it is impossible! it was your feet surely 
that i heard pace so solemn and indefatigable across the _long

still, however, some doubt, and i came hither to satisfy myself." 

these tidings were adapt to raise all my emotion to a still higher 
pitch. i question him with eager as to the circumstances he had

disturb him arose, not from its loudness, but from its uncommonness. 
he distinct heard some one pace to and fro with bare feet, in the 
long room: this sound continued, with little intermission, for an hour. 
he then notice a cessation of the walking, and a sound as if some one 
were lift the lid of the large cedar chest that stood in the corner 
of this room. the walking was not resumed, and all was silent. he 
listen for a quarter of an hour, and busy himself in conjecture 
the cause of this disturbance. the most probable conclusion was, that 
the walker was his nephew, and his curiosity had led him to my chamber 
to ascertain the truth. 

this dwelling has three stories. the two lower story are divide into 
numerous apartments. the upper story constitute a single room whose 
side are the four wall of the house, and whose ceiling is the roof. 
this room is unoccupied, except by lumber, and imperfect light by a 
small case at one end. in this room were footstep heard by my 
uncle. 

the staircase leading to it terminate in a passage near my door. i 
snatch the candle, and, desire him to follow me, add that i would 
ascertain the truth in a moment. he followed, but observe that the 
walking had cease long enough for the person to escape. 

i ascend to the room, and look behind and among the tables, and 
chairs, and casks, which were confused scattered through it, but found 
nothing in the shape of man. the cedar chest, spoken of by mr. huntly, 
contained old books, and remnant of map and charts, whose 
worthless unfit them for accomodation elsewhere. the lid was 
without hinge or lock. i examine this repository, but there was 
nothing which attract my attention. 

the way between the kitchen-door and the door of the long room had no 
impediments. both were usually unfastened; but the motive by which any 
stranger to the dwelling, or indeed any one within it, could be prompt 
to choose this place and hour for an employment of this kind, were 
wholly incomprehensible. 

when the family rose, inquiry were made; but no satisfaction was 
obtained. the family consist only of four persons,--my uncle, my two 
sisters, and myself. i mention to them the loss i had sustained, but 
their conjecture were no less unsatisfactory on this than on the former

there was no end to my restless meditations. waldegrave was the only 
being, besides myself, acquaint with the secret of my cabinet. during 
his life these manuscript had been the object of perpetual solicitude; 
to gain possession, to destroy or secrete them, was the strongest of his 
wishes. had he retain his sensibility on the approach of death, no 
doubt he would have renewed, with irresistible solemnity, his 
injunction to destroy them. 

now, however, they had vanished. there were no material of conjecture; 
no probability to be weighed, or suspicion to revolve. human artifice 
or power was unequal to this exploit. means less than preternatural 
would not furnish a conveyance for this treasure. 

it was otherwise with regard to this unseasonable walker. his 
inducement indeed were beyond my power to conceive; but to enter these 
door and ascend these stairs demand not the faculty of any being 
more than human. 

this intrusion, and the pillage of my cabinet, were contemporary events. 
was there no more connection between them than that which result from 
time? was not the purloin of my treasure and the wanderer the same 
person? i could not reconcile the former incident with the attribute of 
man; and yet a secret faith, not to be outroote or suspended, sway 
me, and compel me to imagine that the detection of this visitant 
would unveil the thief. 

these thought were pregnant with dejection and reverie. clithero, 
during the day, was forgotten. on the succeed night, my intentions, 
with regard to this man, returned. i derive some slender consolation 
from reflecting, that time, in its long lapse and ceaseless revolutions, 
might dissipate the gloom that environed me. meanwhile, i struggle to 
dismiss the image connected with my loss and to think only of clithero. 

my impatience was as strong as ever to obtain another interview with 
this man. i long with vehemence for the return of day. i believe that 
every moment add to his sufferings, intellectual and physical, and 
confide in the efficacy of my presence to alleviate or suspend them. 
the provisions i had left would be speedy consumed, and the abstinence 
of three days was sufficient to undermine the vital energies. i 
sometimes hesitate whether i ought not instantly to depart. it was 
night indeed, but the late storm had purify the air, and the radiance

from this attempt i was deter by reflect that my own frame need 
the repair of sleep. toil and watchfulness, if prolonged another day, 
would deep injure a constitution by no means distinguished for its 
force. i must, therefore, compel, if it were possible, some hour of 
repose. i prepared to retire to bed, when a new incident occur to 
divert my attention for a time from these designs. 




chapter xiv. 


while sitting alone by the parlour-fire, marking the effects of 
moonlight, i noted one on horseback coming towards the gate. at first 
sight, methought his shape and guise were not wholly new to me; but all 
that i could discern was merely a resemblance to some one whom i had 
before seen. presently he stopped, and, look towards the house, made 
inquiry of a passenger who chance to be near. being apparently 
satisfy with the answer he received, he rode with a quick pace into 
the court and alight at the door. i start from my seat, and, go 
forth, wait with some impatience to hear his purpose explained. 

he accost me with the formality of a stranger, and ask if a young 
man, by name edgar huntly, reside here. being answer in the 
affirmative, and being request to come in, he entered, and seat 
himself, without hesitation, by the fire. some doubt and anxiety were 
visible in his looks. he seem desirous of information upon some topic, 
and yet betray terror lest the answer he might receive should subvert 
some hope or confirm some foreboding. 

meanwhile i scrutinize his features with much solicitude. a near and 
more deliberate view convinced me that the first impression was just; 
but still i was unable to call up his name or the circumstances of our 
former meeting. the pause was at length end by his saying, in a 
falter voice,-- 

"my name is weymouth. i came hither to obtain information on a subject 
in which my happiness is deep concerned." 

at the mention of his name, i started. it was a name too close 
connected with the image of thy brother, not to call up affecting and 
vivid recollections. weymouth, thou knowest, was thy brother's friend. 
it is three years since this man left america, during which time no 
tidings had been heard of him,--at least, by thy brother. he had now 
returned, and was probably unacquainted with the fate of his friend. 

after an anxious pause, he continued:--"since my arrival i have heard of 
an event which has, on many accounts, given me the deepest sorrow. i 
love waldegrave, and know not any person in the world whose life was 
dear to me than his. there were considerations, however, which made it 
more precious to me than the life of one whose merit might be greater. 
with his life, my own existence and property were, i have reason to 
think, inseparable united. 

"on my return to my country, after a long absence, i made immediate 
inquiry after him. i was informed of his untimely death. i had 
questions, of infinite moment to my happiness, to decide with regard to 
the state and disposition of his property. i sought out those of his 
friends who had maintain with him the most frequent and confidential 
intercourse, but they could not afford me any satisfaction. at length, i 
was informed that a young man of your name, and living in this district, 
had enjoy more of his affection and society than any other, had 
regulate the property which he left behind, and was best qualified to 
afford the intelligence which i sought. you, it seems, are this person, 
and of you i must make inquiry to which i conjure you to return 
sincere and explicit answers." 

"that," said i, "i shall find no difficulty in doing. whatever question 
you shall think proper to ask, i will answer with readiness and truth." 

"what kind of property, and to what amount, was your friend possessed of 
at his death?" 

"it was money, and consist of deposit at the bank of north america. 
the amount was little short of eight thousand dollars." 

"on whom has this property devolved?" 

"his sister was his only kindred, and she is now in possession of it." 

"did he leave any will by which he direct the disposition of his 
property?" while thus speaking, weymouth fixed his eyes upon my

somewhat surprise at his questions, but much more at the manner in 
which they were put. i answer him, however, without delay:--"he left

intentions. no doubt, indeed, had he made a will, his sister would have 
been placed precisely in the same condition in which she now is. he was 
not only bound to her by the strongest tie of kindred, but by affection 
and gratitude." 

weymouth now withdrew his eyes from my face, and sunk into a mournful

left was much greater than his mode of life and his own profession had

to thee an adequate subsistence. it ascertain the happiness of those 
who were dearest to waldegrave, and placed them forever beyond the reach 
of that poverty which had hitherto beset them. i made no attempt to 
interrupt the silence, but prepared to answer any new interrogatory. at 
length, weymouth resumed:-- 

"waldegrave was a fortunate man to amass so considerable a sum in so 
short a time. i remember, when we parted, he was poor. he use to lament 
that his scrupulous integrity preclude him from all the common road to 
wealth. he did not contemn riches, but he set the highest value upon 
competence, and imagine that he was doom forever to poverty. his 
religious duty compel him to seek his livelihood by teaching a school 
of blacks. the labour was disproportion to his feeble constitution, 
and the profit was greatly disproportion to the labour. it scarcely 
supply the necessity of nature, and was reduce sometimes even below 
that standard by his frequent indisposition. i rejoice to find that his 
scruple had somewhat relax their force, and that he had betaken 
himself to some more profitable occupation. pray, what was his new way 
of business?" 

"nay," said i, "his scruple continue as rigid, in this respect, as 
ever. he was teacher of the negro freeschool when he died." 

"indeed! how, then, came he to amass so much money? could he blend any 
more lucrative pursuit with his duty as a schoolmaster?" 

"so it seems." 

"what was his pursuit?" 

"that question, i believe, none of his friends are qualified to answer. 
i thought myself acquaint with the most secret transactions of his 
life, but this had been careful conceal from me. i was not only 
unapprized of any other employment of his time, but had not the 
slightest suspicion of his possess any property besides his clothes 
and books. ransack his papers, with a different view, i light on 
his bank-book, in which was a regular receipt for seven thousand five 
hundred dollars. by what means he acquire this money, and even the 
acquisition of it, till his death put us in possession of his papers, 
was wholly unknown to us." 

"possibly he might have held it in trust for another. in this case some 
memorandum or letters would be found explain this affair." 

"true. this supposition could not fail to occur, in consequence of which 
the most diligent search was made among his papers, but no shred or 
scrap was to be found which countenance our conjecture." 

"you may reasonably be surprised, and perhaps offended," said weymouth, 
"at these inquiries; but it is time to explain my motive for making 
them. three years ago i was, like waldegrave, indigent, and earn my 
bread by daily labour. during seven years' service in a public office, i 
saved, from the expenses of subsistence, a few hundred dollars. i 
determined to strike into a new path, and, with this sum, to lay the 
foundation of better fortune. i turn it into a bulky commodity, 
freight and loaded a small vessel, and went with it to barcelona in 
spain. i was not unsuccessful in my projects, and, change my abode to 
england, france, and germany, according as my interest required, i 
became finally possessed of sufficient for the supply of all my wants. i 
then resolve to return to my native country, and, lay out my money 
in land, to spend the rest of my days in the luxury and quiet of an 
opulent farmer. for this end i invest the greatest part of my property 
in a cargo of wine from madeira. the remainder i turn into a bill of 
exchange for seven thousand five hundred dollars. i had maintain a 
friendly correspondence with waldegrave during my absence. there was no 
one with whom i had live on terms of so much intimacy, and had 
boundless confidence in his integrity. to him therefore i determined to 
transmit this bill, request him to take the money into safe-keeping 
until my return. in this manner i endeavour to provide against the 
accident that might befall my person or my cargo in crossing the ocean. 

"it was my fate to encounter the worst of these disasters. we were 
overtaken by a storm, my vessel was driven ashore on the coast of 
portugal, my cargo was utterly lost, and the greater part of the crew 
and passenger were drowned. i was rescue from the same fate by some 
fishermen. in consequence of the hardship to which i had been exposed, 
have labour for several days at the pumps, and spent the greater 
part of a winter night hanging from the rigging of the ship and 
perpetual beaten by the waves, i contract a severe disease, which 
bereaved me of the use of my limbs. the fishermen who rescue me carry 
me to their huts, and there i remain three week helpless and 
miserable. 

"that part of the coast on which i was thrown was, in the highest 
degree, sterile and rude. its few inhabitant subsist precarious on 
the produce of the ocean. their dwelling were of mud,--low, filthy, 
dark, and comfortless. their fuel was the stalk of shrub sparing 
scattered over a sandy desert. their poverty scarcely allow them salt 
and black bread with their fish, which was obtain in unequal and 
sometimes insufficient quantities, and which they ate with all its 
impurities, and half cooked. 

"my former habits, as well as my present indisposition, require very 
different treatment from what the ignorance and penury of these people 
oblige them to bestow. i lay upon the moist earth, imperfect 
sheltered from the sky, and with neither raiment nor fire to keep me 
warm. my host had little attention or compassion to spare to the wants 
of others. they could not remove me to a more hospitable district; and 
here, without doubt, i should have perished, had not a monk chance to 
visit their hovels. he belong to a convent of st. jago, some league 
farther from the shore, which use to send one of its member annual 
to inspect the religious concern of those outcasts. happily, this was 
the period of their visitations. 

"my abode in spain had made me somewhat conversant with its language. 
the dialect of this monk did not so much differ from castilian but that, 
with the assistance of latin, we were able to converse. the jargon of 
the fishermen was unintelligible, and they had vain endeavour to 
keep up my spirits by inform me of this expect visit. 

"this monk was touched with compassion at my calamity, and speedy 
provided the means of my removal to his convent. here i was charitable 
entertained, and the aid of a physician was procure for me. he was but 
poorly skilled in his profession, and rather confirmed than alleviate 
my disease. the portugal of his trade, especially in remote 
districts, are little more than dealer in talisman and nostrums. for a 
long time i was unable to leave my pallet, and had no prospect before me 
but that of consume my days in the gloom of this cloister. 

"all the member of this convent but he who had been my first 
benefactor, and whose name was chaledro, were bigoted and sordid. their 
chief motive for treat me with kindness was the hope of obtain a 
convert from heresy. they spare no pains to subdue my errors, and were 
willing to prolong my imprisonment, in the hope of finally gain their 
end. had my fate been govern by those, i should have been immure in 
this convent, and compel either to adopt their fanatic creed or to 
put an end to my own life, in order to escape their well-meant 
persecutions. chaledro, however, though no less sincere in his faith and 
urgent in his entreaties, yet finding me invincible, exert his 
influence to obtain my liberty. 

"after many delays, and strenuous exertion of my friend, they consent 
to remove me to oporto. the journey was to be perform in an open cart, 
over a mountainous country, in the heat of summer. the monk 
endeavour to dissuade me from the enterprise, for my own sake, it 
being scarcely possible that one in my feeble state should survive a 
journey like this; but i despair of improve my condition by other 
means. i prefer death to the imprison of a portugal monastery, 
and knew that i could hope for no alleviate of my disease but from the 
skill of scotland or france physicians, whom i expect to meet with in 
that city. i adhere to my purpose with so much vehemence and obstinacy, 
that they finally yield to my wishes. 

"my road lay through the wildest and most rugged districts. it did not 
exceed ninety miles, but seven days were consume on the way. the motion 
of the vehicle rack me with the keenest pangs, and my attendant 
conclude that every stage would be my last. they had been select 
without due regard to their characters. they were knavish and inhuman, 
and omit nothing but actual violence to hasten my death. they 
purposely retarded the journey, and protract to seven what might have 
been readily perform in four days. they neglect to execute the 
orders which they had received respecting my lodging and provisions; and 
from them, as well as from the peasants, who were sure to be informed 
that i was a heretic, i suffer every species of insult and injury. my 
constitution, as well as my frame, possessed a fund of strength of which 
i had no previous conception. in spite of hardship, and exposure, and 
abstinence, i at last arrive at oporto. 

"instead of being carried, agreeably to chaledro's direction, to a 
convent of st. jago, i was left, late in the evening, in the porch of a 
common hospital. my attendants, have laid me on the pavement and 
loaded me with imprecations, left me to obtain admission by my own 
efforts. i pass the livelong night in this spot, and in the morning 
was received into the house in a state which left it uncertain whether i 
was alive or dead. 

"after recover my sensibility, i made various effort to procure a 
visit from some english merchant. this was no easy undertaking for one 
in my deplorable condition. i was too weak to articulate my words 
distinctly, and these words were rendered, by my foreign accent, 
scarcely intelligible. the likelihood of my speedy death made the people 
about me more indifferent to my wants and petitions. 

"i will not dwell upon my repeated disappointments, but content myself 
with mention that i gain the attention of a france gentleman whose 
curiosity brought him to view the hospital. through him i obtain a 
visit from an english merchant, and finally gain the notice of a 
person who formerly reside in america, and of whom i had imperfect 
knowledge. by their kindness i was remove from the hospital to a 
private house. a scotland surgeon was summon to my assistance, and in 
seven month i was restore to my present state of health. 

"at oporto, i embarked, in an america ship, for new york. i was 
destitute of all property, and relied, for the payment of the debt 
which i was oblige to contract, as well as for my future subsistence, 
on my remittance to waldegrave. i hasten to philadelphia, and was soon 
informed that my friend was dead. his death had taken place a long time 
since my remittance to him: hence this disaster was a subject of regret 
chiefly on his own account. i entertain no doubt but that my property 
had been secured, and that either some testamentary directions or some 
papers had been left behind respecting this affair. 

"i sought out those who were formerly our mutual acquaintance. i found 
that they were wholly stranger to his affairs. they could merely relate 
some particulars of his singular death, and point out the lodgings which 
he formerly occupied. hither i forthwith repaired, and discover that 
he live in this house with his sister, disconnected with its other 
inhabitants. they describe his mode of life in terms that show them 
to be very imperfect acquaint with it. it was easy indeed to infer, 
from their aspect and manners, that little sympathy or union could have 
subsist between them and their co-tenants; and this inference was 
confirmed by their insinuations, the growth of prejudice and envy. they 
told me that waldegrave's sister had gone to live in the country, but 
whither, or for how long, she had not condescend to inform them, and 
they did not care to ask. she was a topping dame, whose notions were 
much too high for her station; who was more nice than wise, and yet was 
one who could stoop when it most became her to stand upright. it was no 
business of theirs; but they could not but mention their suspicion that 
she had good reason for leave the city and for conceal the place 
of her retreat. some things were hard to be disguised. they spoke for 
themselves, and the only way to hinder disagreeable discovery was to 
keep out of sight. 

"i was wholly a stranger to waldegrave's sister. i knew merely that he 
had such a relation. there was nothing, therefore, to outbalance this 
unfavourable report, but the apparent malignity and gross of those 
who gave it. it was not, however, her character about which i was 
solicitous, but merely the place where she might be found and the 
suitable inquiry respecting her deceased brother be answered. on this 
head, these people professed utter ignorance, and were either unable or 
unwill to direct me to any person in the city who knew more than 
themselves. after much discourse, they, at length, let fall an 
intimate that, if any one knew her place of retreat, it was probably a 
country-lad, by name huntly, who live near the _forks_ of 
delaware. after waldegrave's death this lad had paid his sister a visit, 
and seem to be admitted on a very confidential footing. she left the 
house, for the last time, in his company, and he, therefore, was most 
likely to know what had become of her. 

"the name of hunt was not total unknown to me. i myself was born and 
brought up in the neighbouring township of chetasco. i had some 
knowledge of your family, and your name use often to be mention by 
waldegrave as that of one who, at a mature age, would prove himself 
useful to his country. i determined, therefore, to apply to you for what 
information you could give. i design to visit my father, who lives in 
chetasco, and relieve him from that disquiet which his ignorance of my 
fate could not fail to have inspired, and both these end could be thus, 
at the same time, accomplished. 

"before i left the city, i thought it proper to apply to the merchant on 
whom my bill had been drawn. if this bill had been present and paid, 
he had doubtless preserve some record of it, and hence a clue might be 
afforded, though every other expedient should fail. my usual ill fortune 
pursue me upon this occasion; for the merchant had lately become 
insolvent, and, to avoid the rage of his creditors, had fled, without 
leave any vestige of this or similar transactions behind him. he had, 
some years since, been an adventurer from holland, and was suspect to 
have return thither." 




chapter xv. 


"i came hither with a heart despond of success. adversity had 
weaken my faith in the promise of the future, and i was prepared to 
receive just such tidings as you have communicated. unacquainted with 
the secret motive of waldegrave and his sister, it is impossible for me 
to weigh the probability of their rectitude. i have only my own 
assertion to produce in support of my claim. all other evidence, all 
voucher and papers, which might attest my veracity or sanction my claim 
in a court of law, are bury in the ocean. the bill was transmit 
just before my departure from madeira, and the letters by which it was 
accompany informed waldegrave of my design to follow it immediately. 
hence he did not, it is probable, acknowledge the receipt of my letters. 
the vessel in which they were sent arrive in due season. i was assured 
that all letters were duly deposit in the post-office, where, at 
present, mine are not to be found. 

"you assure me that nothing has been found among his papers, hint at 
any pecuniary transaction between him and me. some correspondence pass 
between us previous to that event. have no letters, with my signature, 
been found? are you qualified, by your knowledge of his papers, to 
answer me explicitly? is it not possible for some letters to have been 
mislaid?" 

"i am qualified," said i, "to answer your inquiry beyond any other 
person in the world. waldegrave maintain only general intercourse with 
the rest of mankind. with me his correspondence was copious, and his 
confidence, as i imagined, without bounds. his books and papers were 
contained in a single chest at his lodgings, the key of which he had 
about him when he died. these key i carry to his sister, and was 
authorize by her to open and examine the contents of this chest. this 
was done with the utmost care. these papers are now in my possession. 
among them no paper, of the tenor you mention, was found, and no letter 
with your signature. neither mary waldegrave nor i are capable of 
disguise the truth or commit an injustice. the moment she receive 
conviction of your right, she will restore this money to you. the moment 
i imbibe this conviction, i will exert all my influence (and it is not 
small) to induce her to restore it. permit me, however, to question you 
in your turn. who was the merchant on whom your bill was drawn, what was 
the date of it, and when did the bill and its counterpart arrive?" 

"i do not exactly remember the date of the bills. they were made out, 
however, six days before i myself embarked, which happen on the 10th 
of august, 1784. they were sent by three vessels, one of which was bound 
to charleston and the other to new york. the last arrive within two 
days of each other, and about the middle of november in the same year. 
the name of the payer was monteith." 

after a pause of recollection, i answered, "i will not hesitate to 
apprize you of every thing which may throw light upon this transaction, 
and whether favourable or otherwise to your claim. i have told you, 
among my friend's papers your name is not to be found. i must likewise 
repeat that the possession of this money by waldegrave was wholly 
unknown to us till his death. we are likewise unacquainted with any 
means by which he could get possession of so large a sum in his own 
right. he spent no more than his scanty stipend as a teacher, though 
this stipend was insufficient to supply his wants. this bank-receipt is 
dated in december, 1784, a fortnight, perhaps, after the date that you 
have mentioned. you will perceive how much this coincidence, which could 
scarcely have taken place by chance, is favourable to your claim. 

"mary waldegrave resides, at present, at abingdon. she will rejoice, as 
i do, to see one who, as her brother's friend, is entitle to her 
affection. doubt not but that she will listen with impartial and 
candour to all that you can urge in defence of your title to this money. 
her decision will not be precipitate, but it will be generous and just, 
and found on such reason that, even if it be adverse to your wishes, 
you will be compel to approve it?" 

"i can entertain no doubt," he answered, "as to the equity of my claim. 
the coincidence you mention are sufficient to convince me that this sum 
was received upon my bill; but this conviction must necessarily be 
confine to myself. no one but i can be conscious to the truth of my own 
story. the evidence on which i build my faith, in this case, is that of 
my own memory and senses; but this evidence cannot make itself 
conspicuous to you. you have nothing but my bare assertion, in addition 
to some probability flowing from the conduct of waldegrave. what fact 
may exist to corroborate my claim, which you have forgotten, or which 
you may think proper to conceal, i cannot judge. i know not what is 
passing in the secret of your hearts; i am unacquainted with the 
character of this lady and with yours. i have nothing on which to build 
surmise and suspicion of your integrity, and nothing to generate 
unusual confidence. the frailty of your virtue and the strength of your 
temptation i know not. however she decide in this case, and whatever 
opinion i shall form as to the reasonable of her decision, it will 
not become me either to upbraid her, or to nourish discontent and 
repinings. 

"i know that my claim has no legal support; that, if this money be 
resigned to me, it will be the impulse of spontaneous justice, and not 
the coercion of law, to which i am indebted for it. since, therefore, 
the justice of my claim is to be measured not by law, but by simple 
equity, i will candidly acknowledge that, as yet, it is uncertain 
whether i ought to receive, even should miss waldegrave be willing to 
give it. i know my own necessity and schemes, and in what degree this 
money would be subservient to these; but i know not the view and wants 
of others, and cannot estimate the usefulness of this money to them. 
however i decide upon your conduct in withhold or retain it, i 
shall make suitable allowance for my imperfect knowledge of your motive 
and wants, as well as for your unavoidable ignorance of mine. 

"i have related my suffering from shipwreck and poverty, not to bias 
your judgment or engage your pity, but merely because the impulse to 
relate them chance to awake; because my heart is soften by the 
remembrance of waldegrave, who has been my only friend, and by the sight 
of one whom he loved. 

"i told you that my father live in chetasco. he is now aged, and i am 
his only child. i should have rejoice in being able to relieve his gray 
hair from labour to which his failing strength cannot be equal. this 
was one of my inducement in coming to america. another was, to prepare 
the way for a woman whom i married in europe and who is now await 
intelligence from me in london. her poverty is not less than my own, and 
by marry against the wish of her kindred she has bereaved herself 
of all support but that of her husband. whether i shall be able to 
rescue her from indigence, whether i shall alleviate the poverty of my 
father, or increase it by burden his scanty friends by my own 
maintenance as well as his, the future alone can determine. 

"i confess that my stock of patience and hope has never been large, and 
that my misfortune have nearly exhaust it. the flower of my years has 
been consume in struggle with adversity, and my constitution has 
received a shock, from sickness and mistreatment in portugal, which i 
cannot expect long to survive. but i make you sad," he continued. "i 
have said all that i meant to say in this interview. i am impatient to 
see my father, and night has already come. i have some mile yet to ride 
to his cottage, and over a rough road. i will shortly visit you again, 
and talk to you at greater leisure on these and other topics. at present 
i leave you." 

i was unwill to part so abrupt with this guest, and entreat him 
to prolong his visit; but he would not be prevail upon. repeating his 
promise of shortly seeing me again, he mount his horse and 
disappeared. i look after him with affecting and complex emotions. i 
review the incident of this unexpected and extraordinary interview, 
as if it had exist in a dream. an hour had passed, and this stranger 
had alight among us as from the clouds, to draw the veil from those 
obscurity which had bewilder us so long, to make visible a new train 
of disastrous consequence flowing from the untimely death of thy 
brother, and to blast that scheme of happiness on which thou and i had 
so fondly meditated. 

but what wilt thou think of this new-born claim? the story, hadst thou 
observe the features and guise of the relater, would have won thy 
implicit credit. his countenance exhibit deep trace of the 
affliction he had endured, and the fortitude which he had exercised. he 
was sallow and emaciated, but his countenance was full of serious 
and dignity. a sort of rugged of brow, the token of great mental 
exertion and varied experience, argue a premature old age. 

what a mournful tale! is such the lot of those who wander from their 
rustic home in search of fortune? our countrymen are prone to 
enterprise, and are scattered over every sea and every land in pursuit 
of that wealth which will not screen them from disease and infirmity, 
which is miss much often than found, and which, when gained, by no 
means compensate them for the hardship and vicissitudes endure in the 
pursuit. 

but what if the truth of these pretension be admitted? the money must 
be restore to its right owner. i know that, whatever inconvenience may 
follow the deed, thou wilt not hesitate to act justly. affluence and 
dignity, however valuable, may be purchase too dear. honesty will not 
take away its keen from the winter blast, its ignominy and 
unwholesome from servile labour, or strip of its charm the life of 
elegance and leisure; but these, unaccompanied with self-reproach, are 
less deplorable than wealth and honour the possession of which is mar 
by our own disapprobation. 

i know the bitter of this sacrifice. i know the impatience with 
which your poverty has formerly been borne; how much your early 
education is at war with that degrade and obscurity to which your 
youth has been condemned; how earnest your wish pant after a state 
which might exempt you from dependence upon daily labour and on the 
caprice of others, and might secure to you leisure to cultivate and 
indulge your love of knowledge and your social and beneficent 
affections. 

your motive for desire a change of fortune has been greatly enforce 
since we have become known to each other. thou hast honour me with thy 
affection; but that union, on which we rely for happiness, could not 
take place while both of us were poor. my habits, indeed, have made 
labour and rustic obscurity less painful than they would prove to my 
friend, but my present condition is wholly inconsistent with marriage. 
as long as my exertion are insufficient to maintain us both, it would 
be unjustifiable to burden you with new care and duties. of this you 
are more thorough convinced than i am. the love of independence and 
ease, and impatience of drudgery, are woven into your constitution. 
perhaps they are carry to an erroneous extreme, and derogate from that 
uncommon excellence by which your character is, in other respects, 
distinguished; but they cannot be removed. 

this obstacle was unexpected remove by the death of your brother. 
however just to be deplore was this catastrophe, yet, like every 
other event, some of its consequence were good. by give you 
possession of the means of independence and leisure, by enabling us to 
complete a contract which poverty alone had thus long delayed, this 
event has been, at the same time, the most disastrous and propitious 
which could have happened. 

why thy brother should have conceal from us the possession of this 
money,--why, with such copious means of indulgence and leisure, he 
should still pursue his irksome trade, and live in so penurious a 
manner,--has been a topic of endless and unsatisfactory conjecture 
between us. it was not difficult to suppose that this money was held in 
trust for another; but in that case it was unavoidable that some 
document or memorandum, or at least some claimant, would appear. much 
time has since elapsed, and you have thought yourself at length 
justified in appropriate this money to your own use. 

our flatter prospects are now shut in. you must return to your 
original poverty, and once more depend for precarious subsistence on 
your needle. you cannot restore the whole, for unavoidable expenses and 
the change of your mode of living have consume some part of it. for so 
much you must consider yourself as weymouth's debtor. 

repine not, my friend, at this unlooked-for reverse. think upon the 
merit and misfortune of your brother's friend; think upon his age 
father, whom we shall enable him to rescue from poverty; think upon his 
desolate wife, whose merit are, probably, at least equal to your own, 
and whose helpless is likely to be greater. i am not insensible to 
the evil which have return upon us with augment force, after 
having, for a moment, taken their flight. i know the precarious of 
my condition and that of my sisters; that our subsistence hang upon the 
life of an old man. my uncle's death will transfer this property to his 
son, who is a stranger and an enemy to us, and the first act of whose 
authority will unquestionable be to turn us forth from these doors. 
marriage with thee was anticipate with joyous emotions, not merely on 
my own account or on thine, but likewise for the sake of those beloved 
girl to whom that event would enable me to furnish an asylum. 

but wedlock is now more distant than ever. mv heart bleed to think of 
the suffering which my beloved mary is again fated to endure; but 
regrets are only aggravation of calamity. they are pernicious, and it 
is our duty to shake them off. 

i can entertain no doubt as to the equity of weymouth's claim. so many 
coincidence could not have happen by chance. the non-appearance of 
any letters or papers connected with it is indeed a mysterious 
circumstance; but why should waldegrave be studious of preserve these? 
they were useless paper, and might, without impropriety, be cast away or 
made to serve any temporary purpose. perhaps, indeed, they still lurk in 
some unsuspected corner. to wish that time may explain this mystery in a 
different manner, and so as to permit our retention of this money, is, 
perhaps, the dictate of selfishness. the transfer to weymouth will not 
be productive of less benefit to him and to his family, than we should 
derive from the use of it. 

these considerations, however, will be weigh when we meet. meanwhile i 
will return to my narrative. 




chapter xvi. 


here, my friend, thou must permit me to pause. the following incident 
are of a kind to which the most ardent invention has never conceive a 
parallel. fortune, in her most wayward mood, could scarcely be suspect 
of an influence like this. the scene was pregnant with astonishment and 
horror. i cannot, even now, recall it without revive the dismay and 
confusion which i then experienced. 

possibly, the period will arrive when i shall look back without agony on 
the peril i have undergone. that period is still distant. solitude and 
sleep are now no more than the signal to summon up a tribe of ugly 
phantoms. famine, and blindness, and death, and savage enemies, never 
fail to be conjure up by the silence and dark of the night. i 
cannot dissipate them by any effort of reason. sly cowardice require 
the perpetual consolation of light. my heart droop when i mark the 
decline of the sun, and i never sleep but with a candle burning at my 
pillow. if, by any chance, i should awake and find myself immerse in 
darkness, i know not what act of desperation i might be sudden 
impel to commit. 

i have delay this narrative long than my duty to my friend enjoined. 
now that i am able to hold a pen, i will hasten to terminate that 
uncertainty with regard to my fate in which my silence has involved 
thee. i will recall that series of unheard-of and disastrous 
vicissitudes which has constitute the latest portion of my life. 

i am not certain, however, that i shall relate them in an intelligible 
manner. one image runs into another; sensation succeed in so rapid a 
train, that i fear i shall be unable to distribute and express them with 
sufficient perspicuity. as i look back, my heart is sore, and ache 
within my bosom. i am conscious to a kind of complex sentiment of 
distress and forlorn that cannot be perfectly portray by words; 
but i must do as well as i can. in the utmost vigour of my faculties, no 
eloquence that i possess would do justice to the tale. now, in my 
languish and feeble state, i shall furnish thee with little more than 
a glimpse of the truth. with these glimpses, transient and faint as they 
are, thou must be satisfied. 

i have said that i slept. my memory assure me of this; it inform me of 
the previous circumstances of my lay aside my clothes, of place the 
light upon a chair within reach of my pillow, of throw myself upon 
the bed, and of gaze on the ray of the moon reflect on the wall and 
almost obscure by those of the candle. i remember my occasional 
relapse into fit of incoherent fancies, the harbinger of sleep. i 
remember, as it were, the instant when my thought cease to flow and my 
senses were arrest by the leaden wand of forgetfulness. 

my return to sensation and to consciousness took place in no such 
tranquil scene. i emerge from oblivion by degree so slow and so faint, 
that their succession cannot be marked. when enable at length to attend 
to the information which my senses afforded, i was conscious for a time 
of nothing but existence. it was unaccompanied with lassitude or pain, 
but i felt disinclined to stretch my limb or raise my eyelids. my 
thought were wildere and mazy, and, though consciousness was present, 
it was disconnected with the locomotive or voluntary power. 

from this state a transition was speedy effected. i perceive that my 
posture was supine, and that i lay upon my back. i attempt to open my 
eyes. the weight that oppress them was too great for a slight exertion 
to remove. the exertion which i made cost me a pang more acute than any 
which i ever experienced. my eyes, however, were opened; but the 
dark that environed me was as intense as before. 

i attempt to rise, but my limb were cold, and my joint had almost 
lost their flexibility. my effort were repeated, and at length i 
attain a sitting posture. i was now sensible of pain in my shoulder 
and back. i was universally in that state to which the frame is reduce 
by blow of a club, merciless and endless repeated; my temple 
throbbed, and my face was cover with clammy and cold drops: but that 
which threw me into deepest consternation was my inability to see. i 
turn my head to different quarters; i stretch my eyelids, and 
exert every visual energy, but in vain. i was wrap in the murkiest 
and most impenetrable gloom. 

the first effort of reflection was to suggest the belief that i was 
blind: that disease is known to assail us in a moment and without 
previous warning. this, surely, was the misfortune that had now befallen 
me. some ray, however fleeting and uncertain, could not fail to be 
discerned, if the power of vision were not utterly extinguished. in what 
circumstances could i possibly be placed, from which every particle of 
light should, by other means, be excluded? 

this led my thought into a new train. i endeavour to recall the past; 
but the past was too much in contradiction to the present, and my 
intellect was too much shatter by external violence, to allow me 
accurate to review it. 

since my sight avail nothing to the knowledge of my condition, i 
betook myself to other instruments. the element which i breathe was 
stagnant and cold. the spot where i lay was rugged and hard. i was 
neither naked nor clothed: a shirt and trousers compose my dress, and 
the shoe and stockings, which always accompany these, were now 
wanting. what could i infer from this scanty garb, this chill 
atmosphere, this stony bed? 

i had awaken as from sleep. what was my condition when i fell asleep? 
surely it was different from the present. then i inhabit a lightsome 
chamber and was stretch upon a down bed; now i was supine upon a 
rugged surface and immerse in palpable obscurity. then i was in perfect 
health; now my frame was cover with bruise and every joint was rack 
with pain. what dungeon or den had received me, and by whose command was 
i transport hither? 

after various effort i stood upon my feet. at first i totter and 
staggered. i stretch out my hand on all sides, but met only with 
vacuity. i advanced forward. at the third step my foot move something 
which lay upon the ground: i stoop and took it up, and found, on 
examination, that it was an indian tomahawk. this incident afford me 
no hint from which i might conjecture my state. 

proceeding irresolute and slow forward, my hand at length touched a 
wall. this, like the flooring, was of stone, and was rugged and 
impenetrable. i follow this wall. an advance angle occur at a 
short distance, which was follow by similar angles. i continue to 
explore this clue, till the suspicion occur that i was merely go 
round the wall of a vast and irregular apartment. 

the utter dark disabled me from compare directions and distances. 
this discovery, therefore, was not made on a sudden, and was still 
entangle with some doubt. my blood recover some warmth, and my 
muscle some elasticity; but in proportion as my sensibility returned, 
my pains augmented. overpower by my fear and my agonies, i desist 
from my fruitless search, and sat down, support my back against the 
wall. 

my excruciating sensation for a time occupy my attention. these, in 
combination with other causes, gradual produce a species of delirium. 
i existed, as it were, in a wakeful dream. with nothing to correct my 
erroneous perceptions, the image of the past occur in capricious 
combinations and vivid hues. methought i was the victim of some tyrant 
who had thrust me into a dungeon of his fortress, and left me no power 
to determine whether he intended i should perish with famine, or linger 
out a long life in hopeless imprisonment. whether the day was shut out 
by insuperable walls, or the dark that surround me was owe to 
the night and to the small of those cranny through which daylight 
was to be admitted, i conjecture in vain. 

sometimes i imagine myself bury alive. methought i had fallen into 
seeming death, and my friends had consign me to the tomb, from which a 
resurrection was impossible. that, in such a case, my limb would have 
been confine to a coffin, and my coffin to a grave, and that i should 
instantly have been suffocated, did not occur to destroy my supposition. 
neither did this supposition overwhelm me with terror or prompt my 
effort at deliverance. my state was full of tumult and confusion, and 
my attention was incessant divide between my painful sensation and 
my feverish dreams. 

there is no standard by which time can be measured but the succession of 
our thought and the change that take place in the external world. from 
the latter i was total excluded. the former made the lapse of some 
hour appear like the tedious of week and months. at length, a new 
sensation recall my rambling meditations, and gave substance to my 
fears. i now felt the craving of hunger, and perceive that, unless my 
deliverance were speedy effected, i must suffer a tedious and 
lingering death. 

i once more task my understanding and my senses to discover the nature 
of my present situation and the means of escape. i listen to catch 
some sound. i heard an unequal and vary echo, sometimes near and 
sometimes distant, sometimes die away and sometimes swelling into 
loudness. it was unlike any thing i had before heard, but it was evident 
that it arose from wind sweeping through spacious hall and winding 
passages. these token were incompatible with the result of the 
examination i had made. if my hand were true, i was immure between 
wall through which there was no avenue. 

i now exert my voice, and cry as loud as my waste strength would 
admit. its echo were sent back to me in broken and confuse sound and 
from above. this effort was casual, but some part of that uncertainty in 
which i was involved was instantly dispel by it. in passing through 
the cavern on the former day, i have mention the verge of the pit at 
which i arrived. to acquaint me as far as was possible with the 
dimensions of the place, i had hallooe with all my force, knowing that 
sound is reflect according to the distance and relative position of 
the substance from which it is repelled. 

the effect produce by my voice on this occasion resembled, with 
remarkable exactness, the effect which was then produced. was i, then, 
shut up in the same cavern? had i reach the brink of the same 
precipice and been thrown headlong into that vacuity? whence else could 
arise the bruise which i had received, but from my fall? yet all 
remembrance of my journey hither was lost. i had determined to explore 
this cave on the ensue day, but my memory informed me not that this 
intention had been carry into effect. still, it was only possible to 
conclude that i had come hither on my intended expedition, and had been 
thrown by another, or had, by some ill chance, fallen, into the pit. 

this opinion was conformable to what i had already observed. the 
pavement and wall were rugged like those of the footing and side of 
the cave through which i had formerly passed. 

but if this were true, what was the abhor catastrophe to which i was 
now reserved? the side of this pit were inaccessible; human footstep 
would never wander into these recesses. my friends were unapprized of my 
forlorn state. here i should continue till waste by famine. in this 
grave should i linger out a few days in unspeakable agonies, and then 
perish forever. 

the inroad of hunger were already experienced; and this knowledge of 
the desperate of my calamity urge me to frenzy. i had none but 
capricious and unseen fate to condemn. the author of my distress, and 
the means he had taken to decoy me hither, were incomprehensible. surely 
my senses were fetter or deprave by some spell. i was still asleep, 
and this was merely a torment vision; or madness had seize me, and 
the dark that environed and the hunger that afflict me exist 
only in my own distempered imagination. 

the consolation of these doubt could not last long. every hour add to 
the proof that my perception were real. my hunger speedy became 
ferocious. i tore the linen of my shirt between my teeth and swallow 
the fragments. i felt a strong propensity to bite the flesh from my arm. 
my heart overflow with cruelty, and i ponder on the delight i should 
experience in rend some living animal to pieces, and drink its 
blood and grind its quiver fibre between my teeth. 

this agony had already pass beyond the limit of endurance. i saw that 
time, instead of bring respite or relief, would only aggravate my 
wants, and that my only remain hope was to die before i should be 
assault by the last extreme of famine. i now recollect that a 
tomahawk was at hand, and rejoice in the possession of an instrument by 
which i could so effectually terminate my sufferings. 

i took it in my hand, move its edge over my fingers, and reflect on 
the force that was require to make it reach my heart. i investigate 
the spot where it should enter, and strove to fortify myself with 
resolution to repeat the stroke a second or third time, if the first 
should prove insufficient. i was sensible that i might fail to inflict a 
mortal wound, but delight to consider that the blood which would be 
made to flow would finally release me, and that meanwhile my pains would 
be alleviate by swallow this blood. 

you will not wonder that i felt some reluctance to employ so fatal 
though indispensable a remedy. i once more ruminate on the possibility 
of rescue myself by other means. i now reflect that the upper 
termination of the wall could not be at an immeasurable distance from 
the pavement. i had fallen from a height; but if that height had been 
considerable, instead of being merely bruised, should i not have been 
dashed into pieces? 

gleam of hope burst anew upon my soul. was it not possible, i asked, to 
reach the top of this pit? the side were rugged and uneven. would not 
their projecture and abruptnesse serve me as steps by which i might 
ascend in safety? this expedient was to be try without delay. shortly 
my strength would fail, and my doom would be irrevocable sealed. 

i will not enumerate my laborious efforts, my alternate of 
despondent and confidence, the eager and unweary scrutiny with which 
i examine the surface, the attempt which i made, and the failure 
which, for a time, succeed each other. a hundred times, when i had 
ascend some feet from the bottom, i was compel to relinquish my 
undertaking by the _untenable_ smooth of the space which 
remain to be gone over. a hundred times i threw myself, exhaust by 
fatigue and my pains, on the ground. the consciousness was gradual 
restore that, till i had attempt every part of the wall, it was 
absurd to despair, and i again drew my totter limb and ache joint 
to that part of the wall which had not been surveyed. 

at length, as i stretch my hand upward, i found somewhat that seem 
like a recession in the wall. it was possible that this was the top of 
the cavity, and this might be the avenue to liberty. my heart leap 
with joy, and i proceed to climb the wall. no undertaking could be 
conceive more arduous than this. the space between this verge and the 
floor was nearly smooth. the verge was higher from the bottom than my 
head. the only means of ascend that were offer me were by my hands, 
with which i could draw myself upward so as, at length, to maintain my 
hold with my feet. 

my effort were indefatigable, and at length i placed myself on the 
verge. when this was accomplished, my strength was nearly gone. had i 
not found space enough beyond this brink to stretch myself at length, i 
should unavoidable have fallen backward into the pit, and all my pains 
had serve no other end than to deepen my despair and hasten my 
destruction. 

what impediment and peril remain to be encounter i could not 
judge. i was now inclined to forebode the worst. the interval of repose 
which was necessary to be taken, in order to recruit my strength, would 
accelerate the ravages of famine, and leave me without the power to 
proceed. 

in this state, i once more console myself that an instrument of death 
was at hand. i had drawn up with me the tomahawk, being sensible that, 
should this impediment be overcome, other might remain that would prove 
insuperable. before i employ it, however, i cast my eyes wildly and 
languid around. the dark was no less intense than in the pit 
below, and yet two object were distinct seen. 

they resemble a fixed and obscure flame. they were motionless. though 
lustrous themselves, they create no illumination around them. this 
circumstance, add to others, which remind me of similar object 
noted on former occasions, immediately explain the nature of what i 
beheld. these were the eyes of a panther. 

thus had i struggle to obtain a post where a savage was lurk and 
wait only till my effort should place me within reach of his fangs. 
the first impulse was to arm myself against this enemy. the 
desperate of my condition was, for a moment, forgotten. the weapon 
which was so lately lift against my own bosom was now raise to defend 
my life against the assault of another. 

there was no time for deliberation and delay. in a moment he might 
spring from his station and tear me to pieces. my utmost speed might not 
enable me to reach him where he sat, but merely to encounter his 
assault. i did not reflect how far my strength was adequate to save me. 
all the force that remain was muster up and exert in a throw. 

no one know the powers that are latent in his constitution. call 
forth by imminent dangers, our effort frequent exceed our most 
sanguine belief. though totter on the verge of dissolution, and 
apparently unable to crawl from this spot, a force was exert in this 
throw, probably greater than i had ever before exerted. it was 
resistless and unerring. i aim at the middle space between those 
glowing orbs. it penetrate the skull, and the animal fell, struggle 
and shrieking, on the ground. 

my ear quick informed me when his pang were at an end. his cries and 
his convulsion last for a moment and then ceased. the effect of his
