voice, in these subterranean abodes, was unspeakable rueful. 

the abrupt of this incident, and the preternatural exertion of my 
strength, left me in a state of languor and sinking, from which slow 
and with difficulty i recovered. the first suggestion that occur was 
to feed upon the carcass of this animal. my hunger had arrive at that 
pitch where all fastidious and scruple are at an end. i crept to 
the spot. i will not shock you by relate the extreme to which dire 
necessity had driven me. i review this scene with loathing and horror. 
now that it is past i look back upon it as on some hideous dream. the 
whole appear to be some freak of insanity. no alternative was offered, 
and hunger was capable of being appease even by a banquet so 
detestable. 

if this appetite has sometimes subdued the sentiment of nature, and 
compel the mother to feed upon the flesh of her offspring, it will 
not excite amaze that i did not turn from the yet warm blood and 
reek fibre of a brute. 

one evil was now removed, only to give place to another. the first 
sensation of full had scarcely been felt when my stomach was seize 
by pangs, whose acute exceed all that i ever before experienced. i 
bitter lament my inordinate avidity. the excruciation of famine 
were better than the agony which this abhor meal had produced. 

death was now impending with no less proximity and certainty, though in 
a different form. death was a sweet relief for my present miseries, and 
i vehement long for its arrival. i stretch myself on the ground. i 
threw myself into every posture that promise some alleviate of this 
evil. i roll along the pavement of the cavern, wholly inattentive to 
the danger that environed me. that i did not fall into the pit whence i 
had just emerge must be ascribe to some miraculous chance. 

how long my misery endured, it is not possible to tell. i cannot even 
form a plausible conjecture. judge by the lingering train of my 
sensations, i should conjecture that some days elapse in this 
deplorable condition; but nature could riot have so long sustain a 
conflict like this. 

gradual my pains subsided, and i fell into a deep sleep. i was visit 
by dream of a thousand hues. they led me to flowing stream and 
plenteous banquets, which, though placed within my view, some power 
forbade me to approach. from this sleep i recover to the fruition of 
solitude and darkness, but my frame was in a state less feeble than 
before that which i had eaten had produce temporary distress, but on 
the whole had been of use. if this food had not been provided for me i 
should scarcely have avoid death. i had reason, therefore, to 
congratulate myself on the danger that had lately occurred. 

i had act without foresight, and yet no wisdom could have prescribed 
more salutary measures. the panther was slain, not from a view to the 
relief of my hunger, but from the self-preserving and involuntary 
impulse. had i foreknown the pang to which my ravenous and bloody meal 
would give birth, i should have careful abstained; and yet these pang 
were a useful effort of nature to subdue and convert to nourishment the 
matter i had swallowed. 

i was now assail by the torment of thirst. my invention and my 
courage were anew bent to obviate this pressing evil. i reflect that 
there was some recess from this cavern, even from the spot where i now 
stood. before, i was doubtful whether in this direction from this pit 
any avenue could be found; but, since the panther had come hither, there 
was reason to suppose the existence of some such avenue. 

i now likewise attend to a sound, which, from its invariable tenor, 
denote somewhat different from the whistle of a gale. it seem like 
the murmur of a running stream. i now prepared to go forward and 
endeavour to move along in that direction in which this sound apparently 
came. 

on either side, and above my head, there was nothing but vacuity. my 
steps were to be guide by the pavement, which, though unequal and 
rugged, appeared, on the whole, to ascend. my safety require that i 
should employ both hand and feet in explore my way. 

i went on thus for a considerable period. the murmur, instead of 
becoming more distinct, gradual die away. my progress was arrest by 
fatigue, and i began once more to despond. my exertion produce a 
perspiration, which, while it augment my thirst, happily supply me 
with imperfect means of appease it. 

this expedient would, perhaps, have been accidental suggested; but my 
ingenuity was assist by remember the history of certain english 
prisoner in bengal, whom their merciless enemy imprison in a small 
room, and some of whom preserve themselves alive merely by swallow 
the moisture that flow from their bodies. this experiment i now 
perform with no less success. 

this was slender arid transitory consolation. i knew that, wandering at 
random, i might never reach the outlet of this cavern, or might be 
disabled, by hunger and fatigue, from go farther than the outlet. the 
craving which had lately been satiate would speedy return, and my 
negligence had cut me off from the resource which had recently been 
furnished. i thought not till now that a second meal might be 
indispensable. 

to return upon my footstep to the spot where the dead animal lay was a 
heartless project. i might thus be place myself at a hopeless distance 
from liberty. besides, my track could not be retraced. i had frequent 
deviate from a straight direction for the sake of avoid impediments. 
all of which i was sensible was, that i was travel up an irregular 
acclivity. i hope some time to reach the summit, but had no reason for 
adhere to one line of ascent in preference to another. 

to remain where i was was manifest absurd. whether i mount or 
descended, a change of place was most likely to benefit me. i resolve 
to vary my direction, and, instead of ascending, keep along the side of 
what i account a hill. i had gone some hundred feet when the murmur, 
before described, once more salute my ear. 

this sound, being imagine to proceed from a running stream, could not 
but light up joy in the heart of one nearly perishing with thirst. i 
proceed with new courage. the sound approach no nearer, nor became 
more distinct; but, as long as it die not away, i was satisfy to 
listen and to hope. 

i was eager observant if any the least glimmer of light should 
visit this recess. at length, on the right hand, a gleam, infinite 
faint, caught my attention. it was waver and unequal. i direct my 
steps towards it. it became more vivid and permanent. it was of that 
kind, however, which proceed from a fire, kindle with dry sticks, and 
not from the sun. i now heard the crackling of flames. 

this sound made me pause, or, at least, to proceed with circumspection. 
at length the scene opened, and i found myself at the entrance of a 
cave. i quick reach a station, when i saw a fire burning. at first 
no other object was noted, but it was easy to infer that the fire was 
kindle by men, and that they who kindle it could be at no great 
distance. 




chapter xvii. 


thus was i deliver from my prison, and restore to the enjoyment of 
the air and the light. perhaps the chance was almost miraculous that led 
me to this opening. in any other direction, i might have involved myself 
in an inextricable maze and render my destruction sure; but what now 
remain to place me in absolute security? beyond the fire i could see 
nothing; but, since the smoke roll rapid away, it was plain that on 
the opposite side the cavern was open to the air. 

i went forward, but my eyes were fixed upon the fire: presently, in 
consequence of change my station, i perceive several feet, and the 
skirt of blankets. i was somewhat startle at these appearances. the 
leg were naked, and score into uncouth figures. the _moccasins_ 
which lay beside them, and which were adorn in a grotesque manner, in 
addition to other incidents, immediately suggest the suspicion that 
they were indians. no spectacle was more adapt than this to excite 
wonder and alarm. had some mysterious power snatch me from the earth, 
and cast me, in a moment, into the heart of the wilderness? was i still 
in the vicinity of my parental habitation, or was i thousand of mile 
distant? 

were these the permanent inhabitant of this region, or were they 
wanderer and robbers? while in the heart of the mountain, i had 
entertain a vague belief that i was still within the precincts of 
norwalk. this opinion was shaken for a moment by the object which i now 
beheld, but it insensible returned: yet how was this opinion to be 
reconcile to appearances so strange and uncouth, and what measure did a 
due regard to my safety enjoin me to take? 

i now gain a view of four brawny and terrific figures, stretch upon 
the ground. they lay parallel to each other, on their left sides; in 
consequence of which their face were turn from me. between each was 
an interval where lay a musket. their right hand seem placed upon the 
stocks of their guns, as if to seize them on the first moment of alarm. 

the aperture through which these object were seen was at the back of 
the cave, and some feet from the ground. it was merely large enough to 
suffer a human body to pass. it was involved in profound darkness, and 
there was no danger of being suspect or discover as long as i 
maintain silence and kept out of view. 

it was easily imagine that these guest would make but a short sojourn 
in this spot. there was reason to suppose that it was now night, and 
that, after a short repose, they would start up and resume their 
journey. it was my first design to remain shroud in this covert till 
their departure, and i prepared to endure imprison and thirst 
somewhat longer. 

meanwhile my thought were busy in account for this spectacle. i need 
not tell thee that norwalk is the termination of a sterile and narrow 
tract which begin in the indian country. it form a sort of rugged and 
rocky vein, and continue upwards of fifty miles. it is cross in a few 
place by narrow and intricate paths, by which a communication is 
maintain between the farm and settlement on the opposite side of 
the ridge. 

during former indian wars, this rude surface was sometimes traverse by 
the red men, and they made, by means of it, frequent and destructive 
inroad into the heart of the english settlements. during the last war, 
notwithstanding the progress of population, and the multiply peril of 
such an expedition, a band of them had once penetrate into norwalk, and 
linger long enough to pillage and murder some of the neighbouring 
inhabitants. 

i have reason to remember that event. my father's house was placed on 
the verge of this solitude. eight of these assassin assail it at the 
dead of night. my parent and an infant child were murder in their 
beds; the house was pillaged, and then burnt to the ground. happily, 
myself and my two sister were abroad upon a visit. the preceding day 
had been fixed for our return to our father's house; but a storm 
occurred, which made it dangerous to cross the river, and, by obliging 
us to defer our journey, rescue us from captivity or death. 

most men are haunt by some species of terror or antipathy, which they 
are, for the most part, able to trace to some incident which befell them 
in their early years. you will not be surprise that the fate of my 
parents, and the sight of the body of one of this savage band, who, in 
the pursuit that was made after them, was overtaken and killed, should 
produce lasting and terrific image in my fancy. i never look upon or 
call up the image of a savage without shuddering. 

i knew that, at this time, some hostilities had been committed on the 
frontier; that a long course of injury and encroachment had lately 
exasperate the indian tribes; that an implacable and exterminate war 
was generally expected. we imagine ourselves at an inaccessible 
distance from the danger; but i could not but remember that this 
persuasion was formerly as strong as at present, and that an expedition 
which had once succeed might possibly be attempt again. here was 
every token of enmity and bloodshed. each prostrate figure was furnish 
with a rifle musket, and a leathern bag tie round his waist, which 
was, probably, store with powder and ball. 

from these reflections, the sense of my own danger was revive and 
enforced; but i likewise ruminate on the evil which might impend over 
others. i should, no doubt, be safe by remain in this nook; but might 
not some means be pursue to warn other of their danger? should they 
leave this spot without notice of their approach being given to the 
fearless and pacific tenant of the neighbouring district, they might 
commit, in a few hours, the most horrid and irreparable devastation. 

the alarm could only be diffuse in one way. could i not escape, 
unperceived, and without alarm the sleepers, from this cavern? the 
slumber of an indian is broken by the slightest noise; but, if all noise 
be precluded, it is commonly profound. it was possible, i conceived, to 
leave my present post, to descend into the cave, and issue forth without 
the smallest signal. their supine posture assured me that they were 
asleep. sleep usually come at their bidding, and if, perchance, they 
should be wakeful at an unseasonable moment, they always sit upon their 
haunches, and, leaning their elbow on their knees, consume the tedious 
hour in smoking. my peril would be great. accident which i could not 
foresee, and over which i had no command, might occur to awaken some one 
at the moment i was passing the fire. should i pass in safety, i might 
issue forth into a wilderness, of which i had no knowledge, where i 
might wander till i perish with famine, or where my footstep might be 
noted and pursue and overtaken by these implacable foes. these peril 
were enormous and imminent; but i likewise considered that i might be at 
no great distance from the habitation of men, and that my escape might 
rescue them from the most dreadful calamities. i determined to make this 
dangerous experiment without delay. 

i came near to the aperture, and had, consequently, a large view of 
this recess. to my unspeakable dismay, i now caught a glimpse of one 
seat at the fire. his back was turn towards me, so that i could 
distinct survey his gigantic form and fantastic ornaments. 

my project was frustrated. this one was probably commission to watch 
and to awaken his companion when a due portion of sleep had been taken. 
that he would not be unfaithful or remiss in the performance of the part 
assign to him was easily predicted. to pass him without exciting his 
notice (and the entrance could not otherwise be reached) was impossible. 
once more i shrunk back, and revolve with hopeless and anguish the 
necessity to which i was reduced. 

this interval of dreary foreboding did not last long. some motion in him 
that was seat by the fire attract my notice. i looked, and beheld 
him rise from his place and go forth from the cavern. this unexpected 
incident led my thought into a new channel. could not some advantage be 
taken of his absence? could not this opportunity be seize for making my 
escape? he had left his gun and hatchet on the ground. it was likely, 
therefore, that he had not gone far, and would speedy return. might 
not these weapon be seized, and some provision be thus made against the 
danger of meeting him without, or of being pursued? 

before a resolution could be formed, a new sound salute my ear. it was 
a deep groan, succeed by sob that seem struggle for utterance but 
were vehement counteract by the sufferer. this low and bitter 
lamentation apparently proceed from some one within the cave. it could 
not be from one of this swarthy band. it must, then, proceed from a 
captive, whom they had reserved for torment or servitude, and who had 
seize the opportunity afford by the absence of him that watch to 
give vent to his despair. 

i again thrust my head forward, and beheld, lie on the ground, apart 
from the rest, and bound hand and foot, a young girl. her dress was the 
coarse russet garb of the country, and bespoke her to be some farmer's 
daughter. her features denote the last degree of fear and anguish, and 
she move her limb in such a manner as show that the ligature by 
which she was confine produced, by their tightness, the utmost degree 
of pain. 

my wish were now bent not only to preserve myself and to frustrate the 
future attempt of these savages, but likewise to relieve this miserable 
victim. this could only be done by escape from the cavern and 
return with seasonable aid. the sob of the girl were likely to rouse 
the sleepers. my appearance before her would prompt her to testify her 
surprise by some exclamation or shriek. what could hence be predict 
but that the band would start on their feet and level their unerring 
pieces at my head? 

i know not why i was insensible to these dangers. my thirst was render 
by these delay intolerable. it took from me, in some degree, the power 
of deliberation. the murmur which had drawn me hither continue still 
to be heard. some torrent or cascade could not be far distant from the 
entrance of the cavern, and it seem as if one draught of clear water 
was a luxury cheap purchase by death itself. this, in addition to 
consideration more disinterested, and which i have already mentioned, 
impel me forward. 

the girl's cheek rest on the hard rock, and her eyes were dim with 
tears. as they were turn towards me, however, i hope that my 
movements would be notice by her gradual and without abruptness. this 
expectation was fulfilled. i had not advanced many steps before she 
discover me. this moment was critical beyond all other in the course 
of my existence. my life was suspended, as it were, by a spider's 
thread. all rest on the effect which this discovery should make upon 
this feeble victim. 

i was watchful of the first movement of her eye which should indicate a 
consciousness of my presence. i laboured, by gesture and looks, to 
deter her from betray her emotion. my attention was, at the same 
time, fixed upon the sleepers, and an anxious glance was cast towards 
the quarter whence the watchful savage might appear. 

i stoop and seize the musket and hatchet. the space beyond the fire 
was, as i expected, open to the air. i issue forth with tremble 
steps. the sensation inspired by the danger which environed me, add 
to my recent horrors, and the influence of the moon, which had now 
gain the zenith, and whose lustre dazzle my long-benighted senses, 
cannot be adequate described. 

for a minute, i was unable to distinguish objects. this confusion was 
speedy corrected, and i found myself on the verge of a steep. craggy 
eminence arose on all sides. on the left hand was a space that offer 
some footing, and hither i turned. a torrent was below me, and this path 
appear to lead to it. it quick appear in sight, and all foreign 
care were, for a time, suspended. 

this water fell from the upper regions of the hill, upon a flat 
projecture which was continue on either side, and on part of which i 
was now standing. the path was bound on the left by an inaccessible 
wall, and on the right terminated, at the distance of two or three feet 
from the wall, in a precipice. the water was eight or ten pace distant, 
and no impediment seem likely to rise between us. i rush forward 
with speed. 

my progress was quick checked. close to the fall water, seat on 
the edge, his back support by the rock, and his leg hanging over the 
precipice, i now beheld the savage who left the cave before me. the 
noise of the cascade and the improbability of interruption, at least 
from this quarter, had made him inattentive to my motions. 

i paused. along this verge lay the only road by which i could reach the 
water, and by which i could escape. the passage was completely occupy 
by this antagonist. to advance towards him, or to remain where i was, 
would produce the same effect. i should, in either case, be detected. he 
was unarmed; but his outcry would instantly summon his companion to 
his aid. i could not hope to overpower him, and pass him in defiance of 
his opposition. but, if this were effected, pursuit would be instantly 
commenced. i was unacquainted with the way. the way was unquestionable 
difficult. my strength was nearly annihilated; i should be overtaken in 
a moment, or their deficiency in speed would be supply by the accuracy 
of their aim. their bullets, at least, would reach me. 

there was one method of remove this impediment. the piece which i held 
in my hand was cocked. there could be no doubt that it was loaded. a 
precaution of this kind would never be omit by a warrior of this hue. 
at a greater distance than this, i should not fear to reach the mark. 
should i not discharge it, and, at the same moment, rush forward to 
secure the road which my adversary's death would open to me? 

perhaps you will conceive a purpose like this to have argue a 
sanguinary and murderous disposition. let it be remembered, however, 
that i entertain no doubt about the hostile designs of these men. 
this was sufficient indicate by their arms, their guise, and the 
captive who attend them. let the fate of my parent be, likewise, 
remembered. i was not certain but that these very men were the assassin 
of my family, and were those who had reduce me and my sister to the 
condition of orphan and dependants. no words can describe the torment 
of my thirst. relief to these torments, and safety to my life, were 
within view. how could i hesitate? 

yet i did hesitate. my aversion to bloodshed was not to be subdued but 
by the direst necessity. i knew, indeed, that the discharge of a musket 
would only alarm the enemy who remain behind; but i had another and 
a better weapon in my grasp. i could rive the head of my adversary, and 
cast him headlong, without any noise which should be heard, into the 
cavern. 

still i was willing to withdraw, to re-enter the cave, and take shelter 
in the darksome recess from which i had emerged. here i might remain, 
unsuspected, till these detest guest should depart. the hazard 
attend my re-entrance were to be bold encountered, and the torment 
of unsatisfy thirst were to be patient endured, rather than imbrue 
my hand in the blood of my fellowmen. but this expedient would be 
ineffectual if my retreat should be observe by this savage. of that i 
was bound to be incontestable assured. i retreated, therefore, but kept 
my eye fixed at the same time upon the enemy. 

some ill fate decree that i should not retreat unobserved. scarcely had 
i withdrawn three pace when he start from his seat, and, turning 
towards me, walk with a quick pace. the shadow of the rock, and the 
improbability of meeting an enemy here, conceal me for a moment from 
his observation. i stood still. the slightest motion would have 
attract his notice. at present, the narrow space engaged all his 
vigilance. cautious footsteps, and attention to the path, were 
indispensable to his safety. the respite was momentary, and i employ 
it in my own defence. 

how otherwise could i act? the danger that impende aim at nothing 
less than my life. to take the life of another was the only method of 
avert it. the means were in my hand, and they were used. in an 
extremity like this, my muscle would have act almost in defiance of 
my will. 

the stroke was quick as lightning, and the wound mortal and deep. he had 
not time to descry the author of his fate, but, sink on the path, 
expire without a groan. the hatchet bury itself in his breast, and 
roll with him to the bottom of the precipice. 

never before had i taken the life of a human creature. on this head i 
had, indeed, entertain somewhat of religious scruples. these scruple 
did not forbid me to defend myself, but they made me cautious and 
reluctant to decide. though they could not withhold my hand when urge 
by a necessity like this, they were sufficient to make me look back upon 
the deed with remorse and dismay. 

i did not escape all compunction in the present instance, but the tumult 
of my feelings was quick allayed. to quench my thirst was a 
consideration by which all other were supplanted. i approach the 
torrent, and not only drank copiously, but lave my head, neck, and 
arms, in this delicious element. 




chapter xviii. 


never was any delight worthy of comparison with the rapture which i 
then experienced. life, that was rapid ebbing, appear to return upon 
me with redouble violence. my languors, my excruciating heat, vanish 
in a moment, and i felt prepared to undergo the labour of hercules. 
have fully supply the demand of nature in this respect, i return 
to reflection on the circumstances of my situation. the path winding 
round the hill was now free from all impediments. what remain but to 
precipitate my flight? i might speedy place myself beyond all danger. 
i might gain some hospitable shelter, where my fatigues might be 
repair by repose, and my wound be cured. i might likewise impart to 
my protector seasonable information of the enemy who meditate their 
destruction. 

i thought upon the condition of the hapless girl whom i had left in the 
power of the savages. was it impossible to rescue her? might i not 
relieve her from her bonds, and make her the companion of my flight? the 
exploit was perilous, but not impracticable. there was something 
dastard and ignominious in withdraw from the danger, and leave a 
helpless being expose to it. a single minute might suffice to snatch 
her from death or captivity. the parent might deserve that i should 
hazard or even sacrifice my life in the cause of their child. 

after some fluctuation, i determined to return to the cavern and attempt 
the rescue of the girl. the success of this project depend on the 
continuance of their sleep. it was proper to approach with wariness, and 
to heed the smallest token which might bespeak their condition. i crept 
along the path, bend my ear forward to catch any sound that might 
arise. i heard nothing but the half-stifled sob of the girl. 

i enter with the slowest and most anxious circumspection. every thing 
was found in its pristine state. the girl notice my entrance with a 
mixture of terror and joy. my gesture and looks enjoin upon her 
silence. i stoop down, and, taking another hatchet, cut asunder the 
deer-skin thong by which her wrist and ankle were tied. i then made 
sign for her to rise and follow me. she willing comply with my 
directions; but her benumbed joint and lacerate sinew refuse to 
support her. there was no time to be lost; i therefore lift her in my 
arms, and, feeble and totter as i was, proceed with this burden 
along the perilous steep and over a most rugged-path. 

i hope that some exertion would enable her to retrieve the use of her 
limbs. i set her, therefore, on her feet, exhort her to walk as well 
as she was able, and promising her my occasional assistance. the poor 
girl was not deficient in zeal, and presently move along with light and 
quick steps. we speedy reach the bottom of the hill. 

no fancy can conceive a scene more wild and desolate than that which now 
present itself. the soil was nearly cover with sharp fragment of 
stone. between these, sprung bramble and creep vines, whose twigs, 
crossing and intertwine with each other, add to the roughness below, 
made the passage infinite toilsome. scattered over this space were 
single cedar with their ragged spine and wreath of moss, and copse 
of dwarf oaks, which were only new emblem of sterility. 

i was wholly unacquainted with the scene before me. no mark of 
habitation or culture, no trace of the footstep of men, were 
discernible. i scarcely knew in what region of the globe i was placed. i 
had come hither by means so inexplicable as to leave it equally in doubt 
whether i was separate from my paternal abode by a river or an ocean. 

i made inquiry of my companion, but she was unable to talk coherently. 
she answer my question with weeping, and sobs, and entreaty to fly 
from the scene of her distress. i collected from her, at length, that 
her father's house had been attack on the preceding evening, and all 
the family but herself destroyed. since this disaster she had walk 
very fast and a great way, but knew not how far or in what direction. 

in a wilderness like this, my only hope was to light upon obscure paths, 
made by cattle. meanwhile i endeavour to adhere to one line, and to 
burst through the vexatious obstacle which encumber our way. the 
ground was conceal by the bushes, and we were perplexed and fatigue 
by a continual succession of hollow and prominences. at one moment we 
were nearly thrown headlong into a pit. at another we struck our feet 
against the angle of stones. the branch of the oak rebound in our 
face or entangle our legs, and the unseen thorn inflict on us a 
thousand wounds. 

i was obliged, in these arduous circumstances, to support not only 
myself, but my companion. her strength was overpower by her evening 
journey, and the terror of being overtaken incessant harass her. 

sometimes we light upon track which afford us an easy footing and 
inspired us with courage to proceed. these, for a time, terminate at a 
brook or in a bog, and we were once more compel to go forward at 
random. one of these track insensible became more beaten, and, at 
length, exhibit the trace of wheels. to this i adhered, confident 
that it would finally conduct us to a dwelling. 

on either side, the undergrowth of shrub and bramble continue as 
before. sometimes small space were observed, which had lately been 
clear by fire. at length a vacant space, of large dimensions than had 
hitherto occurred, present itself to my view. it was a field of some 
acres, that had, apparently, been upturned by the hoe. at the corner of 
this field was a small house. 

my heart leap with joy at this sight. i hasten towards it, in the 
hope that my uncertainties, and toils, and dangers, were now drawing to 
a close. this dwelling was suit to the poverty and desolate which 
surround it. it consist of a few unhewn log laid upon each other, 
to the height of eight or ten feet, including a quadrangular space of 
similar dimensions, and cover by a thatch. there was no window, light 
being sufficient admitted into the crevice between the logs. these 
had formerly been loose plastered with clay; but air and rain had 
crumble and wash the greater part of this rude cement away. somewhat 
like a chimney, built of half-burnt bricks, was perceive at one corner. 
the door was fasten by a leathern thong, tie to a peg. 

all within was silence and darkness. i knock at the door and called, 
but no one move or answered. the tenant, whoever he was, was absent. 
his leave could not be obtained, and i, therefore, enter without it. 
the autumn had made some progress, and the air was frosty and sharp. my 
mind and muscle had been of late so strenuous occupied, that the cold 
had not been felt. the cessation of exercise, however, quick restore 
my sensibility in this respect, but the unhappy girl complain of being 
half frozen. 

fire, therefore, was the first object of my search. happily, some ember 
were found upon the hearth, together with potato-stalks and dry chips. 
of these, with much difficulty, i kindle a fire, by which some warmth 
was impart to our shiver limbs. the light enable me, as i sat upon 
the ground, to survey the interior of this mansion. three saplings, 
strip of their branch and bound together at their end by twigs, 
form a kind of bedstead, which was raise from the ground by four 
stones. ropes stretch across these, and cover by a blanket, 
constitute the bed. a board, of which one end rest on the bedstead 
and the other was thrust between the log that compose the wall, 
sustain the stale fragment of a rye-loaf, and a cedar bucket kept 
entire by withe instead of hoops. in the bucket was a little water, 
full of droppings from the roof, drown insects, and sand. a basket or 
two neat made, and a hoe, with a stake thrust into it by way of 
handle, made up all the furniture that was visible. 

next to cold, hunger was the most urgent necessity by which we were now 
pressed. this was no time to give ear to scruples. we, therefore, 
unceremonious divide the bread and water between us. i had now 
leisure to bestow some regards upon the future. 

these remnant of fire and food convinced me that this dwelling was 
usually inhabited, and that it had lately been deserted. some engagement 
had probably carry the tenant abroad. his absence might be terminate 
in a few minutes, or might endure through the night. on his return, i 
question not my power to appease any indignation he might feel at the 
liberties which i had taken. i was willing to suppose him one who would 
readily afford us all the information and succour that we needed. 

if he should not return till sunrise, i meant to resume my journey. by 
the comfortable meal we had made, and the repose of a few hours, we 
should be considerably invigorate and refreshed, and the road would 
lead us to some more hospitable tenement. 

my thought were too tumultuous, and my situation too precarious, to 
allow me to sleep. the girl, on the contrary, soon sank into a sweet 
oblivion of all her cares. she laid herself, by my advice, upon the bed, 
and left me to ruminate without interruption. 

i was not wholly free from the apprehension of danger. what influence 
this boisterous and solitary life might have upon the temper of the 
being who inhabit this hut, i could not predict. how soon the indian 
might awake, and what path they would pursue, i was equally unable to 
guess. it was by no means impossible that they might tread upon my 
footsteps, and knock, in a few minutes, at the door of this cottage. it 
behoove me to make all the preparation in my power against untoward 
incidents. 

i had not part with the gun which i had first seize in the cavern, 
nor with the hatchet which i had afterwards use to cut the band of the 
girl. these were at once my trophy and my means of defence, which it 
had been rash and absurd to have relinquished. my present reliance was 
placed upon these. 

i now, for the first time, examine the prize that i had made. other 
consideration had prevent me, till now, from examine the structure 
of the piece; but i could not but observe that it had two barrels, and 
was lighter and small than an ordinary musket. the light of the fire 
now enable me to inspect it with more accuracy. 

scarcely had i fixed my eyes upon the stock, when i perceive mark that 
were familiar to my apprehension. shape, ornaments, and ciphers, were 
evidently the same with those of a piece which i had frequent handled. 
the mark were of a kind which could not be mistaken. this piece was 
mine; and, when i left my uncle's house, it was deposited, as i 
believed, in the closet of my chamber. 

thou wilt easily conceive the inference which this circumstance 
suggested. my hair rose and my teeth chatter with horror. my whole 
frame was petrified, and i pace to and fro, hurried from the chimney to 
the door, and from the door to the chimney, with the misguided fury of a 
maniac. 

i need no proof of my calamity more incontestable than this. my uncle 
and my sister had been murdered; the dwelling had been pillaged, and 
this had been a part of the plunder. defenceless and asleep, they were 
assail by these inexorable enemies, and i, who ought to have been 
their protector and champion, was remove to an immeasurable distance, 
and was disabled, by some accursed chance, from afford them the 
succour which they needed. 

for a time, i doubt whether i had not witness and share this 
catastrophe. i had no memory of the circumstances that precede my 
awake in the pit. had not the cause of my being cast into this abyss 
some connection with the ruin of my family? had i not been drag 
hither by these savage and reduced, by their malice, to that breathless 
and insensible condition? was i born to a malignant destiny never tired 
of persecuting? thus had my parent and their infant offspring perished, 
and thus complete was the fate of all those to whom my affection 
cleaved, and whom the first disaster had spared. 

hitherto the death of the savage, whom i had dispatch with my hatchet, 
had not been remember without some remorse. now my emotion were 
total changed. i was somewhat comfort in thinking that thus much of 
necessary vengeance had been executed. new and more vehement regrets 
were excited by reflect on the forbearance i had practised when so 
much was in my power. all the miscreant had been at my mercy, and a 
bloody retribution might, with safety and ease, have been inflict on 
their prostrate bodies. 

it was now too late. what of consolation or of hope remain to me? to 
return to my ancient dwelling, now pollute with blood, or, perhaps, 
nothing but a smoking ruin, was abhorred. life, connected with the 
remembrance of my misfortunes, was detestable. i was no long anxious 
for flight. no change of the scene but that which terminate all 
consciousness could i endure to think of. 

amidst these gloomy meditation the idea was sudden suggest of 
returning, with the utmost expedition, to the cavern. it was possible 
that the assassin were still asleep. he who was appoint to watch, and 
to make, in due season, the signal for resume their march, was forever 
silent. without this signal it was not unlikely that they would sleep 
till dawn of day. but, if they should be roused, they might be overtaken 
or met, and, by choose a proper station, two victim might at least 
fall. the ultimate event to myself would surely be fatal; but my own 
death was an object of desire rather than of dread. to die thus 
speedily, and after some atone was made for those who had already 
been slain, was sweet. 

the way to the mountain was difficult and tedious, but the ridge was 
distinct seen from the door of the cottage, and i trust that 
auspicious chance would lead me to that part of it where my prey was to 
be found. i snatch up the gun and tomahawk in a transport of 
eagerness. on examine the former, i found that both barrel were 
deep loaded. 

this piece was of extraordinary workmanship. it was the legacy of an 
english officer, who die in bengal, to sarsefield. it was construct 
for the purpose not of sport but of war. the artist had made it a 
congery of tube and springs, by which every purpose of protection and 
offence was effectually served. a dagger's blade was attach to it, 
capable of being fixed at the end, and of answer the destructive 
purpose of a bayonet. on his departure from solesbury, my friend left 
it, as a pledge of his affection, in my possession. hitherto i had 
chiefly employ it in shoot at a mark, in order to improve my sight; 
now was i to profit by the gift in a different way. 

thus armed, i prepared to sally forth on my adventurous expedition. 
sober view might have speedy succeed to the present tempest of my 
passions. i might have gradual discover the romantic and criminal 
temerity of my project, the folly of revenge, and the duty of preserve 
my life for the benefit of mankind. i might have suspect the propriety 
of my conclusion, and have admitted some doubt as to the catastrophe 
which i imagine to have befallen my uncle and sisters. i might, at 
least, have consent to ascertain their condition with my own eyes, and 
for this end have return to the cottage, and have patient wait 
till the morning light should permit me to resume my journey. 

this conduct was preclude by a new incident. before i open the door i 
look through a crevice of the wall, and perceive three human figures 
at the farther end of the field. they approach the house. though 
indistinct seen, something in their port persuade me that these were 
the indian from whom i had lately parted. i was startle but not 
dismayed. my thirst of vengeance was still powerful, and i believe that 
the moment of its gratification was hastening. in a short time they 
would arrive and enter the house. in what manner should they be 
received? 

i studied not my own security. it was the scope of my wish to kill the 
whole number of my foes; but, that being done, i was indifferent to the 
consequences. i desire not to live to relate or to exult in the deed. 

to go forth was perilous and useless. all that remain was to sit upon 
the ground opposite the door, and fire at each as he entered. in the 
hasty survey i had taken of this apartment, one object had been 
overlooked, or imperfect noticed. close to the chimney was an 
aperture, form by a cavity partly in the wall and in the ground. it 
was the entrance of an oven, which resembled, on the outside, a mound of 
earth, and which was fill with dry stalk of potato and other 
rubbish. 

into this it was possible to thrust my body. a sort of screen might be 
form of the brushwood, and more deliberate and effectual execution be 
done upon the enemy. i weigh not the disadvantage of this scheme, but 
precipitate threw myself into this cavity. i discovered, in an 
instant, that it was total unfit for my purpose; but it was too late 
to repair my miscarriage. 

this wall of the hovel was placed near the verge of a sand-bank. the 
oven was erect on the very brink. this bank, being of a loose and 
mutable soil, could not sustain my weight. it sunk, and i sunk along 
with it. the height of the bank was three or four feet, so that, though 
disconcert and embarrassed, i received no injury. i still grasp my 
gun, and resume my feet in a moment. 

what was now to be done? the bank screen me from the view of the 
savages. the thicket was hard by, and, if i were eager to escape, the 
way was obvious and sure. but, though single, though enfeeble by toil, 
by abstinence, and by disease, and though so much exceed in number and 
strength by my foes, i was determined to await and provoke the contest. 

in addition to the desperate impulse of passion, i was sway by 
thought of the danger which beset the sleep girl, and from which my 
flight would leave her without protection. how strange is the destiny 
that govern mankind! the consequence of shroud myself in this cavity 
had not been foreseen. it was an expedient which courage and not 
cowardice suggested; and yet it was the only expedient by which flight 
had been render practicable. to have issue from the door would only 
have been to confront, and not to elude, the danger. 

the first impulse prompt me to re-enter the cottage by this avenue, 
but this could not be done with certainty and expedition. what then 
remained? while i deliberated, the men approached, and, after a moment's 
hesitation, enter the house, the door being partly open. 

the fire on the hearth enable them to survey the room. one of them 
utter a sudden exclamation of surprise. this was easily interpreted. 
they had notice the girl who had lately been their captive lie asleep 
on the blanket. their astonishment at finding her here, and in this 
condition, may be easily conceived. 

i now reflect that i might place myself, without being observed, near 
the entrance, at an angle of the building, and shoot at each as he 
successive came forth. i perceive that the bank conform to two 
side of the house, and that i might gain a view of the front and of the 
entrance, without expose myself to observation. 

i lost no time in gain this station. the bank was as high as my 
breast. it was easy, therefore, to crouch beneath it, to bring my eye 
close to the verge, and, lay my gun upon the top of it among the 
grass, with its muzzle pointed to the door, patient to wait their 
forthcoming. 

my eye and my ear were equally attentive to what was passing. a low and 
mutter conversation was maintain in the house. presently i heard a 
heavy stroke descend. i shuddered, and my blood ran cold at the sound. i 
entertain no doubt but that it was the stroke of a hatchet on the head 
or breast of the helpless sleeper. 

it was follow by a loud shriek. the continuance of these shriek 
prove that the stroke had not been instantly fatal. i wait to hear it 
repeated, but the sound that now arose were like those produce by 
drag somewhat along the ground. the shrieks, meanwhile, were 
incessant and piteous. my heart faltered, and i saw that mighty effort 
must be made to preserve my joint and my nerves steadfast. all depend 
on the strenuous exertion and the fortunate dexterity of a moment. 

one now approach the door, and came forth, drag the girl, whom he 
held by the hair, after him. what hinder me from shoot at his first 
appearance, i know not. this had been my previous resolution. my hand 
touched the trigger, and, as he moved, the piece was level at his 
right ear. perhaps the momentous consequence of my failure made me wait 
till his cease to move might render my aim more sure. 

have drag the girl, still piteous shrieking, to the distance of 
ten feet from the house, he threw her from him with violence. she fell 
upon the ground, and, observing him level his piece at her breast, 
renew her supplicate in a still more piercing tone. little did the 
forlorn wretch think that her deliverance was certain and near. i 
rebuke myself for have thus long delayed. i fired, and my enemy sunk 
upon the ground without a struggle. 

thus far had success attend me in this unequal contest. the next shot 
would leave me nearly powerless. if that, however, prove as unerring as 
the first, the chance of defeat were lessened. the savage within, 
knowing the intentions of their associate with regard to the captive 
girl, would probably mistake the report which they heard for that of his 
piece. their mistake, however, would speedy give place to doubts, and 
they would rush forth to ascertain the truth. it behoove me to provide 
a similar reception for him that next appeared. 

it was as i expected. scarcely was my eye again fixed upon the entrance, 
when a tawny and terrific visage was stretch fearful forth. it was 
the signal of his fate. his glances, cast wildly and swift round, 
light upon me, and on the fatal instrument which was pointed at his 
forehead. his muscle were at once exert to withdraw his head, and to 
vociferate a warning to his fellow; but his movement was too slow. the 
ball enter above his ear. he tumble headlong to the ground, bereaved 
of sensation though not of life, and had power only to struggle and 
mutter. 




chapter xix. 


think not that i relate these things with exultation or tranquillity. 
all my education and the habit of my life tend to unfit me for a 
contest and a scene like this. but i was not govern by the soul which 
usually regulate my conduct. i had imbibed, from the unparalleled 
event which had lately happened, a spirit vengeful, unrelenting, and 
ferocious. 

there was now an interval for flight. throw my weapon away, i might 
gain the thicket in a moment. i had no ammunition, nor would time be 
afford me to reload my piece. my antagonist would render my poniard 
and my speed of no use to me. should he miss me as i fled, the girl 
would remain to expiate, by her agony and death, the fate of his 
companions. 

these thought pass through my mind in a short time than is demand 
to express them. they yield to an expedient suggest by the sight of 
the gun that had been raise to destroy the girl, and which now lay upon 
the ground. i am not large of bone, but am not deficient in agile and 
strength. all that remain to me of these quality was now exerted; 
and, drop my own piece, i leap upon the bank, and flew to seize my 
prize. 

it was not till i snatch it from the ground, that the propriety of 
regain my former post rush upon my apprehension. he that was still 
post in the hovel would mark me through the seam of the wall, and 
render my destruction sure. i once more ran towards the bank, with the 
intention to throw myself below it. all this was perform in an 
instant; but my vigilant foe was aware of his advantage, and fire 
through an opening between the logs. the bullet graze my cheek, and 
produce a benumb sensation that made me instantly fall to the earth. 
though bereaved of strength, and fraught with the belief that i had 
received a mortal wound, my caution was not remitted. i loosen not my 
grasp of the gun, and the posture into which i accidental fell enable 
me to keep an eye upon the house and a hand upon the trigger. perceive 
my condition, the savage rush from his covert in order to complete his 
work; but at three steps from the threshold he received my bullet in his 
breast. the uplift tomahawk fell from his hand, and, utter a loud 
shriek, he fell upon the body of his companion. his cries struck upon my 
heart, and i wish that his better fortune had cast this evil from him 
upon me. 

thus i have told thee a bloody and disastrous tale. when thou reflectest 
on the mild of my habits, my antipathy to scene of violence and 
bloodshed, my unacquaintance with the use of fire-arms and the motive 
of a soldier, thou wilt scarcely allow credit to my story. that one 
rush into these dangers, unfurnished with stratagem or weapons, 
dishearten and enfeeble by hardship and pain, should subdue four 
antagonist train from their infancy to the artifice and exertion of 
indian warfare, will seem the vision of fancy, rather than the lesson of 
truth. 

i lift my head from the ground and ponder upon this scene. the 
magnitude of this exploit made me question its reality. by attend to 
my own sensations, i discover that i had received no wound, or, at 
least, none of which there was reason to complain. the blood flow 
plentiful from my cheek, but the injury was superficial. it was 
otherwise with my antagonists. the last that had fallen now cease to 
groan. their huge limbs, inure to combat and _war-worn_, were useless 
to their own defence, and to the injury of others. 

the destruction that i witness was vast. three beings, full of energy 
and heroism, endow with mind strenuous and lofty, pour out their 
lives before me. i was the instrument of their destruction. this scene 
of carnage and blood was laid by me. to this havoc and horror was i led 
by such rapid footsteps! 

my anguish was mingle with astonishment. in spite of the force and 
uniform with which my senses were impress by external objects, the 
transition i had undergone was so wild and inexplicable; all that i had 
performed, all that i had witness since my egress from the pit, were 
so contradictory to precedent events, that i still clung to the belief 
that my thought were confuse by delirium. from these reverie i was at 
length recall by the groan of the girl, who lay near me on the 
ground. 

i went to her and endeavour to console her. i found that, while lie 
in the bed, she had received a blow upon the side, which was still 
productive of acute pain. she was unable to rise or to walk, and it was 
plain that one or more of her rib had been fracture by the blow. 

i knew not what means to devise for our mutual relief. it was possible 
that the nearest dwelling was many league distant. i knew not in what 
direction to go in order to find it, and my strength would not suffice 
to carry my wound companion thither in my arms. there was no expedient 
but to remain in this field of blood till the morning. 

i had scarcely form this resolution before the report of a musket was 
heard at a small distance. at the same moment, i distinct heard the 
whistle of a bullet near me. i now remember that, of the five 
indian whom i saw in the cavern, i was acquaint with the destiny only 
of four. the fifth might be still alive, and fortune might reserve for 
him the task of avenge his companions. his steps might now be tend 
hither in search of them. 

the musket belong to him who was shot upon the threshold was still 
charged. it was discreet to make all the provision in my power against 
danger. i possessed myself of this gun, and, seating myself on the 
ground, look careful on all sides, to descry the approach of the 
enemy. i listen with breathless eagerness. 

presently voice were heard. they ascend from that part of the thicket 
from which my view was intercept by the cottage. these voice had 
something in them that bespoke them to belong to friends and countrymen. 
as yet i was unable to distinguish words. 

presently my eye was attract to one quarter, by a sound as of feet 
trample down bushes. several head were seen moving in succession, and 
at length the whole person was conspicuous. one after another leap 
over a kind of mound which border the field, and made towards the spot 
where i sat. this band was compose of ten or twelve persons, with each 
a gun upon his shoulder. their guise, the moment it was perceived, 
dissipated all my apprehensions. 

they came within the distance of a few pace before they discover me. 
one stopped, and, bespeak the attention of his followers, call to 
know who was there. i answer that i was a friend, who entreat their 
assistance. i shall not paint their astonishment when, on coming nearer, 
they beheld me surround by the arms and dead body of my enemies. 

i sat upon the ground, support my head with my left hand, and rest 
on my knee the stock of a heavy musket. my countenance was wan and 
haggard, my neck and bosom were dyed in blood, and my limbs, almost 
strip by the bramble of their slender covering, were lacerate by a 
thousand wounds. three savages, two of whom were steep in gore, lay at 
a small distance, with the trace of recent life on their visages. hard 
by was the girl, vent her anguish in the deepest groans, and 
entreat relief from the new-comers. 

one of the company, on approach the girl, betray the utmost 
perturbation. "good god!" he cried, "is this a dream? can it be you? 
speak!" 

"ah, my father! my father!" answer she, "it is i indeed." 

the company, attract by this dialogue, crowded round the girl, whom 
her father, clasp in his arms, lift from the ground, and pressed, 
in a transport of joy, to his breast. this delight was succeed by 
solicitude respecting her condition. she could only answer his inquiry 
by complain that her side was bruise to pieces. "how came you 
here?"--"who hurt you?"--"where did the indian carry you?"--were 
question to which she could make no reply but by sob and plaints. 

my own calamity were forgotten in contemplate the fond and 
compassion of the man for his child. i derive new joy from reflect 
that i had not abandoned her, and that she owe her preservation to my 
efforts. the inquiry which the girl was unable to answer were now put 
to me. every one interrogate me who i was, whence i had come, and what 
had given rise to this bloody contest. 

i was not willing to expatiate on my story. the spirit which had 
hitherto sustain me began now to subside. my strength ebb away with 
my blood. tremors, lassitude, and deadly cold, invade me, and i faint 
on the ground. 

such is the capricious constitution of the human mind. while danger 
were at hand, while my life was to be preserve only by zeal, and 
vigilance, and courage, i was not wanting to myself. had my peril 
continued, or even multiplied, no doubt my energy would have kept 
equal pace with them; but the moment that i was encompass by 
protectors, and placed in security, i grew powerless and faint. my 
weakness was proportion to the duration and intensity of my previous 
efforts, and the swoon into which i now sunk was, no doubt, mistaken by 
the spectator for death. 

on recover from this swoon, my sensation were not unlike those which 
i had experienced on awake in the pit. for a moment a misty 
involved every object, and i was able to distinguish nothing. my sight, 
by rapid degrees, was restored, my painful dizzy was banished, and i 
survey the scene before me with anxiety and wonder. 

i found myself stretch upon the ground. i perceive the cottage and 
the neighbouring thicket, illuminate by a decline moon. my head 
rest upon something, which, on turning to examine, i found to be one 
of the slain indians. the other two remain upon the earth, at a small 
distance, and in the attitude in which they had fallen. their arms, the 
wound girl, and the troop who were near me when i fainted, were gone. 

my head had repose upon the breast of him whom i had shot in this part 
of his body. the blood had cease to ooze from the wound, but my 
dishevelled locks were matt and steep in that gore which had 
overflow and choke up the orifice. i start from this detest 
pillow, and regain my feet. 

i did not sudden recall what had lately passed, or comprehend the 
nature of my situation. at length, however, late event were 
recollected. 

that i should be abandoned in this forlorn state by these men seem to 
argue a degree of cowardice or cruelty of which i should have thought 
them incapable. presently, however, i reflect that appearances might 
have easily misle them into a belief of my death. on this supposition, 
to have carry me away, or to have stay beside me, would be useless. 
other enemy might be abroad; or their families, now that their fear 
were somewhat tranquillized, might require their presence and 
protection. 

i went into the cottage. the fire still burned, and afford me a genial 
warmth. i sat before it, and began to ruminate on the state to which i 
was reduced, and on the measure i should next pursue. daylight could 
not be very distant. should i remain in this hovel till the morning, or 
immediately resume my journey? i was feeble, indeed; but, by remain 
here, should i not increase my feebleness? the soon i should gain some 
human habitation the better; whereas watchful and hunger would 
render me, at each minute, less able to proceed than on the former. 

this spot might be visit on the next day; but this was involved in 
uncertainty. the visitants, should any come, would come merely to 
examine and bury the dead, and bring with them neither the clothing nor 
the food which my necessity demanded. the road was sufficient 
discernible, and would, unavoidably, conduct me to some dwelling. i 
determined, therefore, to set out without delay. even in this state i 
was not unmindful that my safety might require the precaution of being 
armed. besides, the fusil which had been given me by sarsefield, and 
which i had so unexpected recovered, had lost none of its value in my 
eyes. i hope that it had escape the search of the troop who had been 
here, and still lay below the bank in the spot where i had drop it. 

in this hope i was not deceived. it was found. i possessed myself of the 
powder and shot belong to one of the savages, and loaded it. thus 
equip for defence, i regain the road, and proceeded, with alacrity, 
on my way. for the wound in my cheek, nature had provided a styptic, but 
the sore was extreme, and i thought of no remedy but water, with 
which i might wash away the blood. my thirst likewise incommode me, and 
i look with eager for the trace of a spring. in a soil like that 
of the wilderness around me, nothing was less to be expect than to 
light upon water. in this respect, however, my destiny was propitious. i 
quick perceive water in the ruts. it trickle hither from the thicket 
on one side, and, pursue it among the bushes, i reach the bubble 
source. though scanty and brackish, it afford me unspeakable 
refreshment. 

thou wilt think, perhaps, that my peril were now at an end; that the 
blood i had already shed was sufficient for my safety. i fervent hope 
that no new exigence would occur compel me to use the arms that i 
bore in my own defence. i form a sort of resolution to shun the 
contest with a new enemy, almost at the expense of my own life. i was 
satiate and gorge with slaughter, and thought upon a new act of 
destruction with abhorrence and loathing. 

but, though i dread to encounter a new enemy, i was sensible that an 
enemy might possibly be at hand. i had move forward with caution, and 
my sight and hearing were attentive to the slightest tokens. other 
troops, besides that which i encountered, might be hover near, and of 
that troop i remember that one at least had survived. 

the gratification which the spring had afford me was so great, that i 
was in no haste to depart. i lay upon a rock, which chance to be shade 
by a tree behind me. from this post i could overlook the road to some 
distance, and, at the same time, be shade from the observation of 
others. 

my eye was now caught by movements which appear like those of a beast. 
in different circumstances, i should have instantly supposed it to be a 
wolf, or panther, or bear. now my suspicion were alive on a different 
account, and my startle fancy figured to itself nothing but a human 
adversary. 

a thicket was on either side of the road. that opposite to my station 
was discontinue at a small distance by the cultivated field. the road 
continue along this field, bound by the thicket on the one side and 
the open space on the other. to this space the being who was now 
describe was cautious approaching. 

he move upon all fours, and presently came near enough to be 
distinguished. his disfigure limbs, pendant from his ear and nose, 
and his shorn locks, were indubitable indication of a savage, 
occasional he rear himself above the bushes, and scanned, with 
suspicious vigilance, the cottage and the space surrounding it. then he 
stooped, and crept along as before. 

i was at no loss to interpret these appearances. this was my survive 
enemy. he was unacquainted with the fate of his associates, and was now 
approach the theatre of carnage to ascertain their fate. 

once more was the advantage afford me. from this spot might unerring 
aim be taken, and the last of this hostile troop be made to share the 
fate of the rest. should i fire, or suffer him to pass in safety? 

my abhorrence of bloodshed was not abated. but i had not foreseen this 
occurrence. my success hitherto had seem to depend upon a combination 
of fortunate incidents, which could not be expect again to take place; 
but now was i invest with the same power. the mark was near; nothing 
obstruct or delayed; i incur no danger, and the event was certain. 

why should he be suffer to live? he came hither to murder and despoil 
my friends; this work he has, no doubt, performed. nay, has he not borne 
his part in the destruction of my uncle and my sisters? he will live 
only to pursue the same sanguinary trade; to drink the blood and exult 
in the lament of his unhappy foe and of my own brethren. fate has 
reserved him for a bloody and violent death. for how long a time soever 
it may be deferred, it is thus that his career will inevitable 
terminate. 

should he be spared, he will still roam in the wilderness, and i may 
again be fated to encounter him. then our mutual situation may be widely 
different, and the advantage i now possess may be his. 

while hasty revolving these thoughts, i was thorough aware that one 
event might take place which would render all deliberation useless. 
should he spy me where i lay, my fluctuate must end. my safety would 
indispensable require me to shoot. this persuasion made me keep a 
steadfast eye upon his motions, and be prepared to anticipate his 
assault. 

it now most seasonable occur to me that one essential duty remain 
to be performed. one operation, without which fire-arms are useless, had 
been unaccountable omitted. my piece was uncocked. i did not reflect 
that in moving the spring a sound would necessarily be produce 
sufficient to alarm him. but i knew that the chance of escape his 
notice, should i be perfectly mute and still, were extremely slender, 
and that, in such a case, his movements would be quick than the light: 
it behoove me, therefore, to repair my omission. 

the sound struck him with alarm. he turn and dart at me an inquiring 
glance. i saw that forbearance was no long in my power; but my heart 
sunk while i comply with what may surely be deem an indispensable 
necessity. this faltering, perhaps, it was that made me swerve somewhat 
from the fatal line. he was disabled by the wound, but not killed. 

he lost all power of resistance, and was, therefore, no long to be 
dreaded. he roll upon the ground, utter doleful shrieks, and 
throw his limb into those contortion which bespeak the keenest 
agony to which ill-fated man is subject. horror, and compassion, and 
remorse, were mingle into one sentiment, and took possession of my 
heart. to shut out this spectacle, i withdrew from the spot, but i 
stop before i had move beyond hearing of his cries. 

the impulse that drove me from the scene was pusillanimous and cowardly. 
the past, however deplorable, could not be recalled; but could not i 
afford some relief to this wretch? could not i at least bring his pang 
to a speedy close? thus he might continue, writhe and calling upon 
death, for hours. why should his misery be useless prolonged? 

there was but one way to end them. to kill him outright was the dictate 
of compassion and of duty. i hasty returned, and once more level my 
piece at his head. it was a loathsome obligation, and was perform with 
unconquerable reluctance. thus to assault and to mangle the body of an 
enemy, already prostrate and powerless, was an act worthy of abhorrence; 
yet it was, in this case, prescribed by pity. 

my falter hand render this second bullet ineffectual. one 
expedient, still more detestable, remained. have gone thus far, it 
would have been inhuman to stop short. his heart might easily be pierce 
by the bayonet, and his struggle would cease. 

this task of cruel lenity was at length finished. i drop the weapon 
and threw myself on the ground, overpower by the horrors of this 
scene. such are the deed which perverse nature compel thousand of 
rational being to perform and to witness! such is the spectacle, 
endless prolonged and diversified, which is exhibit in every field 
of battle; of which habit and example, the temptation of gain, and the 
illusion of honour, will make us, not reluctant or indifferent, but 
zealous and delight actor and beholders! 

thus, by a series of event impossible to be compute or foreseen, was 
the destruction of a band, select from their fellow for an arduous 
enterprise, distinguished by prowess and skill, and equally armed 
against surprise and force, complete by the hand of a boy, uninured to 
hostility, unprovided with arms, precipitate and timorous! i have noted 
men who seem born for no end but by their achievement to belie 
experience, and baffle foresight, and outstrip belief. would to god that 
i had not deserve to be number among these! but what power was it 
that call me from the sleep of death just in time to escape the 
merciless knife of this enemy? had my swoon continue till he had 
reach the spot, he would have effectuate my death by new wound and 
torn away the skin from my brows. such are the subtle thread on which 
hang the fate of man and of the universe! 

while engaged in these reflections, i perceive that the moonlight had 
begun to fade before that of the sun. a dusky and reddish hue spread 
itself over the east. cheer by this appearance, i once more resume my 
feet and the road. i left the savage where he lay, but made prize of 
his tomahawk. i had left my own in the cavern; and this weapon add 
little to my burden. prompt by some freak of fancy, i stuck his musket 
in the ground, and left it standing upright in the middle of the road. 




chapter xx. 


i move forward with as quick a pace as my feeble limb would permit. i 
did not allow myself to meditate. the great object of my wish was a 
dwelling where food and repose might be procured. i look earnest 
forward, and on each side, in search of some token of human residence; 
but the spot of cultivation, the _well-pole_, the _worm fence_, 
and the hayrick, were nowhere to be seen. i did not even meet with a 
wild hog or a bewilder cow. the path was narrow, and on either side 
was a trackless wilderness. on the right and left were the wave 
lines of mountainous ridges, which had no peculiarity enabling me to 
ascertain whether i had ever before seen them. 

at length i notice that the track of wheels had disappear from the 
path that i was treading; that it became more narrow, and exhibit 
few mark of being frequented. these appearances were discouraging. i 
now suspect that i had taken a wrong direction, and, instead of 
approaching, was recede from, the habitation of men. 

it was wisest, however, to proceed. the road could not but have some 
origin as well as end. some hour pass away in this uncertainty. the 
sun rose, and by noonday i seem to be farther than ever from the end 
of my toils. the path was more obscure, and the wilderness more rugged. 
thirst more incommode me than hunger, but relief was seasonable 
afford by the brook that flow across the path. 

coming to one of these, and have slake my thirst, i sat down upon the 
bank, to reflect on my situation. the circu of the path had 
frequent been noticed, and i began to suspect that, though i had 
travelled long, i had not move far from the spot where i had commence 
my pilgrimage. 

turning my eyes on all sides, i notice a sort of pool, form by the 
rivulet, at a few pace distant from the road. in approach and 
inspect it, i observe the footstep of cattle, who had retired by a 
path that seem much beaten: i likewise notice a cedar bucket, broken 
and old, lie on the margin. these token revive my droop spirits, 
arid i betook myself to this new track. it was intricate, but, at 
length, led up a steep, the summit of which was of better soil than that 
of which the flat consisted. a clover-field, and several 
apple-trees,--sure attendant of man,--were now discovered. from this 
space i enter a corn-field, and at length, to my inexpressible joy, 
caught a glimpse of a house. 

this dwelling was far different from that i had lately left. it was as 
small and as low, but its wall consist of boards. a window of four 
pane admitted the light, and a chimney of brick, well burnt and neat 
arranged, peep over the roof. as i approached, i heard the voice of 
children and the hum of a spinning-wheel. 

i cannot make thee conceive the delight which was afford me by all 
these tokens. i now found myself, indeed, among being like myself, and 
from whom hospitable entertainment might be confident expected. i 
compass the house, and made my appearance at the door. 

a good woman, busy at her wheel, with two children play on the ground 
before her, were the object that now present themselves. the 
uncouth of my garb, my wild and weatherworn appearance, my fusil and 
tomahawk, could not but startle them. the woman stop her wheel, and 
gaze as if a spectre had start into view. 

i was somewhat aware of these consequences, and endeavour to elude 
them by assume an air of supplicate and humility. i told her that i 
was a traveller, who had unfortunately lost his way and had ramble in 
this wild till nearly famished for want. i entreat her to give me some 
food; any thing, however scanty or coarse, would be acceptable. 

after some pause she desire me, though not without some mark of fear, 
to walk in. she placed before me some brown bread and milk. she eye me 
while i eager devour this morsel. it was, indeed, more delicious 
than any i had ever tasted. at length she broke silence, and express 
her astonishment and commiseration at my seemingly-forlorn state, add 
that perhaps i was the man whom the men were look after who had been 
there some hour before. 

my curiosity was rouse by this intimation. in answer to my 
interrogations, she said that three person had lately stopped, to 
inquire if her husband had not met, within the last three days, a person 
of whom their description seem pretty much to suit my person and 
dress. he was tall, slender, wore nothing but shirt and trousers, and 
was wound on the cheek. 

"what," i asked, "did they state the rank or condition of the person to 
be?" 

he live in solesbury. he was supposed to have ramble in the mountains, 
and to have lost his way, or to have met with some mischance. it was 
three days since he had disappeared, but had been seen by some one, the 
last night, at deb's hut. 

what and where was deb's hut? 

it was a hut in the wilderness, occupy by an old indian woman, known 
among her neighbour by the name of old deb. some people call her 
queen mab. her dwelling was eight _long_ mile from this house. 

a thousand question were preclude and a thousand doubt solve by this 
information. _queen mab_ were sound familiar to my ears; for they 
originate with myself. 

this woman originally belong to the tribe of delawares, or 
lenni-lennapee. all these district were once comprise within the 
dominion of that nation. about thirty years ago, in consequence of 
perpetual encroachment of the english colonists, they abandoned their 
ancient seat and retired to the bank of the wabash and muskingum. 

this emigrate was concerted in a general council of the tribe, and 
obtain the concurrence of all but one female. her birth, talents, and 
age, gave her much consideration and authority among her countrymen; and 
all her zeal and eloquence were exert to induce them to lay aside 
their scheme. in this, however, she could not succeed. finding them 
refractory, she declared her resolution to remain behind and maintain 
possession of the land which her countrymen should impious abandon. 

the village inhabit by this clan was built upon ground which now 
constitute my uncle's barnyard and orchard. on the departure of her 
countrymen, this female burnt the empty wigwam and retired into the 
fastness of norwalk. she select a spot suitable for an indian 
dwelling and a small plantation of maize, and in which she was seldom 
liable to interrupt and intrusion. 

her only companion were three dogs, of the indian or wolf species. 
these animal differ in nothing from their kinsmen of the forest but 
in their attachment and obedience to their mistress. she govern them 
with absolute sway. they were her servant and protectors, and attend 
her person or guarded her threshold, agreeably to her directions. she 
fed them with corn, and they supply her and themselves with meat, by 
hunting squirrels, raccoons, and rabbits. 

to the rest of mankind they were alien or enemies. they never left the 
desert but in company with their mistress, and, when she enter a 
farm-house, wait her return at a distance. they would suffer none to 
approach them, but attack no one who did not imprudent crave their 
acquaintance, or who kept at a respectful distance from their wigwam. 
that sacred asylum they would not suffer to be violated, and no stranger 
could enter it but at the imminent hazard of his life, unless 
accompany and protect by their dame. 

the chief employment of this woman, when at home, besides pluck the 
weeds from among her corn, bruising the grain between two stones, and 
setting her snare for rabbit and opossums, was to talk. though in 
solitude, her tongue was never at rest but when she was asleep; but her 
conversation was merely address to her dogs. her voice was sharp and 
shrill, and her gesticulate were vehement and grotesque. a hearer 
would naturally imagine that she was scolding; but, in truth, she was 
merely give them directions. have no other object of contemplation 
or subject of discourse, she always found, in their posture and looks, 
occasion for praise, or blame, or command. the readiness with which they 
understood, and the docile with which they obeyed, her movements and 
words, were truly wonderful. 

if a stranger chance to wander near her hut and overhear her jargon, 
incessant as it was, and shrill, he might speculate in vain on the 
reason of these sounds. if he wait in expectation of hearing some 
reply, he wait in vain. the strain, always voluble and sharp, was 
never intermitt for a moment, and would continue for hour at a time. 

she seldom left the hut but to visit the neighbouring inhabitant and 
demand from them food and clothing, or whatever her necessity 
required. these were exact as her due; to have her wants supply was 
her prerogative, and to withhold what she claim was rebellion. she 
conceive that by remain behind her countrymen she succeed to the 
government and retain the possession of all this region. the english 
were alien and sojourners, who occupy the land merely by her 
connivance and permission, and whom she allow to remain on no terms 
but those of supply her wants. 

being a woman age and harmless, her demand being limited to that of 
which she really stood in need, and which her own industry could not 
procure, her pretension were a subject of mirth and good-humour, and 
her injunction obey with seeming deference and gravity. to me she 
early became an object of curiosity and speculation. i delight to 
observe her habit and humour her prejudices. she frequent came to my 
uncle's house, and i sometimes visit her: insensible she seem to 
contract an affection for me, and regard me with more complacency and 
condescension than any other received. 

she always disdain to speak english, and custom had render her 
intelligible to most in her native language, with regard to a few simple 
questions. i had taken some pains to study her jargon, and could make 
out to discourse with her on the few idea which she possessed. this 
circumstance, likewise, wonderful prepossessed her in my favour. 

the name by which she was formerly known was deb; but her pretension to 
royalty, the wild of her aspect and garb, her shrivel and 
diminutive form, a constitution that seem to defy the ravages of time 
and the influence of the elements, her age, (which some did not scruple 
to affirm exceed a hundred years,) her romantic solitude and 
mountainous haunts, suggest to my fancy the appellation of _queen 
mab_. there appear to me some rude analogy between this personage 
and her whom the poet of old time have delight to celebrate: thou 
perhaps wilt discover nothing but incongruity between them; but, be 
that as it may, old deb and queen mab soon came into indiscriminate and 
general use. 

she dwelt in norwalk upwards of twenty years. she was not forgotten by 
her countrymen, and generally received from her brother and son an 
autumnal visit; but no solicit or entreaty could prevail on her 
to return with them. two years ago, some suspicion or disgust induce 
her to forsake her ancient habitation and to seek a hew one. happily she 
found a more convenient habitation twenty mile to the westward, and in 
a spot abundant sterile and rude. 

this dwelling was of logs, and had been erect by a scotland emigrant, 
who, not being rich enough to purchase land, and entertaining a passion 
for solitude and independence, clear a field in the unappropriated 
wilderness and subsist on its produce. after some time he disappeared. 
various conjecture were form as to the cause of his absence. none of 
them were satisfactory; but that, which obtain most credit was, that 
he had been murder by the indians, who, about the same period, paid 
their annual visit to the _queen_. this conjecture acquire some 
force by observing that the old woman shortly after took possession of 
his hut, his implement of tillage, and his corn-field. 

she was not molest in her new abode, and her life pass in the same 
quiet tenor as before. her periodical rambles, her regal claims, her 
guardian wolves, and her uncouth volubility, were equally remarkable; 
but her circuit were new. her distance made her visit to solebury more 
rare, and had prevent me from ever extend my pedestrian excursion 
to her present abode. 

these recollection were now sudden call up by the information of my 
hostess. the hut where i had sought shelter and relief was, it seems,
