the residence of queen mab. some fortunate occurrence had call her 
away during my visit. had she and her dogs been at home, i should have 
been set upon by these ferocious sentinels, and, before their dame could 
have interfered, have been, together with my helpless companion, mangle 
or killed. these animal never barked: i should have enter unaware of 
my danger, and my fate could scarcely have been avert by my fusil. 

her absence at this unseasonable hour was mysterious. it was now the 
time of year when her countrymen were accustomed to renew their visit. 
was there a league between her and the plunder whom i had 
encountered? 

but who were they by whom my footstep were so industrious traced? 
those whom i had seen at deb's hut were stranger to me, but the wound 
upon my face was known only to them. to this circumstance was now add 
my place of residence and name. i supposed them impress with the 
belief that i was dead; but this mistake must have speedy been 
rectified. revisite the spot, finding me gone, and obtain some 
intelligence of my former condition, they had institute a search after 
me. 

but what tidings were these? i was supposed to have been bewilder in 
the mountains, and three days were said to have pass since my 
disappearance. twelve hour had scarcely elapse since i emerge from 
the cavern. had two days and a half been consume in my subterranean 
prison? 

these reflection were quick supplant by others. i now gain a 
sufficient acquaintance with the region that was spread around me. i was 
in the midst of a vale included between ridge that gradual approach 
each other, and, when joined, were broken up into hollow and steeps, 
and, spread themselves over a circular space, assume the appellation 
of norwalk. this vale gradual widen as it tend to the westward, 
and was, in this place, ten or twelve mile in breadth. my devious 
footstep had brought me to the foot of the southern barrier. the outer 
basis of this was lave by the river; but, as it tend eastward, the 
mountain and river recede from each other, and one of the cultivable 
district lie between them was solesbury, my natal _township_. 
hither it was now my duty to return with the utmost expedition. 

there were two ways before me. one lay along the interior base of the 
hill, over a sterile and trackless space, and expose to the encounter 
of savages, some of whom might possibly be lurk here. the other was 
the well-frequented road on the outside and along the river, and which 
was to be gain by passing over this hill. the practicable of the 
passage was to be ascertain by inquiry made to my hostess. she 
pointed out a path that led to the rocky summit and down to the river's 
brink. the path was not easy to be kept in view or to be trodden, but it 
was undoubted to be prefer to any other. 

a route somewhat circuitous would terminate in the river-road. 
thenceforward the way to solesbury was level and direct; but the whole 
space which i had to traverse was not less than thirty miles. in six 
hour it would be night, and to perform the journey in that time would 
demand the agile bound of a leopard and the indefatigable sinew of 
an elk. 

my frame was in a miserable plight. my strength had been assail by 
anguish, and fear, and watchfulness, by toil, and abstinence, and 
wounds. still, however, some remnant was left; would it not enable me to 
reach my home by nightfall? i had delighted, from my childhood, in feat 
of agile and perseverance. in rove through the maze of thicket and 
precipices, i had put my energies, both moral and physical, frequent 
to the test. greater achievement than this had been performed, and i 
disdain to be outdone in perspicac by the lynx, in his sure-footed 
instinct by the roe, or in patience under hardship, and contention with 
fatigue, by the mohawk. i have ever aspire to transcend the rest of 
animal in all that is common to the rational and brute, as well as in 
all by which they are distinguished from each other. 




chapter xxi. 


i likewise burn with impatience to know the condition of my family, to 
dissipate at once their torment doubt and my own with regard to our 
mutual safety. the evil that i fear had befallen them was too enormous 
to allow me to repose in suspense, and my restless and ominous 
foreboding would be more intolerable than any hardship or toils to 
which i could possibly be subject during this journey. 

i was much refresh and invigorate by the food that i had taken, and 
by the rest of an hour. with this stock of recruit force i determined 
to scale the hill. after receiving minute directions, and, return 
many thanks for my hospitable entertainment, i set out. 

the path was indeed intricate, and deliberate attention was oblige to 
be exert in order to preserve it. hence my progress was slow than i 
wished. the first impulse was to fix my eye upon the summit, and to leap 
from crag to crag till i reach it; but this my experience had taught 
me was impracticable. it was only by winding through gullies, and 
coast precipice and bestride chasms, that i could hope finally to 
gain the top; and i was assured that by one way only was it possible to 
accomplish even this. 

an hour was spent in struggle with impediments, and i seem to have 
gain no way. hence a doubt was suggest whether i had not miss the 
true road. in this doubt i was confirmed by the difficulty which now 
grew up before me. the brooks, the angles, and the hollows, which my 
hostess had described, were not to be seen. instead of these, deep 
dells, more headlong torrents, and wider-gaping rifts, were incessant 
encountered. 

to return was as hopeless as to proceed. i console myself with thinking 
that the survey which my informant had made of the hill-side might prove 
inaccurate, and that, in spite of her predictions, the heights might be 
reach by other means than by those pointed out by her. i will not 
enumerate my toilsome expedients, my frequent disappointments, and my 
desperate exertions. suffice it to say that i gain the upper space not 
till the sun had dip beneath the horizon. 

my satisfaction at accomplish thus much was not small, and i hied, 
with renovate spirits, to the opposite brow. this prove to be a steep 
that could not be descended. the river flow at its foot. the opposite 
bank was five hundred yard distant, and was equally towering and steep 
as that on which i stood. appearances were adapt to persuade you that 
these rocks had formerly joined, but by some mighty effort of nature had 
been severed, that the stream might find way through the chasm. the 
channel, however, was encumber with asperities, over which the river 
fret and foam with thundering impetuosity. 

i ponder for a while on these stupendous scenes. they ravish my 
attention from consideration that related to myself; but this interval 
was snort, and i began to measure the descent, in order to ascertain the 
practicable of tread it. my survey terminate in bitter 
disappointment. i turn my eye successive eastward and westward. 
solesbury lay in the former direction, and thither i desire to go. i 
kept along the verge in this direction till i reach an impassable 
rift. beyond this i saw that the steep grew lower; but it was impossible 
to proceed farther. higher up the descent might be practicable, and, 
though more distant from solesbury, it was better to reach the road even 
at that distance than never to reach it. 

change my course, therefore, i explore the space above. the night 
was rapid advancing; the gray cloud gather in the southeast, and a 
chill blast, the usual attendant of a night in october, began to 
whistle among the pigmy cedar that scanty grew upon these heights. my 
progress would quick be arrest by darkness, and it behoove me to 
provide some place of shelter and repose. no recess better than a hollow 
in the rock present itself to my anxious scrutiny. 

meanwhile, i would not dismiss the hope of reach the road, which i 
saw some hundred feet below, winding along the edge of the river, before 
daylight should utterly fail. speedy these hope derive new vigour 
from meeting a ledge that irregular decline from the brow of the 
hill. it was wide enough to allow of cautious footing. on a similar 
stratum, or ledge, project still farther from the body of the hill, 
and close to the surface of the river, was the road. this stratum 
ascend from the level of the stream, while that on which i trod 
rapid descended. i hope that they would speedy be blended, or, at 
least, approach so near as to allow me to leap from one to the other 
without enormous hazard. 

this fond expectation was frustrated. presently i perceive that the 
ledge below began to descend, while that above began to tend upward and 
was quick terminate by the uppermost surface of the cliff. here it 
was needful to pause. i look over the brink, and considered whether i 
might not leap from my present station without endanger my limbs. the 
road into which i should fall was a rocky pavement far from being 
smooth. the descent could not be less than forty or fifty feet. such an 
attempt was, to the last degree, hazardous; but was it not better to 
risk my life by leap from this eminence than to remain and perish on 
the top of this inhospitable mountain? the toils which i had endure in 
reach this height appeared, to my panic-struck fancy, less easy to be 
borne again than death. 

i know not but that i should have finally resolve to leap, had not 
different view been suggest by observing that the outer edge of the 
road was, in like manner, the brow of a steep which terminate in the 
river. the surface of the road was twelve or fifteen feet above the 
level of the stream, which, in this spot, was still and smooth. hence i 
infer that the water was not of inconsiderable depth. to fall upon 
rocky points was, indeed, dangerous, but to plunge into water of 
sufficient depth, even from a height greater than that at which i now 
stood, especially to one to whom habit had render water almost as 
congenial an element as air, was scarcely attend with inconvenience. 
this expedient was easy and safe. twenty yard from this spot, the 
channel was shallow, and to gain the road from the stream was no 
difficult exploit. 

some disadvantages, however, attend this scheme. the water was smooth; 
but this might arise from some other cause than its depth. my gun, 
likewise, must be left behind me; and that was a loss to which i felt 
invincible repugnance. to let it fall upon the road would put it in my 
power to retrieve the possession, but it was likely to be irreparable 
injure by the fall. 

while muse upon this expedient, and weigh injury with benefits, 
the night closed upon me. i now considered that, should i emerge in 
safety from the stream, i should have many mile to travel before i 
could reach a house. my clothes meanwhile would be loaded with wet. i 
should be heart-pierced by the icy blast that now blew, and my wound 
and bruise would be chafe into insupportable pain. 

i reasoned likewise on the folly of impatience and the necessity of 
repose. by thus long continuance in one posture, my sinew began to 
stiffen, and my reluctance to make new exertion to increase. my brow 
were heavy, and i felt an irresistible propensity to sleep. i conclude 
to seek some shelter, and resign myself, my painful recollections, and 
my mournful presages, to sweet forgetfulness. for this end, i once more 
ascend to the surface of the cliff. i drag my weary feet forward, 
till i found somewhat that promise me the shelter that i sought. 

a cluster of cedar appeared, whose branch overarch a space that 
might be call a bower. it was a slight cavity, whose flooring was 
compose of loose stone and a few fade leaves blown from a distance 
and finding a temporary lodgment here. on one side was a rock, form a 
wall rugged and project above. at the bottom of the rock was a rift, 
somewhat resemble a coffin in shape, and not much large in 
dimensions. this rift terminated, on the opposite side of the rock, in 
an opening that was too small for the body of a man to pass. the 
distance between each entrance was twice the length of a man. 

this bower was open to the southeast, whence the gale now blew. it 
therefore imperfect afford the shelter of which i stood in need; 
but it was the best that the place and the time afforded. to stop the 
small entrance of the cavity with a stone, and to heap before the 
other branch lop from the tree with my hatchet, might somewhat 
contribute to my comfort. 

this was done, and, thrust myself into this recess as far as i was 
able, i prepared for repose. it might have been reasonably suspect to 
be the den of rattlesnake or panthers; but my late contention with 
superior danger and more formidable enemy made me reckless of these. 
but another inconvenience remained. in spite of my precautions, my 
motionless posture and slender covering expose me so much to the cold 
that i could not sleep. 

the air appear to have sudden assume the temperature of midwinter. 
in a short time, my extremities were benumbed, and my limb shiver and 
ache as if i had been seize by an ague. my bed likewise was dank and 
uneven, and the posture i was oblige to assume, unnatural and painful. 
it was evident that my purpose could not be answer by remain here. 

i therefore crept forth, and began to reflect upon the possibility of 
continue my journey. motion was the only thing that could keep me from 
freezing, and my frame was in that state which allow me to take no 
repose in the absence of warmth, since warmth was indispensable. it now 
occur to me to ask whether it were not possible to kindle a fire. 

sticks and leaves were at hand. my hatchet and a pebble would enable me 
to extract a spark. from this, by suitable care and perseverance, i 
might finally procure sufficient fire to give me comfort and ease, and 
even enable me to sleep. this boon was delicious, and i felt as if i 
were unable to support a long deprivation of it. 

i proceed to execute this scheme. i took the driest leaves, and 
endeavour to use them as tinder; but the driest leaves were moisten 
by the dews. they were only to be found in the hollows, in some of which 
were pools of water and other were dank. i was not speedy 
discouraged; but my repeated attempt failed, and i was finally 
compel to relinquish this expedient. 

all that now remain was to wander forth and keep myself in motion till 
the morning. the night was likely to prove tempestuous and long. the 
gale seem freight with ice, and act upon my body like the points 
of a thousand needles. there was no remedy, and i muster my patience 
to endure it. 

i return again to the brow of the hill. i range along it till i 
reach a place where the descent was perpendicular, and, in consequence 
of afford no sustenance to tree or bushes, was nearly smooth and 
bare. there was no road to be seen; and this circumstance, add to the 
sound which the ripple current produced, afford me some knowledge 
of my situation. 

the ledge along which the road was conduct disappear near this spot. 
the opposite side of the chasm through which flow the river 
approach near to each other, in the form of jut promontories. i 
now stood upon the verge of that on the northern side. the water 
flow at the foot, but, for the space of ten or twelve feet from the 
rock, was so shallow as to permit the traveller and his horse to wade 
through it, and thus to regain the road which the recede precipice had 
allow to be continue on the farther side. 

i knew the nature and dimensions of this ford. i knew that, at a few 
yard from the rock, the channel was of great depth. to leap into it, in 
this place, was a less dangerous exploit than at the spot where i had 
formerly been tempt to leap. there i was unacquainted with the depth, 
but here i knew it to be considerable. still, there was some ground of 
hesitation and fear. my present station was loftier, and how deep i 
might sink into this gulf, how far the fall and the concussion would 
bereave me of my presence of mind, i could not determine. this 
hesitation vanished, and, place my tomahawk and fusil upon the ground, 
i prepared to leap. 

this purpose was suspended, in the moment of its execution, by a faint 
sound, heard from the quarter whence i had come. it was the warning of 
men, but had nothing in common with those which i had been accustomed to 
hear. it was not the howling of a wolf or the yell of a panther. 
these had often been overheard by night during my last year's excursion 
to the lakes. my fear whisper that this was the vociferation of a 
savage. 

i was unacquainted with the number of the enemy who had adventure 
into this district. whether those whom i had encounter at _deb's 
hut_ were of that band whom i had met with in the cavern, was merely 
a topic of conjecture. there might be a half-score of troops, equally 
numerous, spread over the wilderness, and the signal i had just heard 
might betoken the approach of one of these. yet by what means they 
should gain this nook, and what prey they expect to discover, were not 
easily conceived. 

the sounds, somewhat diversified, near and rising from different 
quarters, were again heard. my doubt and apprehension were increased. 
what expedient to adopt for my own safety was a subject of rapid 
meditation:--whether to remain stretch upon the ground or to rise and 
go forward. was it likely the enemy would coast along the edge of the 
steep? would they ramble hither to look upon the ample scene which 
spread on all side around the base of this rocky pinnacle? in that 
case, how should i conduct myself? my arms were ready for use. could i 
not elude the necessity of shed more blood? could i not anticipate 
their assault by casting myself without delay into the stream? 

the sense of danger demand more attention to be paid to external 
object than to the motive by which my future conduct should be 
influenced. my post was on a circular prefecture, in some degree 
detached from the body of the hill, the brow of which continue in a 
straight line, uninterrupted by this projecture, which was somewhat 
higher than the continue summit of the ridge. this line ran at the 
distance of a few pace from my post. object moving along this line 
could merely be perceive to move, in the present obscurity. 

my scrutiny was entire direct to this quarter. presently the 
tread of many feet was heard, and several figures were discovered, 
following each other in that straight and regular succession which is 
peculiar to the indians. they kept along the brow of the hill join 
the promontory. i distinct marked seven figures in succession. 

my resolution was formed. should any one cast his eye hither, suspect or 
discover an enemy, and rush towards me, i determined to start upon my 
feet, fire on my foe as he advanced, throw my piece on the ground, and 
then leap into the river. 

happily, they pass unobservant and in silence. i remain in the same 
posture for several minutes. at length, just as my alarm began to 
subside, the halloos, before heard, arose, and from the same quarter as 
before. this convinced me that my peril were not at an end. this now 
appear to be merely the vanguard, and would speedy be follow by 
others, against whom the same caution was necessary to be taken. 

my eye, anxious bent the only way by which any one could approach, now 
discern a figure, which was indubitable that of a man armed. none 
other appear in company; but doubtless other were near. he 
approached, stood still, and appear to gaze steadfast at the spot 
where i lay. 

the optics of a _lenni-lennapee_ i knew to be far keen than my 
own. a log or a couch fawn would never be mistaken for a man, nor a 
man for a couch fawn or a log. not only a human being would be 
instantly detected, but a decision be unerring made whether it wrere 
friend or foe. that my prostrate body was the object on which the 
attention of this vigilant and steadfast gaze was fixed could not be

a possibility to stand upon that i was not recognised. my fate therefore 
was still in suspense. 

this interval was momentary. i marked a movement, which my fear 
instantly interpret to be that of level a gun at my head. this 
action was sufficient conformable to my prognostics. supposing me to 
be detected, there was no need for him to change his post. aim might be 
too fatally taken, and his prey be secured, from the distance at which 
he now stood. 

these image glance upon my thought, and put an end to my suspense. a 
single effort placed me on my feet. i fire with a precipitation that 
preclude the certainty of hit my mark, drop my piece upon the

the surface, and sunk in a moment to the bottom. 

plunge endlong into the water, the impetus create by my fall from 
such a height would be slow resist by this dense element. had the 
depth been less, its resistance would not perhaps have hinder me from 
being mortally injure against the rocky bottom. had the depth been 
greater, time enough would not have been allow me to regain the 
surface. had i fallen on my side, i should have been bereft of life or 
sensibility by the shock which my frame would have received. as it was, 
my fate was suspend on a thread. to have lost my presence of mind, to 
have forborne to counteract my sinking, for an instant, after i had 
reach the water, would have made all exertion to regain the air 
fruitless. to so fortunate a concurrence of event was thy friend 
indebted for his safety! 

yet i only emerge from the gulf to encounter new perils. scarcely had i 
raise my head above the surface, and inhale the vital breath, when 
twenty shot were aim at me from the precipice above. a shower of 
bullet fell upon the water. some of them did not fall farther than two 
inch from my head. i had not been aware of this new danger, and, now 
that it assail me, continue gasp the air and flounder at 
random. the means of elude it did not readily occur. my case seem 
desperate, and all caution was dismissed. 

this state of discomfit surprise quick disappeared. i made myself 
acquainted, at a glance, with the position of surrounding objects. i 
conceive that the opposite bank of the river would afford me most 
security, and thither i tend with all the expedition in my power. 

meanwhile, my safety depend on elude the bullet that continue 
incessant to strike the water at an arm's-length from my body. for 
this end i plunge beneath the surface, and only rose to inhale fresh 
air. presently the fire ceased, the flash that lately illuminate 
the bank disappeared, and a certain bustle and murmur of confuse voice 
gave place to solitude and silence. 




chapter xxii. 


i reach without difficulty the opposite bank, but the steep was 
inaccessible. i swam along the edge in hope of meeting with some 
projection or recess where i might, at least, rest my weary limbs, and, 
if it were necessary to recross the river, to lay in a stock of 
recruit spirits and strength for that purpose. i trust that the 
water would speedy become shoal, or that the steep would afford rest 
to my feet. in both these hope i was disappointed. 

there is no one to whom i would yield the superior in swimming; but 
my strength, like that of other human beings, had its limits. my 
previous fatigues had been enormous, and my clothes, heavy with 
moisture, greatly encumber and retarded my movements. i had propose 
to free myself from this imprisonment; but i foresaw the inconvenience 
of wandering over this scene in absolute nakedness, and was willing 
therefore, at whatever hazard, to retain them. i continue to struggle 
with the current and to search for the means of scale the steeps. my 
search was fruitless, and i began to meditate the recross of the 
river. 

surely my fate has never been paralleled! where was this series of 
hardship and peril to end? no soon was one calamity eluded, than i 
was beset by another. i had emerge from abhor dark in the heart 
of the earth, only to endure the extremities of famine and encounter the 
fang of a wild beast. from these i was deliver only to be thrown into 
the midst of savages, to wage an endless and hopeless war with adept in 
killing, with appetite that long to feast upon my bowels and to quaff 
my heart's blood. from these likewise was i rescued, but merely to 
perish in the gulf of the river, to welter on unvisited shores, or to 
be wash far away from curiosity or pity. 

formerly water was not only my field of sport but my sofa and my bed. i 
could float for hour on its surface, enjoy its delicious cool, 
almost without the expense of the slightest motion. it was an element as 
fitted for repose as for exercise; but now the buoyant spirit seem to 
have flown. my muscle were shrunk, the air and water were equally 
congealed, and my most vehement exertion were requisite to sustain me 
on the surface. 

at first i had move along with my wonted celerity and ease, but quick 
my forces were exhausted. my pant and effort were augmented, and i 
saw that to cross the river again was impracticable. i must continue, 
therefore, to search out some accessible spot in the bank along which i 
was swimming. 

each moment diminish my stock of strength, and it behoove me to make 
good my footing before another minute should escape. i continue to 
swim, to survey the bank, and to make ineffectual attempt to grasp the 
rock. the shrub which grew upon it would not uphold me, and the 
fragment which, for a moment, inspired me with hope, crumble away as 
soon as they were touched. 

at length i notice a pine which was rooted in a crevice near the water. 
the trunk, or any part of the root, was beyond my reach; but i trust 
that i could catch hold of the branch which hung lowest, and that, when 
caught, it would assist me in gain the trunk, and thus deliver me 
from the death which could not be otherwise averted. 

the attempt was arduous. had it been made when i first reach the bank, 
no difficulty had attend it; but now to throw myself some feet above 
the surface could scarcely be expect from one whose utmost effort 
seem to be demand to keep him from sinking. yet this exploit, 
arduous as it was, was attempt and accomplished. happily the twig 
were strong enough to sustain my weight till i caught at other branch 
and finally placed myself upon the trunk. 

this danger was now past; but i admitted the conviction that others, no 
less formidable, remain to be encountered, and that my ultimate 
destiny was death. i look upward. new effort might enable me to gain 
the summit of this steep, but perhaps i should thus be placed merely in 
the situation from which i had just been delivered. it was of little 
moment whether the scene of my imprison was a dungeon not to be 
broken, or a summit from which descent was impossible. 

the river, indeed, sever me from a road which was level and safe, but 
my recent danger were remember only to make me shudder at the thought 
of incur them a second time by attempt to cross it. i blush at 
the recollection of this cowardice. it was little akin to the spirit 
which i had recently displayed. it was, indeed, an alien to my bosom, 
and was quick supplant by intrepid and perseverance. 

i proceed to mount the hill. from root to root, and from branch to 
branch, lay my journey. it was finished, and i sat down upon the highest 
brow to meditate on future trials. no road lay along this side of the 
river. it was rugged and sterile, and farm were sparing disperse 
over it. to reach one of these was now the object of my wishes. i had 
not lost the desire of reach solesbury before morning, but my wet 
clothes and the cold of the night seem to have bereaved me of the 
power. 

i traverse this summit, keeping the river on my right hand. happily, 
its declination and ascent were by no means difficult, and i was 
cheered, in the midst of my vexations, by observing that every mile 
brought me near to my uncle's dwelling. meanwhile i anxious look 
for some token of a habitation. these at length present themselves. a 
wild heath, whistle over by october blasts, meagre adorn with the 
dry stalk of scent shrub and the bald head of the sapless mullein, 
was succeed by a fence field and a corn-stack. the dwelling to which 
these belong was eager sought. 

i was not surprise that all voice were still and all lights 
extinguished, for this was the hour of repose. have reach a piazza 
before the house, i paused. whether, at this drowsy time, to knock for 
admission, to alarm the peaceful tenant and take from them the rest 
which their daily toils and their rural innocence had made so sweet, or 
to retire to what shelter a haystack or barn could afford, was the theme 
of my deliberations. 

meanwhile, i look up at the house. it was the model of cleanliness and 
comfort. it was built of wood; but the material had undergone the 
plane, as well as the axe and the saw. it was paint white, and the 
window not only had sashes, but these sash were supplied, contrary to 
custom, with glass. in most case the aperture where glass should be is 
stuff with an old hat or a petticoat. the door had not only all its 
parts entire, but was embellish with moulding and a pediment. i 
gather from these token that this was the abode not only of rural 
competence and innocence, but of some being raise by education and 
fortune above the intellectual mediocrity of clowns. 

methought i could claim consangu with such beings. not to share their 
charity and kindness would be inflict as well as receiving injury. 
the trouble of afford shelter, and warmth, and wholesome diet, to a 
wretch destitute as i was, would be eager sought by them. 

still, i was unwill to disturb them. i bethought myself that their 
kitchen might be entered, and all that my necessity require be 
obtain without interrupt their slumber. i need nothing but the 
warmth which their kitchen-hearth would afford. stretch upon the 
bricks, i might dry my clothes, and perhaps enjoy some unmolested sleep, 
in spite of presage of ill and the horrid remembrance of what i had 
perform and endured. i believe that nature would afford a short 
respite to my cares. 

i went to the door of what appear to be a kitchen. the door was wide 
open. this circumstance portend evil. though it be not customary to 
lock or to bolt, it is still less usual to have entrance unclosed. i 
enter with suspicious steps, and saw enough to confirm my 
apprehensions. several pieces of wood, half burned, lay in the midst of 
the floor. they appear to have been remove hither from the chimney, 
doubtless with a view to set fire to the whole building. 

the fire had made some progress on the floor, but had been seasonable 
extinguish by pailful of water thrown upon it. the floor was still 
deluge with wet: the pail, not empty of all its contents, stood upon 
the hearth. the earthen vessel and plates, whose proper place was the 
dresser, were scattered in fragment in all parts of the room. i look 
around me for some one to explain this scene, but no one appeared. 

the last spark of fire was put out, so that, had my curiosity been idle, 
my purpose could not be accomplished. to retire from this scene, neither 
curiosity nor benevolence would permit. that some mortal injury had been 
intended was apparent. what greater mischief had befallen, or whether 
greater might not, by my interposition, be averted, could only be 
ascertain by penetrating farther into the house. i open a door on 
one side which led to the main body of the building and enter to a 
bed-chamber. i stood at the entrance and knocked, but no one answer my 
signals. 

the sky was not total clouded, so that some light pervade the room. i 
saw that a bed stood in the corner, but whether occupy or not its 
curtains hinder me from judging. i stood in suspense a few minutes, 
when a motion in the bed show me that some one was there. i knock 
again, but withdrew to the outside of the door. this rouse the sleeper, 
who, half groaning, and puff the air through his nostrils, grumble 
out, in the hoarsest voice that i ever heard, and in a tone of surly 
impatience, "who is there?" 

i hesitate for an answer; but the voice instantly continued, in the 
manner of one half asleep and enrage at being disturbed, "is't you, 
peg? damn ye, stay away, now! i tell ye, stay away, or, by god, i will

coherence or distinctness. 

these were the accent of drunkenness, and denote a wild and ruffian 
life. they were little in unison with the external appearances of the 
mansion, and blasted all the hope i had form of meeting under this 
roof with gentle and hospitality. to talk with this being, to 
attempt to reason him into humanity and soberness, was useless. i was at 
a loss in what manner to address him, or whether it was proper to 
maintain any parley. meanwhile, my silence was supply by the 
suggestion of his own distempered fancy. "ay," said he; "ye will, will 
ye? well, come on; let's see who's the better at the oak stick. if i 
part with ye before i have bare your bones!--i'll teach ye to be always 
dip in my dish, ye devil's dam ye." 

so saying, he tumble out of bed. at the first step, he struck his head 
against the bedpost, but, setting himself upright, he stagger towards 
the spot where i stood. some new obstacle occurred. he stumble and fell 
at his length upon the floor. 

to encounter or expostulate with a man in this state was plainly absurd. 
i turn and issue forth, with an ache heart, into the court before 
the house. the misery which a debauch husband or father inflict 
upon all whom their evil destiny ally to him were picture by my 
fancy, and wrung from me tear of anguish, these images, however,

remain but to seek the barn and find a covering and a bed of straw. 

i had scarcely set foot within the barnyard when i heard a sound as of 
the crying of an infant. it appear to issue from the barn. i 
approach soft and listen at the door. the cries of the babe 
continued, but were accompany by the entreaty of a nurse or a mother 
to be quiet. these entreaty were mingle with heart-breaking sobs, and 
exclamation of, "ah, me, my babe! canst thou not sleep and afford thy 
unhappy mother some peace? thou art cold, and i have not sufficient 
warmth to cherish thee! what will become of us? thy delude father care 
not if we both perish." 

a glimpse of the true nature of the scene seem to be impart by these

name selby. he was an age person, who united science and taste to the 
simple and laborious habit of a husbandman. he had a son who reside 
several years in europe, but on the death of his father return home, 
accompany by a wife. he had succeed to the occupation of the farm, 
but rumour had whisper many tale to the disadvantage of his morals. 
his wife was affirm to be of delicate and polish manners, and much 
unlike her companion. 

it now occur to me that this was the dwelling of the selbys, and i

suggest a remedy for this distress, was not in my power. to have sought 
an interview would be merely to excite her terror and alarm her 
delicacy, without contribute to alleviate her calamity. here, then,

great distance: the path that led from the spot where i stood, through a

i was anxious to open the gate without noise, but i could not succeed. 
some creak of its hinge was unavoidable produced, which i fear

perplexities. this inconvenience was irremediable. i therefore closed

quiet and remote habitation had been visited, in their destructive 
progress, by the indians. the girl had been slain by them, and her 
scalp, according to their savage custom, had been torn away to be 
preserve as a trophy. 

the fire which had been kindle on the kitchen-floor tva now 
remembered, and corroborate the inference which were drawn from this 
spectacle. and yet that the mischief had been thus limited, that the

in consequence of these reflections, i proceed with more caution. i 
look with suspicious glance before and on either side of me. i now

previous awakened, and my fancy instantly figured to itself an armed

passenger. 

at first i was prompt to fly, but a second thought show me that i 
had already approach near enough to be endangered. notwithstanding my

conjecture was easily supposed. what i saw might be a log, or it might 
be another victim to savage ferocity. this track was that which my 
safety require me to pursue. to turn aside or go back would be merely 
to bewilder myself anew. 

urge by these motives, i went nearer, and at last was close enough to 
perceive that the figure was human. he lay upon his face. near his right 
hand was a musket, unclenched. this circumstance, his deathlike 
attitude, and the garb and ornament of an indian, made me readily 
suspect the nature and cause of this catastrophe. here the invade had 
been encounter and repulsed, and one at least of their number had been 
left upon the field. 

i was weary of contemplate these rueful objects. custom, likewise, 
even in so short a period, had inure me to spectacles of horror. i was 
grown callous and immovable. i stay not to ponder on the scene, but, 
snatch the musket, which was now without an owner, and which might be 
indispensable to my defence, i hasten into the wood. on this side the 
meadow was skirt by a forest; but a beaten road led into it, and might 
therefore be attempt without danger. 




chapter xxiii. 


the road was intricate and long. it seem design to pervade the 
forest in every possible direction. i frequent notice cut wood pile 
in heap upon either side, and rejoice in these token that the 
residence of man was near. at length i reach a second fence, which 
prove to be the boundary of a road still more frequented. i pursue 
this, and presently beheld before me the river and its opposite 
barriers. 

this object afford me some knowledge of my situation. there was a ford 
over which traveller use to pass, and in which the road that i was now 
pursue terminated. the stream was rapid and tumultuous, but in this 
place did not rise higher than the shoulders. on the opposite side was a 
highway, passable by horse and men, though not by carriages, and which 
led into the midst of solesbury. should i not rush into the stream, and 
still aim at reach my uncle's house before morning? why should i 
delay? 

thirty hour of incessant watchful and toil, of enormous effort and 
perils, precede and accompany by abstinence and wounds, were enough 
to annihilate the strength and courage of ordinary men. in the course of 
them, i had frequent believe myself to have reach the verge beyond 
which my force would not carry me; but experience as frequent 
demonstrate my error. though many mile were yet to be traversed, 
though my clothes were once more to be drench and loaded with 
moisture, though every hour seem to add somewhat to the keen of 
the blast, yet how should i know, but by trial, whether my stock of 
energy was not sufficient for this last exploit? 

my resolution to proceed was nearly formed, when the figure of a man

hard by this ford live a man by name bisset, of whom i had slight 
knowledge. he tend his two hundred acre with a plod and 
money-doting spirit, while his son overlook a grist-mill on the river. 
he was a creature of gain, coarse and harmless. the man whom i saw before 
me might be he, or some one belong to his family. being armed for

i therefore called. the figure stop and answer me without surly 
or anger. the voice was unlike that of bisset, but this person's 
information i believe would be of some service. 

coming up to him, he prove to be a clown belong to bisset's 
habitation. his panic and surprise on seeing me made him aghast. in my 
present garb i should not have easily been recognise by my nearest 
kinsman, and much less easily by one who had seldom met me. 

it may be easily conceive that my thoughts, when allow to wander from 
the object before me, were torment with foreboding and inquietude

full extent of it was still uncertain. i desire and dread to discover

i could deal only in circu and hints. i shudder while i wait

had not indians, i asked, been lately seen in this neighbourhood? were 
they not suspect of hostile designs? had they not already committed

observe or any devastate been committed? above the ford or below it? 
at what distance from the river? 

when his attention could be withdrawn from my person and bestow upon

indians, and that party from solesbury and chetasco were out in

house in solesbury had been rifle and burnt on the night before the

these tidings were a dreadful confirmation of my fears. there scarcely

he answer that he had not heard the name of the owner. he was a 
stranger to the people on the other side of the river. 

were any of the inhabitant murdered? 

yes; all that were at home, except a girl whom they carry off. some 
said that the girl had been retaken. 

what was the name? was it huntly? 

huntly? yes. no. he did not know. he had forgotten. 

i fixed my eyes upon the ground. an interval of gloomy meditation 
succeeded. all was lost! all for whose sake i had desire to live had 
perish by the hand of these assassins! that dear home, the scene of 
my sportive childhood, of my studies, labours, and recreations, was 
ravage by fire and the sword,--was reduce to a frightful ruin! 

not only all that embellish and endear existence was destroyed, but 
the means of subsistence itself. thou knowest that my sister and i were 
dependant on the bounty of our uncle. his death would make way for the 
succession of his son, a man fraught with envy and malignity, who always 
testify a mortal hatred to us, merely because we enjoy the 
protection of his father. the ground which furnish me with bread was 
now become the property of one who, if he could have done it with 
security, would gladly have mingle poison with my food. 

all that my imagination or my heart regard as of value had likewise

existence whom i loved, my very clothing, were involved in

calamity? 

but did not he say that one had escaped? the only female in the family 
were my sisters. one of these had been reserved for a fate worse than 
death; to gratify the innate and insatiable cruelty of savages, by 
suffering all the torment their invention can suggest, or to linger out 
years of weary bondage and unintermitt hardship in the bosom of the 
wilderness. to restore her to liberty, to cherish this last survivor of

but soft! had not rumour whisper that the captive was retaken? oh! who

the path was open and direct. my new motive would have trample upon

through a gate i enter an avenue of tall oaks, that led to the house.

few, perhaps, among mankind, have undergone vicissitudes of peril and
