have already related." 

during my tale the features of sarsefield betoken the deepest 
attention. his eye stray not a moment from my face. all my peril and 
foreboding were fresh in my remembrance: they had scarcely gone by; 
their skirts, so to speak, were still visible. no wonder that my 
eloquence was vivid and pathetic; that i portray the past as if it 
were the present scene; and that not my tongue only, but every muscle 
and limb, spoke. 

when i had finished my relation, sarsefield sank into thoughtfulness. 
from this, after a time, he recovered, and said, "your tale, huntly, is 
true; yet, did i not see you before me, were i not acquaint with the 
artless and rectitude of your character, and, above all, had not my 
own experience, during the last three days, confirmed every incident, i 
should question its truth. you have ample gratify my curiosity, and 
deserve that your own should be gratify as fully. listen to me. 

"much has happen since we parted, which shall not be now mentioned. i 
promise to inform you of my welfare by letter, and did not fail to 
write; but whether my letters were received, or any were written by you 
in return, or if written were ever transmitted, i cannot tell: none were 
ever received. 

"some days since, i arrived, in company with a lady who is my wife, in 
america. you have never been forgotten by me. i knew your situation to 
be little in agreement with your wishes, and one of the benefit which 
fortune has lately confer upon me is the power of snatch you from 
a life of labour and obscurity, whose goods, scanty as they are, were 
transient and precarious, and afford you the suitable leisure and 
means of intellectual gratification and improvement. 

"your silence made me entertain some doubt concerning your welfare, and 
even your existence. to solve these doubts, i hasten to solesbury. 
some delay upon the road hinder me from accomplish my journey by 
daylight. it was night before i enter the norwalk path; but my ancient 
ramble with you made me familiar with it, and i was not afraid of being 
obstruct or bewildered. 

"just as i gain the southern outlet, i spy a passenger on foot, 
coming towards me with a quick pace. the incident was of no moment; and 
yet the time of night, the seeming expedition of the walker, 
recollection of the maze and obstacle which he was go to encounter, 
and a vague conjecture that perhaps he was unacquainted with the 
difficulty that await him, made me eye him with attention as he 
passed. 

"he came near, and i thought i recognise a friend in this traveller. 
the form, the gesture, the stature, bore a powerful resemblance to those 
of edgar huntly. this resemblance was so strong, that i stopped, and, 
after he had gone by, call him by your name. that no notice was taken 
of my call prove that the person was mistaken; but, even though it were 
another, that he should not even hesitate or turn at a summons which he 
could not but perceive to be addressed, though erroneously, to him, was 
the source of some surprise. i did not repeat my call, but proceed on 
my way. 

"all had retired to repose in your uncle's dwelling. i did not scruple 
to rouse them, and was received with affectionate and joyous greetings. 
that you allow your uncle to rise before you was a new topic of 
reflection. to my inquiry concerning you, answer were made that 
accord with my wishes. i was told that you were in good health and 
were then in bed. that you had not heard and risen at my knock was 
mention with surprise; but your uncle account for your indolence by 
saying that during the last week you had fatigue yourself by rambling, 
night and day, in search of some maniac or visionary who was supposed to 
have retreat into norwalk. 

"i insist upon awakening you myself. i anticipate the effect of this 
sudden and unlooked-for meeting with some emotion of pride as well as 
of pleasure. to find, in opening your eyes, your old preceptor standing 
by your bedside and gaze in your face, would place you, i conceived, 
in an affecting situation. 

"your chamber-door was open, but your bed was empty. your uncle and 
sister were made acquaint with this circumstance. their surprise gave 
way to conjecture that your restless and romantic spirit had tempt 
you from your repose, that you had ramble abroad on some fantastic 
errand, and would probably return before the dawn. i willing 
acquiesce in this opinion, and, my feelings being too thorough 
arouse to allow me to sleep, i took possession of your chamber and 
patient await your return. 

"the morning returned, but hunt made not his appearance. your uncle 
became somewhat uneasy at this unseasonable absence. much speculation 
and inquiry as to the possible reason of your flight was made. in my 
survey of your chamber, i noted that only part of your clothing remain 
beside your bed. coat, hat, stocking and shoe lay upon the spot where 
they had probably been thrown when you had disrobe yourself; but the 
pantaloons, which, according to mr. huntly's report, complete your 
dress, were nowhere to be found. that you should go forth on so cold a 
night so slender apparelled, was almost incredible. your reason or 
your senses had desert you, before so rash an action could be 
meditated. 

"i now remember the person i had met in norwalk. his resemblance to 
your figure, his garb, which want hat, coat, stocking and shoes, and 
your absence from your bed at that hour, were remarkable coincidences: 
but why did you disregard my call? your name, utter by a voice that 
could not be unknown, was surely sufficient to arrest your steps. 

"each hour add to the impatience of your friends. to their 
recollection and conjecture i listen with a view to extract from 
them some solution of this mystery. at length a story was allude to of 
some one who, on the preceding night, had been heard walking in the long 
room: to this was add the tale of your anxiety and wonders 
occasion by the loss of certain manuscripts. 

"while ruminate upon these incidents, and endeavour to extract from 
this intelligence a clue explanatory of your present situation, a single 
word, casual drop by your uncle, instantly illuminate my dark 
and dispel my doubts.--'after all,' said the old man, 'ten to one but 
edgar himself was the man whom we heard walking, but the lad was asleep, 
and knew not what he was about.' 

"'surely,' said i, 'this inference is just. his manuscript could not be 
remove by any hand but his own, since the rest of mankind were 
unacquainted not only with the place of their concealment, but with 
their existence. none but a man insane or asleep would wander forth so 
slightly dressed, and none but a sleeper would have disregard my 
calls.' this conclusion was generally adopted; but it gave birth in my 
mind to infinite inquietudes. you had rove into norwalk, a scene of 
inequalities, of prominence and pits, among which, thus destitute of 
the guidance of your senses, you could scarcely fail to be destroyed, 
or, at least, irretrievable bewildered. i paint to myself the danger 
to which you were subjected. your careless feet would bear you into some 
whirlpool or to the edge of some precipice; some internal revolution or 
outward shock would recall you to consciousness at some perilous moment. 
surprise and fear would disable you from taking seasonable or suitable 
precautions, and your destruction be made sure. 

"the lapse of every new hour, without bring tidings of your state, 
enhance these fears. at length the propriety of searching for you 
occurred; mr. hunt and i determined to set out upon this pursuit, as 
well as to commission others. a plan was laid by which every accessible 
part of norwalk, the wilderness beyond the flat of solesbury, and the 
valley of chetasco, should be traverse and explored. 

"scarcely had we equip ourselves for this expedition, when a 
messenger arrived, who brought the disastrous news of indian being seen 
within these precincts, and on the last night a farmer was shot in his 
fields, a dwelling in chetasco was burnt to the ground, and its 
inhabitant murder or made captives. rumour and inquiry had been busy, 
and a plausible conjecture had been form as to the course and number 
of the enemies. they were said to be divide into bands, and to amount 
in the whole to thirty or forty warriors. this messenger had come to 
warn us of danger which might impend, and to summon us to join in the 
pursuit and extirpate of these detest foes. 

"your uncle, whose alacrity and vigour age had not abated, eager 
engaged in this scheme. i was not averse to contribute my effort to an 
end like this. the road which we had previous design to take, in 
search of my fugitive pupil, was the same by which we must trace or 
intercept the retreat of the savages. thus two purposes, equally 
momentous, would be answer by the same means. 

"mr. hunt armed himself with your fusil; inglefield supply me with a 
gun. during our absence the dwelling was closed and locked, and your 
sister placed under the protection of inglefield, whose age and pacific 
sentiment unfit him for arduous and sanguinary enterprises. a troop 
of rustic was collected, half of whom remain to traverse solesbury, 
and the other, whom mr. hunt and i accompanied, hasten to chetasco." 




chapter xxv. 


"it was noonday before we reach the theatre of action. fear and 
revenge combine to make the people of chetasco diligent and zealous in 
their own defence. the havoc already committed had been mournful. to 
prevent a repetition of the same calamities, they resolve to hunt out 
the hostile footstep and exact a merciless retribution. 

"it was likely that the enemy, on the approach of day, had withdrawn 
from the valley and conceal themselves in the thicket between the 
parallel ridge of the mountain. this space, which, according to the 
object with which it is compared, is either a vale or the top of a hill, 
was obscure and desolate. it was undoubted the avenue by which the 
robber had issue forth, and by which they would escape to the ohio. 
here they might still remain, intend to emerge from their concealment 
on the next night and perpetrate new horrors. 

"a certain distribution was made of our number, so as to move in all 
directions at the same time. i will not dwell upon particulars. it will 
suffice to say that keen eyes and indefatigable feet brought us at last 
to the presence of the largest number of these marauders. seven of them 
were slain by the edge of a brook, where they sat wholly unconscious of 
the danger which hung over them. five escaped, and one of these secure 
his retreat by wrest your fusil from your uncle and shoot him 
dead. before our companion could be rescue or revenged, the assassin, 
with the remnant of the troop, disappeared, and bore away with him the 
fusil as a trophy of his victory. 

"this disaster was deplored, not only on account of that life which had 
thus been sacrificed, but because a sagacious guide and intrepid leader 
was lost. his acquaintance with the habit of the indians, and his 
experience in their wars, made him trace their footstep with more 
certainty than any of his associates. 

"the pursuit was still continued, and party were so station that the 
escape of the enemy was difficult, if not impossible. our search was 
unremitted, but, during twelve or fourteen hours, unsuccessful. queen 
mab did not elude all suspicion. her hut was visit by different 
parties, but the old woman and her dogs had disappeared. 

"meanwhile your situation was not forgotten. every one was charged to 
explore your footstep as well as those of the savages; but this search 
was no less unsuccessful than the former. none had heard of you or seen 
you. 

"this continue till midnight. three of us made a pause at a brook, and 
intended to repair our fatigues by a respite of a few hours; but 
scarcely had we stretch ourselves on the ground when we were alarm 
by a shot which seem to have been fire at a short distance. we 
start on our feet and consult with each other on the measure to be 
taken. a second, a third, and a fourth shot, from the same quarter, 
excited our attention anew. mab's hut was known to stand at the distance 
and in the direction of this sound, and thither we resolve to repair. 

"this was done with speed, but with the utmost circumspection. we 
shortly gain the road that leads near this hut, and at length gain a 
view of the building. many person were discovered, in a sort of 
bustle inactivity, before the hut. they were easily distinguished to 
be friends, and were therefore approach without scruple. 

"the object that present themselves to a near view were five body 
stretch upon the ground. three of them were savages. the fourth was a 
girl, who, though alive, seem to have received a mortal wound. the 
fifth, breathless and mangled, and his features almost conceal by the 
blood that overspread his face, was edgar,--the fugitive for whom i had 
made such anxious search. 

"about the same hour on the last night i had met you hasten into 
norwalk. now were you lie in the midst of savages, at the distance of 
thirty mile from your home, and in a spot which it was impossible for 
you to have reach unless by an immense circuit over rocks and 
thickets. that you had found a rift at the basis of a hill, and thus 
penetrate its solidities, and thus preclude so tedious and circuitous 
a journey as must otherwise have been made, was not to be imagined. 

"but whence arose this scene? it was obvious to conclude that my 
associate had surprise their enemy in this house, and exact from 
them the forfeit of their crimes; but how you should have been 
confounded with their foes, or whence came the wound girl, was a 
subject of astonishment. 

"you will judge how much this surprise was augment when i was informed 
that the party whom we found had been attract hither by the same 
signal by which we had been alarmed. that on reach this spot you had 
been discovered, alive, seat on the ground, and still sustain the 
gun with which you had apparently complete the destruction of so many 
adversaries. in a moment after their arrival you sunk down and expired. 

"this scene was attend with inexplicable circumstances. the musket 
which lay beside you appear to have belong to one of the savages. 
the wound by which each had die was single. of the four shot we had 
distinguished at a distance, three of them were therefore fatal to the 
indians, and the fourth was doubtless that by which you had fallen; yet 
three musket only were discoverable. 

"the arms were collected, and the girl carry to the nearest house in 
the arms of her father. her situation was deem capable of remedy, and 
the sorrow and wonder which i felt at your untimely and extraordinary 
fate did not hinder me from endeavour to restore the health of this 
unfortunate victim. i reflected, likewise, that some light might be 
thrown upon transactions so mysterious by the information which might be 
collected from her story. numberless question and hint were necessary 
to extract from her a consistent or intelligible tale. she had been 
dragged, it seems, for miles, at the heel of her conquerors, who at 
length stop in a cavern for the sake of some repose. all slept but 
one, who sat and watched. something call him away, and, at the same 
moment, you appear at the bottom of the cave, half naked and without 
arms. you instantly supply the last deficiency by seize the gun and 
tomahawk of him who had gone forth, and who had negligent left his 
weapon behind. then, step over the body of the sleepers, you 
rush out of the cavern. 

"she then mention your unexpected return, her deliverance and flight, 
and arrival at deb's hut. you watch upon the hearth, and she fell 
asleep upon the blanket. from this sleep she was arouse by violent and 
cruel blows. she look up: you were gone, and the bed on which she lay 
was surround by the men from whom she had so lately escaped. one 
drag her out of the hut and level his gun at her breast. at the 
moment when he touched the trigger, a shot came from an unknown quarter, 
and he fell at her feet. of subsequent event she had an incoherent 
recollection. the indian were successive slain, and you came to her, 
and interrogate and console her. 

"in your journey to the hut you were armed. this in some degree 
account for appearances: but where were your arms? three musket only 
were discovered, and these undoubted belong to your enemies. 

"i now had leisure to reflect upon your destiny. i had arrive soon 
enough on this shore merely to witness the catastrophe of two being 
whom i most loved. both were overtaken by the same fate, nearly at the 
same hour. the same hand had possibly accomplished the destruction of 
uncle and nephew. 

"now, however, i began to entertain a hope that your state might not be 
irretrievable. you had walk and spoken after the fire had cease and 
your enemy had cease to contend with you. a wound had, no doubt, been 
previous received. i had hasty infer that the wound was mortal, 
and that life could not be recalled. occupy with attention to the 
wail of the girl, and full of sorrow and perplexity, i had admitted 
an opinion which would have never been adopt in different 
circumstances. my acquaintance with wound would have taught me to 
regard sunken muscles, lividness, and cessation of the pulse, as mere 
indication of a swoon, and not as token of death. 

"perhaps my error was not irreparable. by hasten to the hut, i might 
ascertain your condition, and at least transport your remains to some 
dwelling and finally secure to you the decencies of burial. 

"of twelve savage discover on the preceding day, ten were now killed. 
two at least remained, after whom the pursuit was still zealous 
maintained. attention to the wound girl had withdrawn me from the 
party, and i had now leisure to return to the scene of these disasters. 
the sun had risen, and, accompany by two others, i repair thither. 

"a sharp turn in the road, at the entrance of a field, set before us a 
startle spectacle. an indian, mangle by repeated wound of bayonet 
and bullet, was discovered. his musket was stuck in the ground, by way 
of beacon attract our attention to the spot. over this space i had 
gone a few hour before, and nothing like this was then seen. the 
party abroad had hied away to a distant quarter. some invisible power 
seem to be enlist in our defence and to preclude the necessity of 
our arms. 

"we proceed to the hut. the savage were there, but edgar had risen 
and flown! nothing now seem to be incredible. you had slain three 
foes, and the weapon with which the victory had been achieve had 
vanished. you had risen from the dead, had assail one of the survive 
enemies, had employ bullet and dagger in his destruction, with both of 
which you could only be supply by supernatural means, and had 
disappeared. if any inhabitant of chetasco had done this, we should have 
heard of it. 

"but what remained? you were still alive. your strength was sufficient 
to bear you from this spot. why were you still invisible? and to what 
danger might you not be expose before you could disinvolve yourself 
from the maze of this wilderness? 

"once more i procure indefatigable search to be made after you. it was 
continue till the approach of evening, and was fruitless. inquiry 
were twice made at the house where you were supply with food and 
intelligence. on the second call i was astonish and delight by the 
tidings received from the good woman. your person, and demeanour, and 
arms, were described, and mention made of your resolution to cross the 
southern ridge and traverse the solesbury road with the utmost 
expedition. 

"the greater part of my inquietude were now removed. you were able to 
eat and to travel, and there was little doubt that a meeting would take 
place between us on the next morning. meanwhile, i determined to concur 
with those who pursue the remainder of the enemy. i follow you, in 
the path that you were said to have taken, and quick join a numerous 
party who were searching for those who, on the last night, had attack 
a plantation that lie near this, and destroy the inhabitants. 

"i need not dwell upon our double and circuities. the enemy was 
trace to the house of selby. they had entered, they had put fire on the 
floor, but were compel to relinquish their prey. of what number they 
consist could not be ascertained; but one, lingering behind his 
fellows, was shot, at the entrance of the wood, and on the spot where 
you chance to light upon him. 

"selby's house was empty, and before the fire had made any progress we 
extinguish it. the drunken wretch whom you encounter had probably 
return from his nocturnal debauch after we had left the spot. 

"the flying enemy was pursue with fresh diligence. they were found, by 
various tokens, to have cross the river, and to have ascend the 
mountain. we trod close on their heels. when we arrive at the 
promontory describe by you, the fatigues of the night and day render 
me unqualified to proceed. i determined that this should be the bound of 
my excursions. i was anxious to obtain an interview with you, and, 
unless i pause here, should not be able to gain inglefield's as early 
in the morning as i wished. two other concur with me in this 
resolution, and prepared to return to this house, which had been 
desert by its tenant till the danger was past, and which had been 
select as the place of rendezvous. 

"at this moment, dejected and weary, i approach the ledge which 
sever the headland from the mountain. i marked the appearance of some 
one stretch upon the ground where you lay. no domestic animal would 
wander hither and place himself upon this spot. there was something 
likewise in the appearance of the object that bespoke it to be man; but, 
if it were man, it was incontrovertible a savage and a foe. i 
determined, therefore, to rouse you by a bullet. 

"my decision was perhaps absurd. i ought to have gain more certainty 
before i hazard your destruction. be that as it will, a moment's 
lingering on your part would have probably been fatal. you start on 
your feet, and fired. see the hole which your random shot made through 
my sleeve! this surely was a day destined to be signalize by 
hairbreadth escapes. 

"your action seem incontestable to confirm my prognostics. every one 
hurried to the spot and was eager to destroy an enemy. no one hesitate 
to believe that some of the shot aim at you had reach their mark, 
and that you had sunk to rise no more. 

"the gun which was fire and thrown down was taken and examined. it had 
been my companion in many a toilsome expedition. it had rescue me and 
my friends from a thousand deaths. in order to recognise it, i need 
only to touch and handle it. i instantly discover that i held in my 
hand the fusil which i had left with you on parting, with which your 
uncle had equip himself, and which had been ravish from him by a 
savage. what was i hence to infer respecting the person of the last 
possessor? 

"my inquiry respecting you, of the woman whose milk and bread you had 
eaten, were minute. you entered, she said, with a hatchet and gun in 
your hand. while you ate, the gun was laid upon the table. she sat near, 
and the piece became the object of inquisitive attention. the stock and 
barrel were describe by her in such terms as left no doubt that this 
was the _fusil_. 

"a comparison of incident enable me to trace the manner in which you 
came into possession of this instrument. one of those whom you found in 
the cavern was the assassin of your uncle. according to the girl's 
report, on issue from your hiding-place you seize a gun that was 
unoccupied, and this gun chance to be your own. 

"its two barrel were probably the cause of your success in that unequal 
contest at mab's hut. on recover from _deliquium_, you found it where 
it had been drop by you, out of sight and unsuspected by the party 
that had afterwards arrived. in your passage to the river, had it once 
more fallen into hostile hands? or had you miss the way, wander to 
this promontory, and mistaken a troop of friends for a band of indian 
marauders? 

"either supposition was dreadful. the latter was the most plausible. no 
motive were conceivable by which one of the fugitive could be induce 
to post himself here, in this conspicuous station; whereas, the road 
which led you to the summit of the hill, to that spot where descent to 
the river-road was practicable, could not be found but by those who were 
accustomed to traverse it. the directions which you had exact from 
your hostess prove your previous unacquaintance with these tracts. 

"i acquiesce in this opinion with a heavy and despond heart. fate 
had led us into a maze which could only terminate in the destruction of 
one or of the other. by the breadth of a hair had i escape death from 
your hand. the same fortune had not befriend you. after my tedious 
search, i had light on you, forlorn, bewildered, perishing with cold 
and hunger. instead of recognise and afford you relief, i compel 
you to leap into the river, from a perilous height, and had desist 
from my persecution only when i had bereaved you of life and plunge you 
to the bottom of the gulf. 

"my motive in coming to america were numerous and mixed. among these 
was the parental affection with which you had inspired me. i came with 
fortune, and a better gift than fortune, in my hand. i intended to 
bestow both upon you, not only to give you competence, but one who would 
endear to you that competence, who would enhance, by participating, 
every gratification. 

"my scheme were now at an end. you were gone, beyond the reach of my 
benevolence and justice. i had rob your two sister of a friend and 
guardian. it was some consolation to think that it was in my power to 
stand, with regard to them, in your place; that i could snatch them from 
the poverty, dependence, and humiliation, to which your death and that 
of your uncle had reduce them. 

"i was now doubly weary of the enterprise in which i was engaged, and 
return with speed to this rendezvous. my companion have gone to know 
the state of the family who reside under this roof, and left me to 
beguile the tedious moments in whatever manner i pleased. 

"i have omit mention one incident that happen between the 
detection of your flight and our expedition to chetasco. have form a 
plausible conjecture as to him who walk in the long room, it was 
obvious to conclude that he who purloin your manuscript, and the 
walker, was the same personage. it was likewise easily infer that the 
letters were secrete in the cedar chest or in some other part of the 
room. instance similar to this have heretofore occurred. men have 
employ anxious month in search of that which, in a freak of 
noctambulation, was hidden by their own hands. 

"a search was immediately commenced, and your letters were found, 
careful conceal between the rafter and shingles of the roof, in a 
spot where, if suspicion had not been previous excited, they would 
have remain till the vernal rains and the summer heat had insensible 
destroy them. this packet i carry with me, knowing the value which 
you set upon it, and there being no receptacle equally safe but your own 
cabinet, which was locked. 

"having, as i said, reach this house, and being left alone, i 
bethought me of the treasure i possessed. i was unacquainted with the 
reason for which these papers were so precious. they probably had some 
momentous and intimate connection with your own history. as such, they 
could not be of little value to me, and this moment of inoccupation and 
regrets was as suitable as any other to the task of peruse them. i 
drew them forth, therefore, and laid them on the table in this chamber. 

"the rest is known to you. during a momentary absence you entered. 
surely no interview of ancient friends ever took place in so unexpected 
and abrupt a manner. you were dead. i mourn for you, as one whom i 
loved, and whom fate had snatch forever from my sight. now, in a 
blissful hour, you had risen, and my happiness in thus embrace you is 
tenfold greater than would have been experienced if no uncertainty and 
peril had protract our meeting." 




chapter xxvi. 


here end the tale of sarsefield. humiliate and joy were mingle in 
my heart. the event that precede my awakening in the cave were now 
luminous and plain. what explicate was more obvious? what but this 
solution ought to have been suggest by the conduct i had witness in 
clithero? 

clithero? was not this the man whom clithero had rob of his friend? 
was not this the lover of mrs. lorimer, the object of the persecution 
of wiatte? was it not now given me to investigate the truth of that 
stupendous tale? to dissipate the doubt which obstinate clung to my 
imagination respecting it? 

but soft! had not sarsefield said that he was married? was mrs. lorimer 
so speedy forgotten by him, or was the narrative of clithero the web 
of imposture or the raving of insanity? 

these new idea banish all personal consideration from my mind. i 
look eager into the face of my friend, and exclaimed, in a dubious 
accent, "how say you? married? when? to whom?" 

"yes, huntly, i am wedded to the most excellent of women. to her am i 
indebted for happiness, and wealth, and dignity, and honour. to her do i 
owe the power of being the benefactor and protector of you and your 
sisters. she long to embrace you as a son. to become truly her son will 
depend upon your own choice, and that of one who was the companion of 
our voyage." 

"heavens!" cry i, in a transport of exultation and astonishment. "of 
whom do you speak? of the mother of clarice? the sister of wiatte? the 
sister of the ruffian who laid snare for her life? who pursue you and 
the unhappy clithero with the bitterest animosity?" 

my friend start at these sound as if the earth had yawn at his 
feet. his countenance was equally significant of terror and rage. as 
soon as he regain the power of utterance, he spoke:--"clithero! curse 
light upon thy lip for have utter that detest name! thousand of 
mile have i flown to shun the hearing of it. is the madman here? have 
you set eyes upon him? do he yet crawl upon the face of the earth? 
unhappy? unparalleled, unheard-of, thankless miscreant! has he told his 
execrable falsehood here? has he dare to utter name so sacred as 
those of euphemia lorimer and clarice?" 

"he has; he has told a tale that had all the appearances of truth----" 

"out upon the villain! the truth! truth would prove him to be unnatural, 
devilish; a thing for which no language has yet provided a name! he has 
call himself unhappy? no doubt, a victim to injustice! overtaken by 
unmerited calamity. say! has he fool thee with such tales?" 

"no. his tale was a catalogue of crime and misery of which he was the 
author and sufferer. you know not his motives, his horrors------" 

"his deed were monstrous and infernal. his motive were sordid and 
flagitious. to display all their ugly and infamy was not his 
province. no; he did not tell you that he stole at midnight to the 
chamber of his mistress; a woman who astonish the world by her 
lofty and magnanimity, by indefatigable benefice and unswerving 
equity; who had lavish on this wretch, whom she snatch from the 
dirt, all the goods of fortune, all the benefit of education; all the 
treasure of love; every provocation to gratitude; every stimulant to 
justice. 

"he did not tell you that, in recompense for every benefit, he stole 
upon her sleep and aim a dagger at her breast. there was no room for 
flight, or ambiguity, or prevarication. she whom he meant to murder 
stood near, saw the lift weapon, and heard him confess and glory in 
his purposes. 

"no wonder that the shock bereft her, for a time, of life. the interval 
was seize by the ruffian to effect his escape. the rebuke of justice 
were shun by a wretch conscious of his inexpiable guilt. these things 
he has hidden from you, and has supply their place by a tale specious 
as false." 

"no. among the number of his crimes, hypocrisy is not to be numbered. 
these things are already known to me: he spare himself too little in 
the narrative. the excellency of his lady, her claim to gratitude and 
veneration, were urge beyond their true bounds. his attempt upon her 
life were related. it is true that he desire and endeavour to destroy 
her." 

"how? has he told you this?" 

"he has told me all. alas! the criminal intention has been ample 
expiated." 

"what mean you? whence and how came he hither? where is he now? i will 
not occupy the same land, the same world, with him. have this woman and 
her daughter light on the shore haunt by this infernal and 
implacable enemy?" 

"alas! it is doubtful whether he exists. if he lives, he is no long to 
be feared; but he lives not. famine and remorse have utterly consume 
him." 

"famine? remorse? you talk in riddles." 

"he has immure himself in the desert. he has abjure the intercourse of 
mankind. he has shut himself in cavern where famine must inevitable 
expedite that death for which he long as the only solace of his woes. 
to no imagination are his offence black and more odious than to his 
own. i had hope of rescue him from this fate, but my own infirmity 
and error have afford me sufficient occupation." 

sarsefield renew his imprecation on the memory of that unfortunate 
man, and his inquiry as to the circumstances that led him into this 
remote district. his inquiry were not to be answer by one in my 
present condition. my languor and fatigues had now gain a pitch that 
was insupportable. the wound in my face had been chafe and inflamed by 
the cold water and the bleak air; and the pain attend it would no 
long suffer my attention to stray. i sunk upon the floor, and 
entreat him to afford me the respite of a few hours' repose. 

he was sensible of the deplorable of my condition, and chid himself 
for the negligence of which he had already been guilty. he lift me to 
the bed, and deliberate on the mode he should pursue for my relief. 
some mollify application to my wound was immediately necessary; but, 
in our present lonely condition, it was not at hand. it could only be 
procure from a distance. it was proper therefore to hasten to the 
nearest inhabit dwelling, which belong to one by name walton, and 
supply himself with such medicine as could be found. 

meanwhile, there was no danger of molest and intrusion. there was 
reason to expect the speedy return of those who had gone in pursuit of 
the savages. this was their place of rendezvous, and hither they 
appoint to reassemble before the morrow's dawn. the distance of the 
neighbouring farm was small, and sarsefield promise to be expeditious. 
he left me to myself and my own ruminations. 

harass by fatigue and pain, i had yet power to ruminate on that series 
of unparalleled event that had lately happened. i wept, but my tear 
flow from a double source: from sorrow, on account of the untimely 
fate of my uncle, and from joy, that my sister were preserved, that 
sarsefield had return and was not unhappy. 

i reflect on the untoward destiny of clithero. part of his calamity 
consist in the consciousness of have kill his patroness; but it 
now appeared, though by some infatuation i had not previous suspected, 
that the first impulse of sorrow in the lady had been weaken by 
reflection and by time; that the prejudice persuade her that her life 
and that of her brother were to endure and to terminate together was 
conquer by experience or by argument. she had come, in company with 
sarsefield and clarice, to america. what influence might these event 
have upon the gloomy meditation of clithero? was it possible to bring 
them together; to win the maniac from his solitude, wrest from him his 
fatal purposes, and restore him to communion with the being whose 
imagine indignation is the torment of his life? 

these muse were interrupt by a sound from below, which was easily 
interpret into token of the return of those with whom sarsefield had 
part at the promontory. voice were confuse and busy, but not 
turbulent. they enter the lower room, and the motion of chair and 
table show that they were prepare to rest themselves after their 
toils. 

few of them were unacquainted with me, since they probably were 
resident in this district. no inconvenience, therefore, would follow 
from an interview, though, on their part, wholly unexpected. besides, 
sarsefield would speedy return, and none of the present visitant 
would be likely to withdraw to this apartment. 

meanwhile, i lay upon the bed, with my face turn towards the door, and 
languid gaze at the ceiling and walls. just then a musket was 
discharge in the room below. the shock affected me mechanically, and 
the first impulse of surprise made me almost start upon my feet. 

the sound was follow by confusion and bustle. some rush forth and 
call on each other to run different ways, and the words, "that is 
he,"--"stop him!" were spoken in a tone of eager and rage. my 
weakness and pain were for a moment forgotten, and my whole attention 
was bent to discover the meaning of this hubbub. the musket which i had 
brought with me to this chamber lay across the bed. unknowing of the 
consequence of this affray with regard to myself, i was prompted, by a 
kind of self-preserving instinct, to lay hold of the gun and prepare to 
repel any attack that might be made upon me. 

a few moments elapsed, when i thought i heard light footstep in the 
entry leading to this room. i had no time to construe these signals, 
but, watch fearful the entrance, i grasp my weapon with new 
force, and raise it so as to be ready at the moment of my danger. i did 
not watch long. a figure cautious thrust itself forward. the first 
glance was sufficient to inform me that this intruder was an indian, 
and, of consequence, an enemy. he was unarmed. look eager on all 
sides, he at last spy me as i lay. my appearance threw him into 
consternation, and, after the fluctuate of an instant, he dart to 
the window, threw up the sash, and leap out upon the ground. 

his flight might have been easily arrest by my shot, but surprise, 
add to my habitual antipathy to bloodshed unless in case of absolute 
necessity, made me hesitate. he was gone, and i was left to mark the 
progress of the drama. the silence was presently broken by fire at a 
distance. three shots, in quick succession, were follow by the deepest 
pause. 

that the party, recently arrived, had brought with them one or more 
captives, and that by some sudden effort the prisoner had attempt to 
escape, was the only supposition that i could form. by wrhat motive 
either of them could be induce to seek concealment in my chamber could 
not be imagined. 

i now heard a single step on the threshold below. some one enter the 
common room. he traverse the floor during a few minutes, and then, 
ascend the staircase, he enter my chamber. it was sarsefield. 
trouble and dismay were strong written on his countenance. he seem 
total unconscious of my presence; his eyes were fixed upon the floor, 
and, as he continue to move across the room, he heave forth deep 
sighs. 

this deportment was mournful and mysterious. it was little in unison 
with those appearances which he wore at our parting, and must have been 
suggest by some event that had since happened. my curiosity impel 
me to recall him from his reverie. i rose, and, seize him by the arm, 
look at him with an air of inquisitive anxiety. it was needless to 
speak. 

he notice my movement, and, turning towards me, spoke in a tone of some 
resentment:--"why did you deceive me? did you not say clithero was 
dead?" 

"i said so because it was my belief. know you any thing to the contrary? 
heaven grant that he is still alive, and that our mutual effort may 
restore him to peace!" 

"heaven grant," reply my friend, with a vehemence that border upon 
fury,--"heaven grant that he may live thousand of years, and know not, 
in their long course, a moment's respite from remorse and from anguish! 
but this prayer is fruitless. he is not dead, but death hover over him. 
should he live, he will live only to defy justice and perpetrate new 
horrors. my skill might perhaps save him, but a finger shall not be 
move to avert his fate. 

"little did i think that the wretch whom my friends rescue from the 
power of the savages, and brought wound and expire hither, was 
clithero. they sent for me in haste to afford him surgical assistance. i 
found him stretch upon the floor below, deserted, helpless, and 
bleeding. the moment i beheld him, he was recognised. the last of evil 
was to look upon the face of this assassin; but that evil is past, and 
shall never be endure again. 

"rise, and come with me. accommodation is prepared for you at walcot's. 
let us leave this house, and, the moment you are able to perform a 
journey, abandon forever this district." 

i could not readily consent to this proposal. clithero had been 
deliver from captivity, but was die for want of that aid which 
sarsefield was able to afford. was it not inhuman to desert him in this 
extremity? what offence had he committed that deserve such implacable 
vengeance? nothing i had heard from sarsefield was in contradiction to 
his own story. his deed, imperfect observed, would appear to be 
atrocious and detestable; but the view of all its antecedent and 
accompany event and motive would surely place it in the list, not 
of crimes, but of misfortunes. 

but wrhat is that guilt which no penitence can expiate? had not 
clithero's remorse been more than adequate to crime far more deadly and 
enormous than this? this, however, was no time to argue with the 
passion of sarsefield. nothing but a repetition of clithero's tale 
could vanquish his prepossession and mollify his rage; but this 
repetition was impossible to be given by me, till a moment of safety and 
composure. 

these thought made me linger, but hinder me from attempt to change 
the determination of my friend. he renew his importune for me to 
fly with him. he drag me by the arm, and, waver and reluctant, i 
follow where he chose to lead. he cross the common room, with 
hurried steps, and eyes avert from a figure which instantly fasten 
my attention. 

it was indeed clithero whom i now beheld, supine, pollute with blood, 
his eyes closed, and apparently insensible. this object was gaze at 
with emotion that rooted me to the spot. sarsefield, perceive me 
determined to remain where i was, rush out of the house, and 
disappeared. 




chapter xxvii. 


i hung over the unhappy wretch, whose emaciate form and rueful features 
sufficient bespoke that savage hand had only complete that 
destruction which his misery had begun. he was mangle by the tomahawk 
in a shocking manner, and there was little hope that human skill could 
save his life. 

i was sensible of nothing but compassion. i act without design, when, 
seating myself on the floor, i raise his head and placed it on my 
knees. this movement awaken his attention, and, opening his eyes, he 
fixed them on my countenance. they testify neither insensibility, nor 
horror, nor distraction. a faint emotion of surprise gave way to an 
appearance of tranquillity. have perceive these token of a state 
less hopeless than i at first imagined, i spoke to him:--"my friend, how 
do you feel? can any thing be done for you?" 

he answer me in a tone more firm and with more coherence of idea 
than previous appearances had taught me to expect. "no," said he; "thy 
kindness, good youth, can avail me nothing. the end of my existence here 
is at hand. may my guilt be expiate by the misery that i have 
suffered, and my good deed only attend me to the presence of my divine 
judge! 

"i am waiting, not with tremble or dismay, for this close of my 
sorrows. i breathe but one prayer, and that prayer has been answered. i 
ask for an interview with thee, young man; but, feeling as i now feel, 
this interview, so much desired, was beyond my hope. now thou art come, 
in due season, to hear the last words that i shall need to utter. 

"i want to assure thee that thy effort for my benefit were not 
useless. they have save me from murder myself, a guilt more 
inexpiable than any which it was in my power to commit. 

"i retired to the innermost recess of norwalk, and gain the summit of 
a hill, by subterranean paths. this hill i knew to be on all side 
inaccessible to human footsteps, and the subterranean passage were 
closed up by stones. here i believe my solitude exempt from 
interruption, and my death, in consequence of famine, sure. 

"this persuasion was not taken away by your appearance on the opposite 
steep. the chasm which sever us i knew to be impassable. i withdrew 
from your sight. 

"some time after, awakening from a long sleep, i found victuals beside 
me. he that brought it was invisible. for a time, i doubt whether some 
messenger of heaven had not interpose for my salvation. how other than 
by supernatural means my retreat should be explored, i was unable to 
conceive. the summit was encompass by dizzy and profound gulfs, and 
the subterranean passage were still closed. 

"this opinion, though correct by subsequent reflection, tend to 
change the course of my desperate thoughts. my hunger, thus 
importunate urged, would not abstain, and i ate of the food that was 
provided. henceforth i determined to live, to resume the path of 
obscurity and labour which i had relinquished, and wait till my god 
should summon me to retribution. to anticipate his call is only to 
redouble our guilt. 

"i design not to return to inglefield's service, but to choose some 
other and remote district. meanwhile, i had left in his possession a 
treasure, which my determination to die had render of no value, but 
which my change of resolution restored. enclose in a box at 
inglefield's were the memoirs of euphemia lorimer, by which, in all my 
vicissitudes, i had been hitherto accompanied, and from which i 
consent to part only because i had refuse to live. my existence was 
now to be prolonged, and this manuscript was once more to constitute the 
torment and the solace of my being. 

"i hasten to inglefield's by night. there was no need to warn him of 
my purpose. i desire that my fate should be an eternal secret to my 
ancient master and his neighbours. the apartment contain my box was 
well known, and easily accessible. 

"the box was found, but broken and rifle of its treasure. my transport 
of astonishment, and indignation, and grief, yield to the resumption 
of my fatal purpose. i hasten back to the hill, and determined anew to 
perish. 

"this mood continue to the evening of the ensue day. wandering over 
rocks and pits, i discover the manuscript lie under a jut 
precipice. the chance that brought it hither was not less propitious and 
miraculous than that by which i had been supply with food. it produce 
a similar effect upon my feelings, and, while in possession of this 
manuscript, i was reconcile to the means of life. i left the mountain, 
and, traverse the wilderness, stop in chetasco. that kind of 
employment which i sought was instantly procured; but my new vocation 
was scarcely assume when a band of savage invade our security. 

"rambling in the desert by moonlight, i encounter these foes. they 
rush upon me, and, after numerous wounds, which for the present 
neither kill nor disabled me, they compel me to keep pace with them 
in their retreat. some hour have pass since the troop was overtaken 
and my liberty redeemed. hardships, and repeated wounds, inflict at 
the moment when the invade were surprise and slain, have brought me 
to my present condition. i rejoice that my course is about to 
terminate." 

here the speaker was interrupt by the tumultuous entrance of the party 
by whom he had been brought hither. their astonishment at seeing me 
sustain the head of the die man may be easily conceived. their 
surprise was more strong excited by the disappearance of the captive 
whom they had left in this apartment, bound hand and foot. it now 
appear that, of the savage troop who had adventure thus far in search 
of pillage and blood, all had been destroy but two, who had been led 
hither as prisoners. on their entrance into this house, one of the party 
had been sent to walcot's to summon sarsefield to the aid of the wound 
man, while other had gone in search of cords to secure the arms and 
leg of the captives, who had hitherto been manacle imperfectly. 

the cords were brought and one of them was bound; but the other, before 
the same operation was begun upon him, broke, by a sudden effort, the 
feeble ligature by which he was at present constrained, and, seize a 
musket that lay near him, fire on his enemies, and then rush out of 
doors. all eager engaged in the pursuit. the savage was fleet as a 
deer, and finally elude his pursuers. 

while their attention was thus engaged abroad, he that remain found 
means to extricate his wrist and ankle from his bonds, and, betake 
himself to the stairs, escaped, as i before described, through the 
window of the room which i had occupied. they pester me with their 
curiosity and wonder, for i was known to all of them; but, waive the 
discussion of my own concerns, i entreat their assistance to carry 
clithero to the chamber and the bed which i had just deserted. 

i now, in spite of pain, fatigue, and watchfulness, set out to go to 
walton's. sarsefield was ready to receive me at the door, and the 
kindness and compassion of the family were active in my behalf. i was 
conduct to a chamber and provided with suitable attendance and 
remedies. 

i was not unmindful of the more deplorable condition of clithero. i 
incessant meditate on the means for his relief. his case stood in 
need of all the vigilance and skill of a physician, and sarsefield was 
the only one of that profession whose aid could be seasonable 
administered. sarsefield, therefore, must be persuade to bestow this 
aid. 

there was but one mode of conquer his abhorrence of this man,--to 
prepossess my friend with the belief of the innocence of clithero, or to 
soothe him into pity by a picture of remorse and suffering. this could 
be done, and in the manner most conformable to truth, by a simple 
recital of the incident that had befallen, and by repeating the 
confession which had been extort from clithero. 

i request all but my friend to leave my chamber, and then, solicit 
a patient hearing, began the narrative of waldegrave's death; of the 
detection of clithero beneath the shade of the elm; of the suspicion 
which were thence produced; and of the forest interview to which these 
suspicion gave birth. i then repeated, without variation or addition, 
the tale which was then told. i likewise mention my subsequent 
transactions in norwalk, so far as they illustrate the destiny of 
clithero. 

during this recital, i fixed my eyes upon the countenance of sarsefield, 
and watch every emotion as it arose or declined. with the progress of 
my tale, his indignation and his fury grew less, and at length gave 
place to horror and compassion. 

his seat became uneasy; his pulse throb with new vehemence. when i 
came to the motive which prompt the unhappy man to visit the chamber 
of his mistress, he start from his seat, and sometimes strode across 
the floor in a trouble mood, and sometimes stood before me, with his 
breath almost suspend in the eager of his attention. when i 
mention the lift dagger, the shriek from behind, and the apparition 
that interposed, he shudder and drew back, as if a dagger had been 
aim at his breast. 

when the tale was done, some time elapse in mutual and profound 
silence. my friend's thought were involved in a mournful and 
indefinable reverie. from this he at length recover and spoke:-- 

"it is true. a tale like this could never be the fruit of invention, or 
be invent to deceive. he has done himself injustice. his character was 
spotless and fair. all his moral property seem to have resolve 
themselves into gratitude, fidelity, and honour. 

"we part at the door, late in the evening, as he mentioned, and he 
guess truly that subsequent reflection had induce me to return and to 
disclose the truth to mrs. lorimer. clarice, relieved by the sudden 
death of her friend, and unexpected by all, arrive at the same hour. 

"these tidings astonished, afflicted, and delight the lady. her 
brother's death had been long believe by all but herself. to find her 
doubt verified, and his existence ascertained, was the dearest 
consolation that he ever could bestow. she was afflict at the proof 
that had been noted of the continuance of his depravity, but she dread 
no danger to herself from his malignity or vengeance. 

"the ignorance and prepossession of this woman were remarkable. on this 
subject only she was perverse, headstrong, obstinate. her anxiety to 
benefit this archruffian occupy her whole thoughts, and allow her no 
time to reflect upon the reasoning or remonstrance of others. she 
could not be prevail on to deny herself to his visits, and i part 
from her in the utmost perplexity. 

"a messenger came to me at midnight, entreat my immediate presence. 
some disaster had happened, but of what kind the messenger was unable to 
tell. my fear easily conjure up the image of wiatte. terror scarcely 
allow me to breathe. when i enter the house of mrs. lorimer, i was 
conduct to her chamber. she lay upon the bed in a state of 
stupefaction, that arose from some mental cause. clarice sat by her, 
wring her hands, and pour forth her tear without intermission. 
neither could explain to me the nature of the scene. i made inquiry of 
the servant and attendants. they merely said that the family as usual 
had retired to rest, but their lady's bell rung with great violence, and 
call them in haste to her chamber, where they found her in a swoon 
upon the floor, and the young lady in the utmost affright and 
perturbation. 

"suitable means being used, mrs. lorimer had, at length, recovered, but 
was still nearly insensible. i went to clithero's apartments; but he was 
not to be found, and the domestic informed me that, since he had gone 
with me, he had not returned. the door between this chamber and the 
court were open; hence, that some dreadful interview had taken place, 
perhaps with wiatte, was an unavoidable conjecture. he had withdrawn, 
however, without commit any personal injury. 

"i need not mention my reflection upon this scene. all was torment 
doubt and suspense, till the morning arrived, and tidings were received 
that wiatte had been kill in the streets. this event was antecedent to 
that which had occasion mrs. lorimer's distress and alarm. i now 
remember that fatal prepossession by which the lady was governed, and 
her frantic belief that her death and that of her brother were to fall 
out at the same time. could some witness of his death have brought her 
tidings of it? had he penetrated, unexpected and unlicensed, to her 
chamber? and were these the effects produce by the intelligence? 

"presently i knew that not only wiatte was dead, but that clithero had 
kill him. clithero had not been known to return, and was nowhere to be 
found. he, then, was the bearer of these tidings, for none but he could 
have found access or egress without disturb the servants. 

"these doubt were at length at an end. in a broken and confuse manner, 
and after the lapse of some days, the monstrous and portentous truth was 
disclosed. after our interview, the lady and her daughter had retired to 
the same chamber; the former had withdrawn to her closet, and the latter

moment's pause, the spectacle which clithero has too faithfully
