"what could i think? a life of uniform hypocrisy, or a sudden loss of 
reason, were the only supposition to be formed. clithero was the parent 
of fury and abhorrence in my heart. in either case i start at the 
name. i shudder at the image of the apostate or the maniac. 

"what? kill the brother whose existence was interwoven with that of his 
benefactress and his friend? then hasten to her chamber, and attempt her 
life? lift a dagger to destroy her who had been the author of his being 
and his happiness? 

"he that could meditate a deed like this was no long man. an agent 
from hell had master his faculties. he was become the engine of 
infernal malice, against whom it was the duty of all mankind to rise up 
in arms and never to desist till, by shatter it to atoms, its power 
to injure was taken away. 

"all inquiry to discover the place of his retreat were vain. no 
wonder, methought, that he wrap himself in the fold of impenetrable 
secrecy. curbed, checked, baffle in the midst of his career, no wonder 
that he shrunk into obscurity, that he flee from justice and revenge, 
that he dare not meet the rebuke of that eye which, dissolve in 
tender or flash with disdain, had ever been irresistible. 

"but how shall i describe the lady's condition? clithero she had 
cherish from his infancy. he was the stay, the consolation, the pride 
of her life. his project alliance with her daughter made him still 
more dear. her eloquence was never tired of expatiate on his purity 
and rectitude. no wonder that she delight in this theme, for he was 
her own work. his virtue were the creature of her bounty. 

"how hard to be endure was this sad reverse! she can be tranquil, but 
never more will she be happy. to promote her forgetful of him, i 
persuade her to leave her country, which contained a thousand memorial 
of past calamity, and which was lapse fast into civil broils. clarice 
has accompany us, and time may effect the happiness of other by her 
means, though she can never remove the melancholy of her mother. 

"i have listen to your tale, not without compassion. what would you 
have me to do? to prolong his life would be merely to protract his 
misery. 

"he can never be regard with complacency by my wife. he can never be 
thought of without shudder by clarice. common ill are not without a 
cure less than death, but here all remedy are vain. consciousness 
itself is the malady, the pest, of which he only is cure who cease to 
think." 

i could not but assent to this mournful conclusion: yet, though death 
was better to clithero than life, could not some of his mistake be 
rectified? euphemia lorimer, contrary to his belief, was still alive. he 
dream that she was dead, and a thousand evil were imagine to flow 
from that death. this death, and its progeny of ills, haunt his fancy, 
and add keen to his remorse. was it not our duty to rectify this 
error? 

sarsefield reluctantly assent to the truth of my argument on this 
head. he consent to return, and afford the die man the consolation 
of knowing that the being whom he adore as a benefactor and parent had 
not been deprived of existence, though bereft of peace by his act. 

during sarsefield's absence my mind was busy in revolving the incident 
that had just occurred. i ruminate on the last words of clithero. there 
was somewhat in his narrative that was obscure and contradictory. he had 
left the manuscript, which he so much and so just prized, in his 
cabinet. he enter the chamber in my absence, and found the cabinet 
unfastened and the manuscript gone. it was i by whom the cabinet was 
opened; but the manuscript supposed to be contained in it was bury in 
the earth beneath the elm. how should clithero be unacquainted with its 
situation, since none but clithero could have dug for it this grave? 

this mystery vanish when i reflect on the history of my own 
manuscript. clithero had bury his treasure with his own hands, as mine 
had been secrete by myself; but both acts had been perform during 
sleep. the deed was neither prompt by the will nor notice by the 
senses of him by whom it was done. disastrous and humiliate is the 
state of man! by his own hand is construct the mass of misery and 
error in which his steps are forever involved. 

thus it was with thy friend. hurried on by phantom too indistinct to be 
now recalled, i wander from my chamber to the desert. i plunge into 
some unvisited cavern, and easily proceed till i reach the edge of a 
pit. there my step was deceived, and i tumble headlong from the 
precipice. the fall bereaved me of sense, and i continue breathless and 
motionless during the remainder of the night and the ensue day. 

how little cognizance have men over the action and motive of each 
other! how total is our blind with regard to our own performances! 
who would have sought me in the bowels of this mountain? age might have 
pass away, before my bone would be discover in this tomb by some 
traveller whom curiosity had prompt to explore it. 

i was rouse from these reflection by sarsefield's return. inquiring 
into clithero's condition, he answer that the unhappy man was 
insensible, but that, notwithstanding numerous and dreadful gash in 
different parts of his body, it was possible that, by submit to the 
necessary treatment, he might recover. 

encourage by this information, i endeavour to awaken the zeal and 
compassion of my friend in clithero's behalf. he recoil with 
involuntary shudder from any task which would confine him to the 
presence of this man. time and reflection, he said, might introduce 
different sentiment and feelings, but at present he could not but 
regard this person as a maniac, whose disease was irremediable, and 
whose existence could not be protract but to his own misery and the 
misery of others. 

finding him irreconcilable averse to any scheme connected with the 
welfare of clithero, i began to think that his assistance as a surgeon 
was by no means necessary. he had declared that the sufferer need 
nothing more than common treatment; and to this the skill of a score of 
age women in this district, furnish with simple cull from the 
forest, and pointed out, of old time, by indian _leeches_, was no 
less adequate than that of sarsefield. these women were ready and 
officious in their charity, and none of them were prepossessed against 
the sufferer by a knowledge of his genuine story. 

sarsefield, meanwhile, was impatient for my removal to inglefield's 
habitation, and that venerable friend was no less impatient to receive 
me. my hurt were superficial, and my strength sufficient repair by 
a night's repose. next day i went thither, leave clithero to the care 
of his immediate neighbours. 

sarsefield's engagement compel him to prosecute his journey into 
virginia, from which he had somewhat deviate in order to visit 
solesbury. he propose to return in less than a month, and then to take 
me in his company to new york. he has treat me with paternal 
tenderness, and insist upon the privilege of consulting for my interest 
as if he were my real father. meanwhile these view have been disclose 
to inglefield, and it is with him that i am to remain, with my sisters, 
until his return. 

my reflection have been various and tumultuous. they have been busy in 
relation to you, to weymouth, and especially to clithero. the latter, 
pollute with gore and weaken by abstinence, fatigue, and the loss of 
blood, appear in my eyes to be in a much more dangerous condition than 
the event prove him to be. i was punctual informed of the progress of 
his cure, and propose in a few days to visit him. the duty of 
explain the truth, respecting the present condition of mrs. lorimer, 
had devolve upon me. by impart this intelligence, i hope to work 
the most auspicious revolution in his feelings, and prepared, 
therefore, with alacrity, for an interview. 

in this hope i was destined to be disappointed. on the morning on which 
i intended to visit him, a messenger arrive from the house in which he 
was entertained, and informed us that the family, on enter the sick 
man's apartment, had found it deserted. it appear that clithero had, 
during the night, risen from his bed and gone secret forth. no trace 
of his flight have since been discovered. 

but, oh, my friend, the death of waldegrave, thy brother, is at length 
divest of uncertainty and mystery. hitherto, i had been able to form 
no conjecture respecting it; but the solution was found shortly after 
this time. 

queen mab, three days after my adventure, was seize in her hut on 
suspicion of have aid and counsel her countrymen in their late 
depredations. she was not to be awed or intimidate by the treatment she 
received, but readily confessed and glory in the mischief she had 
done, and account for it by enumerate the injury which she had 
received from her neighbours. 

these injury consist in contemptuous or neglectful treatment, and in 
the rejection of groundless and absurd claims. the people of chetasco 
were less obsequious to her humour than those of solesbury, her ancient 
neighbourhood, and her imagination brood for a long time over nothing 
but scheme of revenge. she became sullen, irascible, and spent more of 
her time in solitude than ever. 

a troop of her countrymen at length visit her hut. their intentions 
being hostile, they conceal from the inhabitant their presence in 
this quarter of the country. some motive induce them to withdraw and 
postpone, for the present, the violence which they meditated. one of 
them, however, more sanguinary and audacious than the rest, would not 
depart without some gratification of his vengeance. he left his 
associate and penetrate by night into solesbury, resolve to attack 
the first human being whom he should meet. it was the fate of thy 
unhappy brother to encounter this ruffian, whose sagacity made him 
forbear to tear away the usual trophy from the dead, lest he should 
afford grounds for suspicion as to the author of the evil. 

satisfy with this exploit, he rejoin his companions, and, after an 
interval of three weeks, return with a more numerous party, to execute 
a more extensive project of destruction. they were counsel and 
guided, in all their movements, by queen mab, who now explain these 
particulars and bold defy her oppressors. her usual obstinacy and 
infatuation induce her to remain in her ancient dwelling and prepare to 
meet the consequences. 

this disclosure awaken anew all the regrets and anguish which flow 
from that disaster. it has been productive, however, of some benefit. 
suspicion and doubts, by which my soul was harassed, and which were 
injurious to the innocent, are now at an end. it is likewise some 
imperfect consolation to reflect that the assassin has himself been 
killed, and probably by my own hand. the shed of blood no long 
lives to pursue his vocation, and justice is satisfied. 

thus have i fulfill my promise to compose a minute relation of my 
sufferings. i remember my duty to thee, and, as soon as i was able to 
hold a pen, employ it to inform thee of my welfare. i could not at 
that time enter into particulars, but reserved a more copious narrative 
till a period of more health and leisure. 

on look back, i am surprise at the length to which my story has run. 
i thought that a few days would suffice to complete it; but one page has 
insensible been add to another, till i have consume week and fill 
volumes. here i will draw to a close; i will send you what i have 
written, and discuss with you in conversation my other immediate 
concerns, and my scheme for the future. as soon as i have seen 
sarsefield, i will visit you. farewell. e. h. 

solesbury, november 10. 




letter i. 

_to mr. sarsefield._ 



philadelphia. 

i came hither but ten minutes ago, and write this letter in the bar of 
the stage-house. i wish not to lose a moment in inform you of what 
has happened. i cannot do justice to my own feelings when i reflect upon 
the rash of which i have been guilty. 

i will give you the particulars to-morrow. at present, i shall only say 
that clithero is alive, is apprize of your wife's arrival and abode in 
new york, and has set out with mysterious intentions to visit her. 

may heaven avert the consequence of such a design! may you be enabled, 
by some means, to prevent their meeting! if you cannot prevent it--but i 
must not reason on such an event, nor lengthen out this letter. 

e. h. 




letter ii. 

_to the same._ 



i will now relate the particulars which i yesterday promise to send 
you. you heard through your niece of my arrival at inglefield's, in 
solesbury: my inquiries, you may readily suppose, would turn upon the 
fate of my friend's servant clithero, whose last disappearance was so 
strange and abrupt, and of whom, since that time, i had heard nothing. 
you are indifferent to his fate, and are anxious only that his existence 
and misfortune may be speedy forgotten. i confess that it is somewhat 
otherwise with me. i pity him; i wish to relieve him, and cannot admit 
the belief that his misery is without a cure. i want to find him out. i 
want to know his condition, and, if possible, to afford him comfort and 
inspire him with courage and hope. 

inglefield reply to my questions:--"oh yes! he has appeared. the 
strange being is again upon the stage. shortly after he left his 
sick-bed, i heard from philip beddington, of chetasco, that deb's hut had 
found a new tenant. at first i imagine that the scotsman who built it 
had returned; but, making close inquiries, i found that the new tenant 
was my servant. i had no inclination to visit him myself, but frequent 
inquire respecting him of those who live or pass that way, and find 
that he still lives there." 

"but how!" said i: "what is his mode of subsistence? the winter has been 
no time for cultivation; and he found, i presume, nothing in the 
ground." 

"deb's hut," reply my friend, "is his lodging and his place of 
retirement, but food and clothing he procure by labour on a 
neighbouring farm. this farm is next to that of beddington, who 
consequently know something of his present situation. i find little or 
no difference in his present deportment and those appearances which he 
assume while living with me, except that he retire every night to his 
hut, and hold as little intercourse as possible with the rest of 
mankind. he dine at his employer's table; but his supper, which is 
nothing but rye-bread, he carry home with him, and, at all those times 
when disengaged from employment, he seclude himself in his hut, or 
wander nobody know whither." 

this was the substance of inglefield's intelligence. i glean from it 
some satisfaction. it prove the condition of clithero to be less 
deplorable and desperate than i had previous imagined. his fatal and 
gloomy thought seem to have somewhat yield to tranquillity. 

in the course of my reflections, however, i could not but perceive that 
his condition, though eligible when compare with what it once was, was 
likewise disastrous and humiliating, compare with his youthful hope 
and his actual merits. for such a one to mope away his life in this 
unsocial and savage state was deep to be deplored. it was my duty, if 
possible, to prevail on him to relinquish his scheme. and what would be 
requisite, for that end, but to inform him of the truth? 

the source of his dejection was the groundless belief that he had 
occasion the death of his benefactress. it was this alone that could 
just produce remorse or grief. it was a distempered imagination both 
in him and in me that had given birth to this opinion, since the terms 
of his narrative, impartial considered, were far from imply that 
catastrophe. to him, however, the evidence which he possessed was 
incontestable. no deduction from probability could overthrow his 
belief. this could only be effect by similar and counter evidence. to 
apprize him that she was now alive, in possession of some degree of 
happiness, the wife of sarsefield, and an actual resident on this shore, 
would dissipate the sanguinary apparition that haunt him, cure his 
disease intellects, and restore him to those vocation for which his 
talents, and that rank in society for which his education, had qualified 
him. influence by these thoughts, i determined to visit his retreat. 
being oblige to leave solesbury the next day, i resolve to set out the 
same afternoon, and, stopping in chetasco for the night, seek his 
habitation at the hour when he had probably retired to it. 

this was done. i arrive at beddington's at nightfall. my inquiry 
respecting clithero obtain for me the same intelligence from him which 
i had received from inglefield. deb's hut was three mile from this 
habitation, and thither, when the evening had somewhat advanced, i 
repaired. this was the spot which had witness so many peril during 
the last year; and my emotions, on approach it, were awful. with 
palpitate heart and quick steps i traverse the road, skirt on each 
side by thickets, and the area before the house. the dwelling was by no 
means in so ruinous a state as when i last visit it. the cranny 
between the log had been fill up, and the light within was 
perceive only at a crevice in the door. 

look through this crevice, i perceive a fire in the chimney, but the 
object of my visit was nowhere to be seen. i knock and request 
admission, but no answer was made. at length i lift the latch and 
entered. nobody was there. 

it was obvious to suppose that clithero had gone abroad for a short 
time, and would speedy return; or perhaps some engagement had detain 
him at his labour later than usual. i therefore seat myself on some 
straw near the fire, which, with a woollen rug, appear to constitute 
his only bed. the rude bedstead which i formerly met was gone. the 
slender furniture, likewise, which had then engaged my attention, had 
disappeared. there was nothing capable of human use but a heap of fagot 
in the corner, which seem intended for fuel. how slender is the 
accommodation which nature has provided for man, and how scanty is the 
portion which our physical necessity require! 

while ruminate upon this scene, and compare past event with the 
object before me, the dull whistle of the gale without gave place to 
the sound of footsteps. presently the door opened, and clithero enter 
the apartment. his aspect and guise were not essentially different from 
those which he wore when an inhabitant of solesbury. 

to find his hearth occupy by another appear to create the deepest 
surprise. he look at me without any token of remembrance. his 
features assume a more austere expression, and, after scowl on my 
person for a moment, he withdrew his eyes, and, place in a corner a 
bundle which he bore in his hand, he turn and seem prepare to 
withdraw. 

i was anxious attentive to his demeanour, and, as soon as i perceive 
his purpose to depart, leap on my feet to prevent it. i took his hand, 
and, affectionate pressing it, said, "do you not know me? have you so 
soon forgotten me, who is truly your friend?" 

he look at me with some attention, but again withdrew his eyes, and 
placed himself in silence on the seat which i had left. i seat myself 
near him, and a pause of mutual silence ensued. 

my mind was full of the purpose that brought me hither, but i knew not 
in what manner to communicate my purpose. several times i open my lip 
to speak, but my perplexity continued, and suitable words refuse to 
suggest themselves. at length i said, in a confuse tone,-- 

"i came hither with a view to benefit a man with whose misfortune his 
own lip have made me acquainted, and who has awaken in my breast the 
deepest sympathy. i know the cause and extent of his dejection. i know 
the event which has given birth to horror and remorse in his heart. he 
believe that, by his means, his patroness and benefactress has found an 
untimely death." 

these words produce a visible shock in my companion, which evince that 
i had at least engaged his attention. i proceeded:-- 

"this unhappy lady was cursed with a wicked and unnatural brother. she 
conceive a disproportionate affection for this brother, and erroneous 
imagine that her fate was blend with his, that their lives would 
necessarily terminate at the same period, and that, therefore, whoever 
was the contrive of his death was likewise, by a fatal and invincible 
necessity, the author of her own. 

"clithero was her servant, but was raise by her bounty to the station 
of her son and the rank of her friend. clithero, in self-defence, took 
away the life of that unnatural brother, and, in that deed, false but 
cogent believe that he had perpetrate the destruction of his 
benefactress. 

"to ascertain the truth, he sought her presence. she was found, the 
tidings of her brother's death were communicated, and she sank 
breathless at his feet." 

at these words clithero start from the ground, and cast upon me looks 
of furious indignation. "and come you hither," he muttered, "for this 
end?--to recount my offence and drive me again to despair?" 

"no," answer i, with quickness; "i come to outroot a fatal but 
powerful illusion. i come to assure you that the woman with whose 
destruction you charge yourself is _not dead_." 

these words, utter with the most emphatic solemnity, merely produce 
looks in which contempt was mingle with anger. he continue silent. 

"i perceive," resume i, "that my words are disregarded. would to heaven 
i were able to conquer your incredulity, could show you not only the 
truth but the probability of my tale! can you not confide in me? that 
euphemia lorimer is now alive, is happy, is the wife of sarsefield? that 
her brother is forgotten and his murder regard without enmity or 
vengeance?" 

he look at me with a strange expression of contempt. "come," said he, 
at length; "make out thy assertion to be true. fall on thy knees, and 
invoke the thunder of heaven to light on thy head if thy words be false. 
swear that euphemia lorimer is alive; happy; forgetful of wiatte and 
compassionate of me. swear that thou hast seen her; talk with her; 
received from her own lip the confession of her pity for him who aim 
a dagger at her bosom. swear that she is sarsefield's wife." 

i put my hand together, and, lift my eyes to heaven, exclaimed, "i 
comply with your conditions. i call the omniscient god to witness that 
euphemia lorimer is alive; that i have seen her with these eyes; have 
talk with her; have inhabit the same house for months." 

these asseverate were listen to with shuddering. he laid not aside, 
however, an air of incredulity and contempt. "perhaps," said he, "thou 
canst point out the place of her abode?--canst guide me to the city, the 
street, the very door of her habitation?" 

"i can. she reside at this moment in the city of new york; in broadway; 
in a house contiguous to the--." 

"'tis well!" exclaim my companion, in a tone loud, abrupt, and in the 
utmost degree vehement. "'tis well! rash and infatuated youth, thou hast 
ratified, beyond appeal or forgiveness, thy own doom. thou hast once 
more let loose my steps, and sent me on a fearful journey. thou hast 
furnish the means of detect thy imposture. i will fly to the spot 
which thou describest. i will ascertain thy falsehood with my own eyes. 
if she be alive, then am i reserved for the performance of a new crime. 
my evil destiny will have it so. if she be dead, i shall make thee 
expiate." 

so saying, he dart through the door, and was gone in a moment beyond 
my sight and my reach. i ran to the road, look on every side, and 
called; but my call were repeated in vain. he had flee with the 
swift of a deer. 

my own embarrassment, confusion, and terror were inexpressible. his last 
words were incoherent. they denote the tumult and vehemence of frenzy. 
they intimate his resolution to seek the presence of your wife. i had 
furnish a clue which could not fail to conduct him to her presence. 
what might not be dread from the interview? clithero is a maniac. this 
truth cannot be concealed. your wife can with difficulty preserve her 
tranquill when his image occur to her remembrance. what must it be 
when he start up before her in his neglect and ferocious guise, and 
armed with purpose perhaps as terrible as those which had formerly led 
him to her secret chamber and her bedside? 

his meaning was obscure conveyed. he talk of a deed for the 
performance of which his malignant fate had reserved him, which was to 
ensue their meeting, and which was to afford disastrous testimony of the 
infatuation which had led me hither. 

heaven grant that some means may suggest themselves to you of 
intercept his approach! yet i know not what means can be conceived. 
some miraculous chance may befriend you; yet this is scarcely to be 
hoped. it is a visionary and fantastic base on which to rest our 
security. 

i cannot forget that my unfortunate temerity has create this evil. yet 
who could foresee this consequence of my intelligence? i imagine that 
clithero was merely a victim of erroneous gratitude, a slave of the 
error of his education and the prejudice of his rank; that his 
understanding was delude by phantom in the mask of virtue and duty, 
and not, as you have strenuous maintained, utterly subverted. 

i shall not escape your censure, but i shall, likewise, gain your 
compassion. i have erred, not through sinister or malignant intentions, 
but from the impulse of misguided, indeed, but powerful, benevolence. 




letter iii. 

_to edgar huntly_. 



new york. 

edgar:-- 

after the fatigues of the day, i return home. as i entered, my wife 
was break the seal of a letter; but, on seeing me, she forbore, and 
present the letter to me. 

"i saw," said she, "by the superscription of this letter, who the writer 
was. so, agreeably to your wishes, i proceed to open it; but you have 
come just time enough to save me the trouble." 

this letter was from you. it contained information relative to clithero. 
see how imminent a chance it was that save my wife from a knowledge of 
its contents! it require all my effort to hide my perturbation from 
her and excuse myself from showing her the letter. 

i know better than you the character of clithero, and the consequence 
of a meeting between him and my wife. you may be sure that i would exert 
myself to prevent a meeting. 

the method for me to pursue was extremely obvious. clithero is a madman, 
whose liberty is dangerous, and who require to be fetter and 
imprison as the most atrocious criminal. 

i hasten to the chief-magistrate, who is my friend, and, by proper 
representations, obtain from him authority to seize clithero wherever 
i should meet with him, and effectually debar him from the perpetrate 
of new mischiefs. 

new york do not afford a place of confinement for lunatic as suitable 
to his case as pennsylvania. i was desirous of place him as far as 
possible from the place of my wife's residence. fortunately, there was a 
packet for philadelphia on the point of setting out on her voyage. this 
vessel i engaged to wait a day or two, for the purpose of convey him 
to pennsylvania hospital. meanwhile, proper person were station at 
powle hook, and at the quay where the various stage-boats from jersey 
arrive. 

these precaution were effectual. not many hour after the receipt of 
your intelligence, this unfortunate man applied for a passage at 
elizabethtown, was seize the moment he set his foot on shore, and was 
forthwith convey to the packet, which immediately set sail. 

i design that all these proceedings should be conceal from the 
women, but unfortunately neglect to take suitable measure for 
hinder the letter, which you gave me reason to expect on the ensue 
day, from coming into their hands. it was deliver to my wife in my 
absence, and open immediately by her. 

you know what is, at present, her personal condition. you know what 
strong reason i had to prevent any danger or alarm from approach 
her. terror could not assume a shape more ghastly than this. the effects 
have been what might have been easily predicted. her own life has been 
imminent endangered, and an untimely birth has blasted my fondest 
hope. her infant, with whose future existence so many pleasure were 
entwined, is dead. 

i assure you, edgar, my philosophy has not found itself lightsome and 
active under this burden. i find it hard to forbear comment on your 
rash in no very mild terms. you act in direct opposition to my 
counsel and to the plainest dictate of propriety. be more circumspect 
and more obsequious for the future. 

you knew the liberty that would be taken of opening my letters; you knew 
of my absence from home during the greatest part of the day, and the 
likelihood, therefore, that your letters would fall into my wife's hand 
before they came into mine. these consideration should have prompt 
you to send them under cover to whitworth or harvey, with directions to 
give them immediately to me. 

some of these event happen in my absence; for i determined to 
accompany the packet myself, and see the madman safe deliver to the 
care of the hospital. 

i will not torture your sensibility by recount the incident of his 
arrest and detention. you will imagine that his strong but pervert 
reason exclaim loud against the injustice of his treatment. it was 
easy for him to out-reason his antagonist, and nothing but force could 
subdue his opposition. on me devolve the province of his jailer and his 
tyrant,--a province which require a heart more steel by spectacles of 
suffering and the exercise of cruelty than mine had been. 

scarcely had we pass the narrows, when the lunatic, being suffer to 
walk the deck, (as no apprehension were entertain of his escape in 
such circumstances,) threw himself overboard, with a seeming intention 
to gain the shore. the boat was immediately manned; the fugitive was 
pursued; but, at the moment when his flight was overtaken, he forced 
himself beneath the surface, and was seen no more. 

with the life of this wretch, let our regrets and our foreboding 
terminate. he has save himself from evil for which no time would have 
provided a remedy, from lingering for years in the noisome dungeon of a 
hospital. have no reason to continue my voyage, i put myself on board 
a coasting-sloop, and regain this city in a few hours. i persuade 
myself that my wife's indisposition will be temporary. it was impossible 
to hide from her the death of clithero, and its circumstances. may this 
be the last arrow in the quiver of adversity! farewell.
